**Introduction**

Reggae has risen, against the odds, to wield a tremendous influence on contemporary popular culture. Despite being initially dismissed even in its land of origin, reggae's liberating message has inspired a broad spectrum of humanity in global superpowers and developing nations alike: freedom fighters, dissidents, dignitaries and above all, ordinary people have rocked to its beat on every continent. Its distinct musical elements increasingly permeate other popular forms: for instance, the dub techniques of Jamaica's pioneering engineers have become the bedrock of modern dance remixes, and sub-genres such as ska, roots and dancehall are the basis of prevalent hybrids abroad. Perhaps most notably, the innovations of Jamaican toasters helped spawn the earliest versions of rap and hip-hop culture, and vibrant cross-fertilization of those forms continues. Though detractors may seek to demean reggae's status, it is evident that it remains a music of great importance; that such an internationally noteworthy innovation has been fostered by the ghetto folk of a small island, born with little more than natural creativity and musical sensibility at their disposal, makes reggae's unlikely rise all the more incredible.

My main motive in writing this book was to allow the men and women, who built the solid foundation upon which the house of reggae music rests, to state their views about its progression and the roles that they played in its formation. Rather than providing a slanted, outsider's view that claims to be a 'definitive' history, this book lets the pioneers of Jamaican popular music use their own voices to tell its tale. And I hoped to answer questions. Who exactly created this remarkable music? Where did these people come from, and how did they become the singers and players, the engineers and producers, the sound system operators and entrepreneurs who fashioned a distinctly Jamaican music? Why did the music take the shape it did, and how did it come to wield such a significant influence – an influence that is sometimes downplayed or glossed over? What were the singers and players trying to express? How do they now feel about the music they created and the chain of events that brought it to the outside world?

I chose an oral history in the hope of presenting as honest and genuine a portrait of the music as possible; first-hand testimony seemed the best way to get there. An oral history also seems to capture the spontaneous circumstances of much reggae invention, and allows the wit and wisdom of its propagators to be better represented. The cadence of Jamaica's spoken patois lends itself more readily to this form than to the dry monotone of a foreign observer seeking to relate events never witnessed and possibly misunderstood. As the people in this book all played important roles in reggae's complex history, they are surely the ones whose voices must be guiding it. Readers will notice that conflicting accounts appear from time to time, notably when the songs in question concern a major shift in style or were particularly popular hits; I have stringently checked conflicting assertions in the hope of presenting information that is as near to accurate as possible.

Another reason for publishing this book is that I wanted to give something back to the originators of music that has been a constant and profoundly moving soundtrack for much of my life. I first became awrare of reggae in my early teens, through the progressive radio stations of the San Francisco Bay area; Junior Murvin's 'Police And Thieves' is among the first tunes I recall. Seeing the films _Tlie Harder They Come_ and _Rockers_ drew me in deeper, as did the early books _Reggae Bloodlines, Catch A Fire_ and _Reggae International,_ but experiencing the floor-shaking reality of Jack Ruby's 50,000-watt sound system in 1982 hooked me completely. Spending several months in London at the age of seventeen was a further revelation: well-established Jamaican communities, vibrant pirate radio stations and the intense festivities of the Notting Hill Carnival brought the context of reggae into clearer focus. It was partly the prominence of reggae that made me base myself in London a few years later, and meeting Lee 'Scratch' Perry shortly after my return in 1987 had all kinds of repercussions on the type of life I have chosen to lead since then. Being part of Scratch's private entourage for a number of years naturally brought me into contact with all manner of reggae folk, yet all this seemed only a minor prelude to the dramatic assault on my senses of subsequent trips to Jamaica, where reggae music is everywhere and cultural revelations abound.

One of the things I have tried to make plain in this book is that reggae is all about connections; its sounds and styles have largely been shaped by select groups of musicians and singers interacting with a small number of producers in different combinations. Finding the characters in this book was often a largely intuitive process, aided by the same connections that are so crucial to the music; this wras particularly true in Jamaica, where the reggae fraternity is very much an extended family. Thus, after taking me to meet producer Bunny Lee, who introduced me to the legendary singer Dennis Brown by telephone, the singer Max Romeo could bring me to guitarist Earl 'Chinna' Smith, who knew where the percussionist Sticky was; Winston Jarrett knew where to find fellow singer Stranger Cole, who directed me to keyboardist Gladdy Anderson. Sometimes, just hanging out at the right spot could work wonders: in an afternoon at Leggo studio on Orange Street I caught toasters U Brown and Tappa Zukie, percussionist Skully and producer Linval Thompson, after mingling with bassist Flabba Holt and trumpeter Johnny Moore next door; at Tuff Gong I encountered singers Albert Griffiths and Carl Dawkins, plus the toaster Dillinger and musicians such as Alva Lewis and Bongo Herman. Having the right connection could really be decisive; for example, Clement 'Sir Coxsone' Dodd refused to give me an interview when I first approached his record shop in New York, but after the Silvertones trio brought me to Studio One in Jamaica he did so without the slightest hesitation.

The earliest interviews I have drawn on in this book began while I was researching _People Funny Boy,_ my biography _of_ Lee 'Scratch' Perry. Scratch's insistence that I was the ghost writer sent to assist the writing of his life story felt like both a blessing and a curse – a cumbersome burden yet at the same time an honour that I could not refuse. Whether the end result was anything close to what either of us had in mind is another matter; nevertheless the quest for knowledge initiated on Perry's behalf was a catalyst that would ultimately lead me to _Solid Foundation._

Just as dub music has taught us that what is absent from a mix is as important as what stays in, one of the hardest parts of assembling this book has been deciding what to leave out. The bulk of the book consists of formal interviews conducted with more than 250 of reggae's prime movers over fifteen years. Suffice to say that for practical reasons I have not been able to include all the material I have compiled, and readers may therefore find that some _of_ their favourite songs or artists are not represented. There were some interviews I was unable to obtain: certain performers died before I had the chance to meet them, others could not be located, and a few declined for reasons best known to themselves or their managers. I am grateful to the few fellow journalists and friends who have allowed me to use extracts from their own interviews, each of which is indicated by a footnote.

This book does not claim to tell the 'complete' story of reggae music. Indeed, as an oral history it has certain natural limitations. Thus the earliest testimony refers to Jamaica's 1930s'j azz scene, with first-hand accounts explaining more fully the postwar R&B, mento and calypso that led directly to the reggae precursors of ska and rock steady; earlier styles from the colonial period, some of which had a decisive impact on reggae, receive only brief mention. Additionally, the book finishes in the mid-1980s, when reggae was on the verge of going digital, as the changes that have come since Jamaican music became computerized are too vast and varied to cover adequately here.

Though I have attempted to highlight the most important songs and styles that made an impact in Jamaica and abroad, this book does not restrict itself to the Jamaican hit parade; equally, while I have never been wilfully obscure, certain lesser-known or forgotten figures are also given voice. The book does not explore in great detail the reggae music that has been made outside Jamaica; though expatriate communities have formed vibrant reggae scenes in London, New York, Miami, Toronto and elsewhere, the reggae of Britain and North America is only commented on where it directly relates to the dominant creations of Jamaica.

This book has taken me to places I surely would not have reached otherwise; much of the journey has been truly wondrous, and all of it highly instructive. I have listened to Ken Boothe sing a chilling a capella in his uptown Kingston front room, eaten roast fish cooked by Congo Ashanti Roy in the ghetto of Independence City, and passed the chalice with several luminaries at the Freedom Sounds yard in Greenwich Farm. I have analyzed the scriptures with Yabby You and Cocoa Tea, debated women's rights with Michael Rose and Junior Reid, and discussed the shortcomings of Western medicine with Augustus Pablo's personal herbalist. I shot the breeze in Gregory Isaacs' shop, swapped African travel stories with U Roy and Niney the Observer, and was regaled by tales of gang warfare from Prince Buster and Big Youth. I drank beer with Rico, sipped vodka with Rupie Edwards, had rum with LKJ, and gnawed sugar cane with Beres Hammond. I traded jazz anecdotes with the Skatalites, dissected rhythmic techniques with Sly and Robbie, learned that Glen Brown's culinary skills are on a par with his music productions, and was treated to the best Chinese meal I'v e ever eaten by Clive Chin. I have met Twelve Tribes, Ethiopian Orthodox, Bobo Dreads, Niyabinghi Theocracy and adherents to other branches of the Rastafari faith. I have pulled the bronze eagle's claw that is the door handle _of_ Edward Seaga's office in New Kingston, heard the frenzied beats of a Pukumina church meeting in Saint Thomas, and been transfixed by the pulsating heartbeat of Niyabinghi groundation drums in Saint Elizabeth. To hear Winston Blake's Merritone sound system at the Turntable club was exquisite; to dance the rub-a-dub in the moonlight at a Sunday night session outside Rae Town's Capricorn Inn was positively heavenly.

In addition to travelling all over Jamaica and spending extended periods in Kingston, this book has brought me to expatriate communities in California, Florida and New York. Almost everyone I came in contact with treated me with the utmost kindness, not only being forthright with tales of their musical careers but welcoming me into their private homes and sometimes their personal lives. Of course, when things did not go according to plan that too became part of the experience. To reach a musician who lived in a remote village I had to drive on dirt roads though a flooded sugar cane estate, guided by an aspiring concert promoter who was apparently the resident 'bad man'. One singer, who lives uptown, insisted on bringing his loaded shotgun when I asked him to drop me at a local bus stop. Another artist invited me to the countryside, but neglected to tell me that on the return journey he and a colleague would be transporting a consignment of counterfeit Gucci bags and the largest quantity of marijuana I have ever seen. As was rightly pointed out by the artist, such experiences helped me see aspects of life normally concealed from the average visitor to Jamaica, and thus understand better the type of tribulation to which the architects of reggae have regularly been subjected.

Overall, I hope this book will contribute to a greater understanding of reggae's complex history, so that the music's significance may be better valued and comprehended. _Solid Foundation_ is also intended to be a testament to the greatness of its originators, whose intense creativity has brought forth such stunning, unique sounds. Reggae sprang from desperation, fashioned by those with little at hand as an expression of their predicament; its rallying cry against injustice and its growing influence ensure that the music will remain a continual source of inspiration.
_I'm going to tell you how reggae music really came about: our foreparents were taken from Africa and sold into slavery out here, that means them lower we down to some lower standard. They carry them to the Indian man's land and call we West Indians, but we know we is not Indians. The white man give we European names, and we know we is not European; we is African descendants, and we don't get African names. Wlien my elders were before me, them try to use this reggae music to establish ourselves, establish a dignity and establish a music that could reach the world, and then the whole world would have to recognize we as a nation – a dreadlocks nation with that light shining upon our hilltop. Our light that we are supposed to shine with the reggae music, it is a light of righteousness, a light of dignity, because that is the only thing that can deliver us from the bondage we find ourselves in._

Yabby You

_Our goal is to have reggae music as exposed as any other music in the world, 'cause it's just as popular as any other music, maybe even more so, but we being a small Third World country, the money power no there behind it to move it. Me know each day the barriers are being broken down bit by bit. It still ain't come yet, but it going come. Right now we still a underground music, a rebel music, which is good, 'cause you have to be a rebel, but you want your work to reach the masses. Every Jamaican musician supposed to have that same hunger to see them work maximize. Not just from a financial standpoint, but from an artistic standpoint, you want your work to go to the widest possible audience._

Lloyd 'Bread' McDonald

**Fats Domino live in Jamaica, February 1961 [Gil Kong/courtesy of Rick Coleman,** **with thanks to Michael Turner and Gene Scaramuzzo]**
**CHAPTER ONE**

[Boogie Rock: Sound System  
Culture and Jamaican R&B](Davi_solidfou_9781596918269_epub_part6.html#d4115821)

_Tlie two first sound system clashes in Jamaica was between Tom the Great Sebastian and Count Nick in i952. Tom beat Nick, and the return was up at Nick's yard in Waltham Park Road – Tom beat him both times. Tom was great, man! Him friend send some big tune for him from New York and that night, if you see the crowd_...

Duke Vin

_We do rhythm and blues songs – all Jamaican artists in those days try to do rhythm and blues._

Skully Sims

JAMAICA'S POPULAR MUSIC has undergone a series of labyrinthine changes, part of a process of transformation that mirrors certain aspects of the island's cultural and political growth. Born from the everyday struggles of the dispossessed in a seemingly peripheral island nation, reggae has often been infused with issues of identity and reactions to the centuries of colonialism that have shaped modern Jamaican society. The music's progression charts a complicated course, shadowing the growing pains of a developing nation.

When independence from Britain was achieved in 1962 the island celebrated with ska, a uniquely Jamaican hybrid creation. As ska is intricately connected to the independence movement and was the first Jamaican style to attract significant foreign interest, it is often regarded as the starting point of contemporary reggae. But in order to understand the emergence of ska properly, certain earlier strands of musical culture must be traced. The most important include Jamaican boogie (its immediate predecessor), the sound system culture (the perpetual arbiter ofJamaican musical taste) that has dominated since the 1940s, the earlier thriving urban jazz scenes and the indigenous folk style called mento.

As the present-day inhabitants _of_ the 'Isle of Springs' all have ancestors from somewhere else, its popular culture has a wide range of influences. Though the British maintained the chief colonial presence for over four hundred years, it was the music of black America that provided the strongest model for Jamaica's urban musicians in the 1940s and 1950s – perhaps not surprising, given the proximity _of_ its largest northern neighbour. The USA continues to exercise not only the greatest industrial, financial and military power in the region, but cultural dominance as well: the airwaves of Jamaica and of many other Caribbean nations are disproportionately filled by American radio and television programmes.

Though black American music may have had the strongest influence on Jamaica in the post-war years, other forms were also important. The _son_ and _bolero_ styles of Cuba, the largest Caribbean island nation, sandwiched between Jamaica and the USA, were also highly influential, as was the music of other nearby Latin American countries including Haiti, the Dominican Republic and Panama. Perhaps the most important elements derived from the ancestral homeland of the majority of Jamaica's population: vestiges of African culture have been retained by Jamaican slave descendants to a much larger degree than by their counterparts in the United States. Examples include the drumming of Pukumina church services and that which accompanies rural Junkanoo masquerade dances; such sacred drumming techniques and percussive practices further evolved from the backbone of Jamaica's folk music and ultimately became defining elements of reggae. Other folk elements developed during slavery days were also drawn upon in later urban styles, including the call-and-response chants of work songs and children's ring games and the adaptation of nineteenth-century European dance music known as quadrille.

The fife and drum of quadrille music was a particularly notable influence on mento, a local platform for social commentary originally associated with the Jamaican countryside; though it has certain similarities to the calypso form popular in other English-speaking Caribbean islands, many commentators have noted uniquely Jamaican aspects of mento. In the 1940s, increased migration to kingston brought mento bands with it. These roving groups of troubadours gave informal street performances satirizing current events. The bands used a particular set of instruments that typically included hand drums, banjo and the oversized _kalimba_ or 'rumba box' (a distant relative of the _mbira_ used for bass notes), often with a fife or penny whistle and perhaps a bamboo saxophone. From the early 1950s, mento groups also became regular features on the North Coast hotel circuit.

Among the sawier of Jamaica's city-dwellers, jazz held greater currency. Since at least the 1930s there had been a vibrant, thriving jazz scene, and in the economically buoyant post-war years big band jazz and swing groups remained the most popular music among the upper echelons of society. At high-class clubs and hotel venues bands played American jazz standards and adapted pan-Caribbean forms like calypso and merengue, but the more creative players were forming new variations of jazz through complex original compositions. 'Big band, that music was also our pop music here,' says Winston Blake, operator of Merritone, the longest-running sound system in Jamaica. 'That was the swing era, the big band era: Count Basie, Tommy Dorsey, Stan Kenton, Glenn Miller. The formative bands in Jamaica played that music as dance music'

**Winston Blake [Trax on Wax archives]**

Chief among the island's hotel groups were the Arawaks, featuring pianist Luther Williams, and saxophonist Val Bennett's band. Eric Deans' Orchestra became the most popular live act in Kingston through a long residence at the Bournemouth Club at East Kingston's Bournemouth Beach; Deans was a multi-instrumentalist whose skills on saxophone drew comparison to Glenn Miller. Redver Cook's band was a favourite of the light-skinned upper class and played regularly at a society spot called the Glass Bucket. John Weston's twelve-piece band, Milton McPherson's group, the Baba Motta Quartet, Wilton Gaynair Quintet and Foggy Mullins Trio were among the other popular acts of the period; trumpeter Sonny Bradshaw, formerly active in several of the above, formed the Sonny Bradshaw Seven in the early 1950s after organizing a landmark jazz concert at Kingston's Ward Theatre. Down at the Coney Island amusement park on East Queen Street, a hot house band with a shifting line-up was blowing jazz nearly every night of the week; its members would later emerge as the key set of studio session players once entrepreneurs began recording local talent.

But poor, working-class Jamaicans could not afford these smart venues. Their entertainment was then – and still is now – most often provided by the sound system. These huge sets of sound equipment first appeared on the island after Jamaicans who had gone to Florida or elsewhere in the American South to do casual farm labour attended dance parties held by black Americans. Restaurant owners and other aspiring businessmen had systems with powerful amplifiers sent down from the States, or had powerful sets custom-built in Jamaica, firing up the sound through any number of large speaker boxes; the sheer power and volume of such systems inevitably drew a large audience when booming out at an open-air dance event. The advent of the sound system was extremely important to Jamaica's popular culture, particularly as it was a defining element in setting up the island's home-grown music industry.

Winston Blake's father, a civil servant, established the Mighty Merritone set in 1950 as a way of earning extra pocket money. It was then the first sound system in Saint Thomas parish and considerably smaller than the Kingston sound systems. When Blake Senior died in 1956 at the age of forty-one, Winston and his older brother Trevor took over the set, moving it to Kingston in 1962. More than fifty years after its creation, Merritone is still going strong. Seated under a portrait of Fidel Castro in his uptown Kingston home, the burly, grey-bearded Blake reminisces on the early days of sound system. 'The situation in Jamaica then was that schoolrooms or church halls was where you had social functions. Then you had the most important part of the Jamaican scenario called lawns, like Jubilee Tile Gardens and Chocomo Lawn, big places that were either concrete slab or wire fence, and you'd have a dance in that area, either at the back of a house or the side of a building – a space where you could put up music. You had control, because there was an entrance gate. That's how the sound system started.'

These dances had a low entry price, and the sound systems' mobility and the fact that live musicians were not involved meant they could be set up at country venues that lacked a stage or had only minimal facilities. So this kind of entertainment was accessible to a wide general audience who were often barred from the elite venues where live jazz was featured. 'In Saint Thomas, you have three big dances a year with bands coming in: one for the police and two for the tennis club,' recalls Blake. 'It was like South Africa: only a certain clientele could attend, so "proper" people would go to those dances and the little people were left out. In Kingston, there was the sound system thing – no bands and people on the ballroom floor and others not getting an entertainment, so the sound system started a certain thing in those poorer areas.'

Those who attended sound system events were further inspired by the witty live commentary from the deejays attached to a particular set and by virtuoso dancers. Each sound was associated with a particular area – typically, a tough patch of the ghetto – with a set of fanatically loyal followers attached to it.

By the start of the 1950s, the sound systems made US rhythm and blues the music of the day. 'Rhythm and blues probably crept in here about '49,' says Blake, 'and became an institution in the fifties.' Sound system competition has always been fierce, with exclusivity particularly important. In this early phase the sound men imported records from America, removing the labels from the most prized ones to stop the competition from locating a copy.

'Those days, our records came into the country in two ways,' Blake explains. 'When the ships came in, the guys would go to the whorehouses, and the touts of the sounds used to hang around the whorehouses – that's where they used to get the records to tout to various sound people. Then a lot of our people used to go up to do farm working and carry back records. Later on, there was some station [WLAC] sponsored by Randy's Record Shop out of Tennessee, and you also had a station out of Miami, WINZ, and we could hear those stations just like we pick up the local station. In those days we would listen to WINZ because we only had one radio station at that time, which was ZQI, and we would hear Cuban stations; later in the night, after about twelve or one o'c lock, the Tennessee stations would come in very clear and they would advertise specials, which would include ten records for a price. That's how a lot of records came into Jamaica. Later on, one or two of the guys started to travel, started shopping for these records and carrying them back.'

From at least as early as 1952, confrontations based on exclusive discs were staged between two sets at downtown venues such as the King's Lawn or Pioneer Club. Sound system battles were waged to establish dominance of an area, with the dancing public making clear which of the rivals they felt had a superior selection. The first sound to rise to prominence in the 1940s was Tom the Great Sebastian, established by Tom Wong, the proprietor of a downtown hardware store. Wong was of mixed parentage: his father was a Chinese Jamaican, his mother of African descent. He is said to have taken his professional name from a circus performer.

'Tom was the number one sound,' the former Sebastian selector and sound system legend now known as Duke Vin states unequivocally. 'He started in the early forties, but I wasn't playing from them times. It was rhythm and blues: Louis Jordan, Coleman Hawkins. I used to stand up outside and listen to him play in a club called Young Men's Progressive in Drum-mond Street; it was a club people hire to keep parties, not far from Tom's Hardware Store at Charles Street and Luke Lane.'

**Legendary sound man Duke Vin** [Mamad **Mossadegh]**

In a West London basement flat lined with sheepskin rugs, Vin relates that Tom's popularity was partly due to the steady supply of records sent by a friend in New York. 'He was a top-class tailor, we call him Won. He was along with Tom's sister.' Another element that contributed to Tom's success was his custom Hi-Fi set, designed by an electrical technician named Headley Jones who had trained in the _KAF_ during World War II; his speakers were hung from trees until more powerful free-standing units were constructed. That Tom sometimes strung up the sound at his hardware store was also beneficial; as Vin notes, 'Some evening he hang up the box and play his music, and people gather round and listen, Jamaica style.'

Singer Derrick Morgan moved to Kingston from rural Clarendon as a child in 1943, after a relative noticed how poor his night vision was. 'I'm born in a district called Mucco, where my father was a deacon for the Presbyterian church. My mother was in Vineyard Town before me and they found out that my eyes weren't good; I was born with retinitis pigmentosa, so they sent me up to come to the doctor at the age of three. I stay in Vineyard Town till I was about five and then I live in a big yard they call Orange Lane, downtown in Orange Street.' Steeped in sound system culture, Morgan points out that Tom had predecessors. 'The first set you have in Jamaica was called Waldron,' he insists. 'Tom the Great Sebastian was more popular than Waldron, but Waldron have him sound long before them man, and Waldron used to play just one place on Slipe Road. Orange Street run into Slipe Road, and he was right at Torrington Bridge corner.'

**Derrick Morgan [David Katz]**

By the start of the 1950s Tom had much competition, but he remained the most respected and popular set to the middle of the decade. Prince Buster, who insists Tom was 'the first dance hall sound system in Jamaica', recalls a number of his early rivals. 'The sounds at the time were Count Nick, Dean's sound system – a mighty sound system run by an Indian man at Maxfield Avenue and Spanish Town Road, him had a bicycle shop – Doc's Thunderstorm at Brotherton Avenue, and Mellow Canary is from the beginning, he had a powerful sound in Saint Thomas. Waldron was a good sound and Buckram was off Lyndhurst Road, a little bit above Waldron's place. There was also Sherwood, but Sherwood wasn't a dancehall sound system: him make him sound to make people come in him shop.' Other notable early rivals included Lord Koos and a somewhat upmarket set called V-Rocket. 'V-Rocket didn't carry that heavy beat,' Morgan notes. 'It was a more lighter sound, what we call Hi-Fi. He would play at a venue called Caterers' in Manchester Square, by Heroes Park.'

'I'd say Count Nick was the biggest competition,' says a misty-eyed Duke Vin, 'because the two first sound system clashes in Jamaica was between Tom and Nick in 1952. Tom beat Nick, and the return was up at Nick's yard in Waltham Park Road – Tom beat him both times. Tom was great, man! Him friend send some big tune for him from New York and that night, if you see the crowd...'

Vin was born in Kingston in 1928 and grew up on Wildman Street, 'right beside the Success Club'. He says his Christian upbringing means he does not smoke or drink, but his smiling mouth still often fills with swearwords despite his advanced age, and he continues to operate his sound sporadically. Vin says that in his teenage years, during the 1940s, he was just another of Tom's local supporters until fate intervened in the form of a punctured tyre; Tom was then reaching the height of his popularity and had three separate sound systems. 'I stand up outside Tom's Hardware Store one evening, him car get puncture and him was fighting to take off the tyre with a spring-blade. I come and press my foot on the spring-blade and him say, "Thank you very much, Vinnie man." When him done and fix up him thing, him say, "You think you can handle a sound, play a party down on Princess Street? Come, we go set up the sound next week." The Monday morning after, them come and pay Tom and say, "Who's that likkle fella you send to play the sound? Everybody was clapping and praising him." Tom say, "From now on, you play the number two sound." It's three set him have: when all three play, his brother play the number three, I play number two and Tom play the number one; when people don't book the number two, I play the number one with Tom.'

Tom the Great Sebastian reigned supreme through a varied selection, which included merengue and Latin music as well as the more standard rhythm and blues. Vin recalls that Tom varied his music so as to reach the upper-class sections of his audience, who were less enamoured of the harder rhythm and blues styles favoured by ghetto folk. 'Tom play everything, because the uptown people like those things like "Mockingbird Hill". We also play calypso, like "She 'Pon Top."'

'I enjoyed the way he played his music probably more than any other sound, because he was adventurous,' confirms Winston Blake. 'He used to play merengue, and Spanish music. He played a repertoire, whereas most of the other sounds would play a hard core of slow and fast rhythm and blues, and drop in a few mentos – that was the music in the fifties that was really dominant, and the slow type of New Orleans music'

Duke Reid the Trojan, who used strong-arm tactics to eliminate competition, eventually superseded Tom downtown. The Duke, who was born Arthur Reid in Port Antonio in 1915, served for ten years in the Kingston police force where he rose to the rank of sergeant; it left him with close connections to the criminal fraternity and a lasting love of guns. After Reid's wife Lucille won the national lottery, the couple set up the Treasure Isle liquor store, restaurant and dry cleaners. Though originally operating from a spot on Pink Lane, it was at 33 Bond Street that the Duke based his Trojan sound system, which he operated with selector Leroy 'Cuttings' Cole and another man called Clifford. It quickly became the most successful system in Jamaica, largely because the Duke's notorious henchmen from the nearby slum of Back-O-Wall launched regular attacks to sabotage his rivals.

Some have suggested that Duke Reid made Tom the Great Sebastian flee the downtown Kingston area; others, including Duke Vin, refute the notion. 'Tom and Duke Reid were good friends,' Vin insists. 'Duke Reid don't [sabotage]. The only person who do that is him followers, the rough guys. They'l l do it to other sound men, but they never do that to Tom. Those rough guys know Tom very good, always come to the shop and say, "Wha'p pen Tom, you all right?"'

**Duke Reid the Trojan**

In any case, Tom's general fear of violent conflict made him move his set to the smart Silver Slipper club in the Cross Roads area, away from the downtown trouble spots and closer to the wealthier residences. 'Tom used to play for the aristocratic, up-class people them,' Vin explains. 'He never like to play for the rough guy – him can't take it, he's a coward. I have to face everything for him: when the police raid the dances, me have to go talk to the police. He don't like rough crowd, so he go and play in Slipper because the crowd is more selective.'

Though Vin denies that Duke Reid used intimidation tactics against Tom Wong, he admits that Reid tried to lure him away from Tom's set with the promise of better pay. The pair first became acquainted when Reid was a policeman and Vin was still wearing short trousers. 'Duke Reid know me as a little boy going to Calabar School, on James's Street and Sutton Street. We likkle youth used to romp out of the Central Police Station, that was not far from the school. Him used to like me, so you find that when I play for Tom, he want to take me away to play for him. Tom used to give me thirty shillings a night to play and Duke offer me five pounds, but I never leave Tom. I dedicated to the man, because the man is a good man to me; it's not the money, it's the loyalty. I stick with Tom to the last, until I come to England in 1954.'

It was only after establishing the first sound system in Britain in 1955 that Tom's former selector became known as Duke Vin - the expatriate public were reminded of Duke Reid. Vin clarifies that during all the years he selected for Tom, the Great Sebastian never officially took part in a head-to-head clash with the Trojan; the closest was when the two sounds were set up at separate parties in adjacent yards. 'It was in the late part down in 1953,' he explains. 'We play next door to each other in Jones Town – me over there so, him here so. Tom have a steel [speaker] and turn it to Reid, Reid have a steel and turn it to Tom, and the crowd come to our dance and Reid flopped. It was fun. We played R&B: Smiley Louis, Fats Domino, James Wayne's "Tend To Your Business", "Little Red Rooster", the Griffen Brothers.'

Jamaica's first commercial station, Radio Jamaica Rediffusion (RJR), was launched in 1950; its broadcasts were supplied to listeners by an inexpensive cable subscription service after a British firm took over the former government station ZQI. Later in the decade, both Tom and Duke sponsored radio programmes to help keep their sound systems in the minds of the public. 'Duke Reid had a programme called _Treasure Isle Time,_ and Tom the Sebastian had a programme called _Sebastian Time,'_ recalls Winston Blake. 'They would play all the new records and tell them where the sound system was playing over the weekend.' But down in Kingston's slum areas, different kinds of battles were being waged.

The early onset of sound-system violence drew definite results, first as Duke Reid carved out a gradually widening territory. Following the Duke's lead, King Edwards the Giant used bullying tactics to secure sound-system glory. The set was established in 1955 by Vincent Edwards, with selector Red Hopeton; Edwards also built up a rough crew that was adept at spoiling dances held by rival sets, and their activities greatly assisted his rise to the top by 1959. As for the pioneering Great Sebastian set, it came to a tragic end in the early 1960s when Tom Wong committed suicide, apparently due to relationship problems rather than to the financial difficulties that have been reported elsewhere. 'He didn't have no debts,' Duke Vin insists. 'He got money, he loaded, but he kill himself because of his wife.'

Derrick Morgan started singing professionally at the age of seventeen; forty-five years later he continues to give energetic stage performances, and was recently awarded the Order of Distinction for his contribution to Jamaica's popular music. Morgan says his debut recording was a sound system exclusive that ultimately helped King Edwards retain popularity. 'I started on the _VereJohns' Opportunity Hour_ at the Palace Theatre; they had double-bill [film] shows [on] the night and a half-hour contest in between. I compete against Monty Morris, Owen Gray, Wilfred Edwards, Hortense Ellis, and I came in first imitating Little Richard. Bim and Bam, the comedians, they hear me at the show and took me on, and I used to go around with them doing stage shows. I stay with them for two years, then '59 I start recording. After hearing Owen Gray, Wilfred Edwards, Higgs and Wilson coming over the radio, I asked them how them get through with it and they wouldn't tell. After a while, I heard of Duke Reid doing auditions, so I wrote "Lover Boy" and "Oh My" and take it by Duke; him say I must come to the rehearsal at Majestic Theatre with Drumbago and the All Star band. I went and rehearsed with them, that was a Wednesday, and we recorded it Thursday at Federal; Saturday I heard my song "Oh My" on the radio, on Duke Reid's _Treasure Isle Time,_ at four in the evening. Now we call it dub plate, but we used to call it soft wax [acetate]; the public couldn't get those songs in their home to play. Duke Reid give "Lover Boy" to King Edwards to play on his sound as they were friends, and Edwards used to play on a corner they call S-corner – at Tewari Crescent, off Spanish Town Road, the road set like an S. This dancehall was right on S-corner, and "Lover Boy" was the top sound on that comer; Edwards would be playing that song all the while.'

Morgan adds that a number of smaller sets, popular with an upscale crowd, were established at this time on the northern borders of Central Kingston. 'Caterers' and number one Lissant Road, those are the two places those Hi-Fi play, like Billy's Hi-Fi, Thunderbird, El Suzy and El Toro, younger sounds than Duke Reid; those are the sound that celebrity people go to, you don't have the ragamuffin dance there. V-Rocket was the top of them, he spin the music better and he have a double-changer [two turntables]; what we calling mix now, they doing that from the early sixties.'

Before the rise of King Edwards the Giant, the most notable challenge to the downtown hegemony of Duke Reid was posed by Sir Coxsone's Downbeat. The set was established by a young friend of the Reids named Clement Dodd, born in Kingston in 1932 and nicknamed Coxsone after an English cricketer. Dodd's selector was Count Machuki, an innovative, jive-talking toaster who had honed his microphone skills on Tom the Great Sebastian's set; his off-the-cuff wisecracks and Coxsone's supreme record collection made the Downbeat sound a formidable contender.

Seated in the dishevelled Kingston office that was once his base of operations, and flanked by deejay King Stitt and other associates, Sir Coxsone details the family fondness for the exciting sounds of rhythm and blues. 'My father was a contractor mason, he built the Carib Theatre and other country theatres. My mother eventually ran a liquor store, but it started out from a little restaurant on Lawes Street and Ladd Lane called Nanny's Corner. This is going back to 1951. We used to play music in the place on a Morphy Richards radio, play Billy Eckstine, Sarah Vaughan, Lionel Hampton and Louis Jordan. My mother loved music, was a great dancer and won many prizes.'

In the early 1950s, Dodd worked as a farm labourer in the USA and began to think about going into the music business after seeing money being made at outdoor block parties. Coxsone explains that it was because of his family association with the Reids that he made his first guest appearances on the Trojan set, and infers that this relationship may have helped keep their rivalry on a more civilized level after he established the Downbeat set. 'Duke was a close friend of the family. Mom and Dad knew him well, and his signature tune was given to him by my mother: "My Mother's Eyes", an instrumental by [alto saxophonist] Tab Smith. I was a cabinetmaker and I did a course on automobile mechanics, used to train at the Ford garage on Church Street. I attended a lot of orchestra dances in the early days, but when the rhythm and blues came in about '53 or '54, I used to attend dances when Duke was playing and play some of my sides. His fans always look forward to seeing me coming with my little case of records, so I eventually went into the sound thing myself. My memories of the days of Duke and meself, it was like a musical challenge.'

**Clement 'Sir Coxsone' Dodd [with crown] in 1957 [Courtesy of Studio One/Heartbeat]**

Duke Vin contradicts part of Coxsone's account, claiming it was Tom the Great Sebastian who sold Reid his treasured signature tune. 'It was 1951,' Vin insists. 'Reid come to him and ask if him have any old record to sell and Tom said, "I have a few that I don't use." Tom use "Blue Moon" as him sign-on tune. When him sell Duke Reid "My Mother's Eyes", Duke Reid use it as a signature tune and it become a hit as a sign-on. That time Coxsone never have no sound at all. I was there when Tom was selling the record.'

Another associate, too, does not recall that the family friendship kept Coxsone's and Duke Reid's rivalry 'clean' – quite the opposite. Prince Buster, born Cecil Bustamante Campbell in downtown Kingston in 1938, speaks of violence and sabotage. Dodd, he says, recruited him from an area associated with those loyal to Duke Reid because of his reputation for toughness. 'I born and grow on Orange Street, right there in West Kingston. Coxsone grow in Saint Thomas, Duke Reid come from Port Antonio, so I am the only one born on Orange Street. My father work with the railroad, my mother work at the match factory and my parents grew me up with a strict principle of Christianity. How I came to work with Coxsone was that on Luke Lane and Charles Street corner, there was two set of gangsters, and one night Noel Horse Jaw and them big men were gambling, playing Parapinto dice. I had some money, asked them if I can join the game and I broke the whole of them – lucky for me, the dice was rolling my way that night. When I start to scrape up the money, one of them named Mean Stick just fling up a foot and kick the felt hat offa me head. The whole _of_ them draw them knife and I couldn't see where my money went to. A week after, I was in my bathroom at Drummond Street and someone call me and say, "Them 'pon the corner!", so I just draw on the clothes _on_ my wet skin and come out and chase Mean Stick with me knife in me hand, and Coxsone and Count Machuki was coming down Luke Lane. Machuki told me later that as Coxsone was scared of people like Mean Stick, it shocked him. He said to Machuki, "Who's the little youth?" Machuki said, "Oh, we grew him, that's who you want to have on your side to take the face off Duke Reid." Then Machuki come to me and say Coxsone want to see me, so me and some youth go in him shop, have some nice drink, and him nice and friendly and say he would like me to move with him, but 1 wouldn't tell him yes. I went back on my corner at Luke Lane and Charles Street and have a meeting writh the youths, and then 1 start to stay at Love Lane and Beeston Street; this was about '57–' 58.'

**Prince Buster live in London, early 1960s, with trombonist Rico Rodriguez at right [Archive du Peuple]**

Buster emphasizes that, though he knew shifting his allegiance could endanger his personal safety, he felt compelled to do so as he bore a lasting grudge against the Duke. 'The only sound system I backed before was Tom the Great Sebastian, which is still the greatest sound system ever, and then Duke Reid ran him out _of_ town. I had vengeance in my heart for Duke Reid, 'cause that man Tom was a nice, decent, calm, respectable man who want to just play music, but Duke Reid come with a band of Back-O-Wall bad man to the dance, they jump the fence and pull over the amplifier, mash up the boxes. Brother Baby Wire, who just die the other day, him was the 'Don' of Back-O-Wall. When him and Rushy and Big Boy them take off at night time, Coxsone haffe jump fence! When wrord reach Duke Reid's camp that I am at Coxsone's sound system, the problem that arise is that I grow with these men, but I'd have to oppose them now because them is Duke Reid's people.'

The conflict rapidly escalated when Coxsone and Duke Reid fought each other in a sound system clash – Buster even resorted to breaking Duke Reid's stylus. 'The first night I went out with Coxsone is a drama, because when the time come for Duke Reid to come on, I tell Machuki, "Don't sign off, continue to play more." Machuki go to play and I scratch [Reid's ] needle, and Duke Reid can't come on. I don't know why I do it, just that this is the thing wha' haffe do and I haffe do it. Duke Reid, when [I] scratch him needle, about half an hour gone, then after him start, we still a play. Duke Reid had a song named "Hey Mr Berry", and Coxsone couldn't find that tune in all his searches of America, so it was Duke Reid's top tune. When he put on the tune, that actually hurt Duke Reid the next day, because the whole town thought Coxsone was playing it. Right there now, the charge of the Back-O-Wall brigade coming to the amplifier that we set up with Machuki, so that was the first forceful demonstration against the criminals them. I was forced that night – for although it's them who grow me and them know me temperament – I flash my knife wild. This is how Coxsone get to become recognized, because the people who want to come to him dance didn't come 'cause them was 'fraid of getting hurt, but because them hear me and other Duke Reid youth now with Coxsone, his dance start to full.'

Buster's formal acceptance within the ranks of Coxsone's camp seems to coincide roughly with a shift in the nascent Jamaican record industry: calypso and mento were being usurped by Jamaican rhythm and blues as a direct byproduct of sound system activity. Members of the Jamaican business community, often of Middle Eastern or Asian origin, were releasing records by the early 1950s. Mento and calypso had been recorded and sold in Jamaica at least as early as 1952, when Jewish Jamaican businessman Stanley Motta issued 78 rpm records on the MRS (Motta's Recording Studio) label by artists such as Lord Fly and the Dan Williams Orchestra, Harold Richardson and the Ticklers, Lord Composer and His Silver Seas Orchestra, and Monty Reynolds' Calypso Clippers featuring singer Boysie Grant. Though Motta's main business was supplying Kodak photographic goods, he also sold records in his store and had a room at the back with a piano, one microphone and primitive recording equipment. Motta had to send the material to Britain to have his records mastered and pressed before being shipped back to Jamaica – a costly and time-consuming process.

'In those days, there was only one recording studio in Jamaica,' says singer Laurel Aitken. 'That was Stanley Motta on Harbour Street. In those days recording wasn't a business – it was more live music' Resident in England since the early 1960s, Aitken is still recording and performing in his seventies; he was born in Havana in 1927 and moved to Kingston at the age of eleven. 'My dad is Jamaican, my mom is Cuban,' he explains, 'and my dad just wanted to go back home.' By 1952, Aitken was a popular figure on talent shows. 'I used to sing from [when I was] a little boy in Cuba, and when I came to Jamaica there was nothing like ska. You had to sing jazz and calypso, which come from Trinidad. I sang "Blue Moon", "Embraceable You", and I won three big contests in Kingston with "Pennies From Heaven". At the time, I used to work with the Jamaica Tourist Board, welcoming people with a big broad hat at the wharf when the ships come in, singing calypsos: "Welcome to Jamaica", "Jamaica Farewell", "Coconut Woman", and they fling money and give me.'

Aitken says his recording debut, at Motta's studio, was a self-produced effort. 'The first recording that I did, I did it for myself, that was a song called "I Met A Senorita". I used my own money and got my own musicians – nobody that's living now. The saxophonist [went by] the name of "Number One" – he was well known in those days. Then I did a merengue, "Merenguitu", and it was released by a man that had a record shop on King Street, by the name of Mr DePass.'

After establishing a residency at the Colony club in Half Way Tree, Aitken recorded further material. His first substantial hit was released by Dada Tewari, another early entrepreneur cutting local product using basic equipment. A Jamaican of Indian descent, Tewari first operated from a small downtown base. By 1956 he had formed the Caribbean Recording Company to release mento and calypso on the Caribou label, later issuing some of the earliest Jamaican R&B recordings as the style moved to the forefront of the Kingston music scene. Tewari has also been named as the first person in Jamaica to have mastering capabilities, but a fire at his Torrington Road premises resulted in his early departure from the music business. 'Dada Tewari was well dressed all the time, with necktie and suit,' recalls Laurel Aitken. 'V-Rocket used to be a top sound, he heard me doing a rehearsal and said, "I'l l take you to see Dada". I did quite a lot [of songs], but the biggest one I did for him was "Roll Jordan Roll", my first big hit in Jamaica.'

**Laurel Aitken [Courtesy of John Mason/Top Beat]**

The duo of Noel 'Zoot' Sims and Arthur 'Bunny' Robinson – known first as Sims and Robinson and later as Bunny and Skully – were the first Jamaican artists to record blues ballads and home-grown R&B, initially for exclusive sound system acetates. 'We started off the first recording in Jamaica on soft wax, for Dada Tewari,' the now partially sighted Skully wistfully recalls, perched on the kerb outside Kingston's Sonic Sounds. 'We did the first recordings in 1953 at a little demo studio at the corner of Hanover Street and Lawes Street – he had a little matches box with quarter-inch tape, but he used to do calypso recording in there with Count Lasher [Terence Parkins], Lord Flea [Norman Thomas], Count Owen [Owen Emanuel] and Lord Tanamo' [Joseph Gordon].

Skully remembers cutting only two songs at this initial session: 'End Of Time' and 'Give Me Another Chance'. 'That was the first tunes made apart from Calypsonians,' he recalls proudly. 'That was the first R&B.' According to Skully, Tewari was not present during the session, which was arranged by the resident pianist. 'He really wasn't so much of a producer,' says Skully of Tewari. 'It was an Indian who owned the theatre that they call Tivoli. Williams played keyboard and you had Lloyd Brevett's father playing bass, a drummer by the name of Percy, and Val Bennett played the saxophone. I don't know if him release them, but we got £37 – 'nough money for me and Bunny.'

Skully was born in 1935 in an area west of Kingston that was then called Smith Village; when the district's population swelled and it became part of the Kingston conurbation it was renamed Denham Town. He and Bunny were school friends who launched their singing careers 'when the first talent show come to Jamaica, which was Vere Johns – he used to write for the _Gleaner._ We was the champion for two years straight. We do rhythm and blues songs – all Jamaican artists in those days try to do rhythm and blues.' The pair favoured slow ballads and were not averse to covering mainstream hits from white America. Skully says that, in these early days, the group concentrated more on live performances than on recording. 'We keep going to shows on the North Coast, but we did not do much more recording because it wasn't popular like now.'

**Noel 'Zoot' Sims, aka Skully: first Jamaican blues on wax [David Katz]**

Skully says a big influence from this era was his neighbour Lord Flea, star of the film _Bop Goes Calypso._ He names Flea as 'the first artist in Jamaica to make a big hit. He was a born Jamaican, start him career at the corner of Regent Street and North Street where they had a little night club, the Esperanza. Then he go to Back-O-Wall and form a little band for himself, and go on the North Coast and sing at hotels before he did the film.'

Flea's main role models were Slim Beckford and Sam Blackwood, street singers active in Kingston in the 1930s. 'Slim and Sam were the two men who start music in Jamaica on the streetside of Spanish Town Road,' says Skully, 'one playing guitar and both of them sing in harmony. The songs that they sing, they have it on paper, printed out, and sell it for a penny – that was their livelihood for years. They used to sing songs like "Man Piabba, Woman Piabba" [a Caribbean folk song], and sometimes Negro spiritual songs.'

Skully could not confirm whether Tewari issued either of their debut recordings, but other singers remember 'End Of Time' as a popular hit in Jamaica. Another early Sims and Robinson number, 'Silent Dreams', is said to have been released on a 78 by Kenneth Khouri and his wife Gloria, founders _of_ the Federal recording studio and group _of_ labels.

Though Stanley Motta is most often cited as the primary issuer of Jamaican recordings, Ken Khouri claims to have been the first. 'He came in long after me,' Khouri insists, 'and that was just a little voice recording set-up he had. Tewari is about two years after. I am the complete pioneer _of_ everything.'

At eighty-four, Ken Khouri is certainly an elder statesman in the Jamaican music industry; he was part _of_ its inception, and was inducted at Jamaica's Hall of Fame for his contributions in January 2001. 'I was born in Kingston in 1917,' he recalls at his uptown Kingston home. 'I had three sisters and I'm the only boy. My father was born in Lebanon, came to Jamaica with his parents when he was twelve. My mother was born here, she was of Cuban parents. When I was growing up, my father had a dry goods stores in the country and a furniture store in Kingston.'

Instead of joining the family business Ken went to work for Joseph Issa, the son of a Syrian immigrant who had established a leading dry goods business; the family would later control a number of luxury hotels. 'His father came and opened a dry goods store and it mushroomed; then the sons took over. I used to work in the office there, controlling the goods that came in. Then I had a furniture business myself at West Street, but I didn't like it. I was born musically [inclined], so that's why I went into the music business. It happened by accident in Miami: I took my father there for his illness and I met someone who was selling a recording machine - a disc recorder; I bought it and came back to Jamaica. I used to go around recording calypsos at different night clubs; the first recording was with Lord Flea, "Where Did The Little Flea Go?" [aka 'Naughty Little Flea']. The discs that I made, I send them as masters to Decca in England, and they made a record out of it and send it down to me.'

'Naughty Little Flea' surfaced on a Capital 78; other Lord Flea sides, plus at least eight 78s by fellow Calypsonian Hubert Porter, were issued by Khouri on Times records, distributed from a variety store on King Street. 'Times Store belonged to Alec Dune – they distributed my first calypso records. All the Hubert Porter, I record them in a studio that I built up myself, in 129 King Street: Records Limited. I brought down equipment from California, just one microphone, one track.' Records Limited controlled the Kalypso label, Khouri's vehicle for calypso 78s. The company also coordinated releases for concerns like Count Lasher's Lasher Disc and Chin's label releases by the Calypso Sextet, featuring a certain A. Bedasse; the Chin's material was backed by Stanley Chin, owner of a radio repair service. Khouri also arranged the first Trojan and Hi Lite 78s, pressed exclusively for Duke Reid's sound system and featuring vocalist Lord Power.

In September 1954 Khouri set up the Pioneer company, securing the Jamaican manufacturing and distribution rights for Mercury records after travelling to New York. 'I used to have franchises from the States, and the first records I put out were "Skokian", [the Chords'] "Sh-Boom" and [the Gaylords'] "The Little Shoemaker".' He brought the first record-pressing equipment to Jamaica while still based on King Street, and subsequently moved to an industrial area west of the wharf to establish Federal, the first fully fledged recording studio and pressing facility on the island. 'I had pressing equipment from 129 King Street,' he recalls, 'where the little studio was, then I moved to the industrial estate at Marcus Garvey Drive in 1957.' For his resident sound engineer Khouri chose Graeme Goodall, an Australian radio technician trained in London who initially came to Jamaica to help set up RJR's cable service. Khouri's sons Richard (born in 1943) and Paul (born in 1946) also joined the business at an early age.

The establishment of Federal gave entrepreneurs greater scope to record local talent; the monophonic studio at radio station RJR was also occasionally used for recording in this period. Gradually, the leading sound system operators saw the potential benefits of recording boogie and R&B with local musicians. Coxsone Dodd says he was recording acts at Federal by 1956, and Skully notes that Dodd was quick to make further use of his group's talents. 'After it start to develop, we did some more recording for Clement Dodd, otherwise known as Coxsone. We started working for Coxsone about '55 – do a few originals and some foreign stuff.'

It is said that much locally created non-calypso music at first remained exclusive to the sound system, and was not pressed for general release. The turning point seems to have been 1957, when Caribou released 'Aitken's Boogie'. The song was a local variant of the type of rhythm and blues that originated in New Orleans – black American music with a pronounced, shuffling beat – and was a template for much of what would follow. 'That was done for Tewari as well, with the same band with Number One,' notes Laurel Aitken, 'and Crackers was the drummer.'

The enthusiastic reception given by Jamaican dance fans to such locally recorded material meant it was only a matter _of_ time before the prominent sound system figures began issuing Jamaican boogie records. Towards the end of the decade Duke Reid, Coxsone Dodd, King Edwards and Lloyd 'the Matador' Daley were all issuing material on seven-inch 45 rpm singles. They were followed shortly by a handful of other business entrepreneurs including Vincent 'Randy' Chin, a record shop owner whose carpenter father had left mainland China in the 1920s to settle in Jamaica after a brief stay in Cuba; Chris Blackwell, the son of a wealthy Jamaican Jewish mother and a military English father; and Edward Seaga, a Harvard-educated anthropologist of Anglo-Syrian and part-African origin, whose father ran a lucrative travel agency.

Seaga, of course, is better known for his highly active role in the nation's politics, as he has been one of the most prominent members of the conservative Jamaica Labour Party (JLP) since the early 1960s; he was Prime Minister from 1980 to 1989, and has since been leader of the opposition. Though his involvement in Jamaica's music industry was brief and happened almost incidentally, his contribution to its development was an important one that was linked to his early political career.

**Edward Seaga: the juncture of music and politics [David Katz]**

At his office in New Kingston the JLP leader, now in his seventies, is surrounded by framed photographs of himself grinning in harmony with such right-wing luminaries as Ronald Reagan and Margaret Thatcher. Seaga explains that he went into the music business after conducting research into African-Jamaican religious practices; his field recordings of folk music were issued in 1956 as _From The Grass Roots Of Jamaica_ on the Smithsonian Folkways label of New York. 'It took three-and-a-half years, because I lived in the communities – was a part of the regular life of the communities – in one rural and one urban setting. I finished the research, but I never used it to get a PhD, because there were no postgrad degrees being given in Jamaica at that time. Smithsonian Folkways were about the only one I knew of who would publish folk music, so I visited them, saw the mass of albums that they had done and this fitted right into that category.'

Seaga recalls that it was an attempt to distribute the record in Jamaica that brought him into the music industry. 'When the album was out in about '56,1 was interested in having the material exposed to people – what's the use of doing research and nobody knows about it? I took it around to music stores – Stanley Motta's on Harbour Street, KG's at Cross Road and Wonard's on Church Street - but they weren't too interested. It was a little bit too way out for them. Then they asked me if I could import other types of music for them, which I did. They wanted stuff like Pat Boone and Nat King Cole, but there was also a very strong interest in rhythm and blues music, like [Marvin and Johnny's ] "Cherry Pie", Professor Longhair, people like those. I found a source for that kind of music: Savoy Records, up in the northern part of Manhattan, and I brought them in, artists like Fats Domino, Stevie Wonder, Wilbert Harrison's "Kansas City", in lots of fifty or one hundred – if it was a big hit, two hundred and fifty. And there would be repeat orders after a certain time.'

Before long, Seaga turned to the domestic manufacturing of foreign material. 'I became an agent for Columbia, Atlantic, ATCO, Epic – probably more labels than anybody else. Then I had a manufacturing operation and there was only one other manufacturer, Khouri – Federal records, but he was more interested in calypsos and mentos. He started two years before me, but they were manufacturing for the tourist market.'

Seaga established his manufacturing base at 13 Bell Road, in the same industrial area as Federal. The next step, naturally, was that Seaga would begin to produce local artists, issuing the work from 1958 on a label called WIRL (West Indies Records Limited). After hearing the duo of Joe Higgs and Roy Wilson at a talent contest, Seaga brought them into the studio to cut 'Manny Oh', a landmark recording as one of the earliest hits by a local Jamaican act. 'The popular music for the masses was rhythm and blues,' Seaga recalls, 'and from that, young Jamaicans began to compose their own rhythm and blues-type music – not exactly the same beat, but that type of music. And I happened to be around at that time, importing and selling rhythm and blues and other types of music. Youngsters were now beginning to compose, but they didn't have an outlet other than to be able to sing on the radio or in live performance. The first popular hit song from those competitions was a song called "Manny Oh". I went to one of the performances at Ward Theatre, because of my interest in the music. I didn't go there with the idea of looking for a song to produce, but when I heard it and heard the audience response, I thought why not try producing it? I spoke with the writer Jackie Edwards, and the singers Higgs and Wilson, about recording it, and we worked out a good arrangement.'

Seaga says he actively supervised the recording session, but notes that the artists themselves selected the musicians. 'That was done at RJR,' he clarifies, 'that big studio they had there. It opened the door, because Jamaicans now realized that they could not only compose and sing their music, but they could get it reproduced on records – it could go on the air and they were on their way. That one effort on record really opened the door for what followed as Jamaican music, and from there it blossomed into different beats.'

Though 'Manny Oh' made quite an impact, Seaga says sales figures have been exaggerated. 'It never sold that many,' he insists. 'I'v e seen figures from other people writing about it, saying it sold thirty thousand copies, but I doubt that. I don't think anything sold thirty thousand copies until much later in the development of the music'

Despite working with other popular artists such as Slim Smith, Seaga says other commitments resulted in him leaving the music industry within a year or two of the success of 'Manny Oh'. 'I didn't do too much after that, because I was moving away from commercial activities to political life.' Seaga was appointed to the Legislative Council in 1959 and shortly afterwards purchased Chocomo Lawn, using it as a base to meet the public. 'Chocomo Lawrn was the first dancehall in Jamaica,' he explains. 'Dances before that wrould take place in back yards, yards with enough space that you could set up your sound and so on, but the first dedicated location that was a dancehall only was Chocomo Lawn. I bought it somewhere around the turning of that decade, because I needed space for the activities. I was using it as my political office, but that was just where I would see people who needed to see me.'

And why, exactly, did Edward Seaga want to leave the academic and commercial worlds he had already tried and enter politics? 'The research that I had done had given me a whole range of new experiences that made me have the benefit of seeing life in a different wTay, from the point of view of the people in folk society,' he explains with gravity. 'Politics is all about dying to better the life of people, especially the people who constitute the majority. And on that basis, I found myself being able to contribute. I gravitated to politics because that's where I could do things that would make a difference.' His role in the music industry, he states, 'wasn't a career objective. It was just something I got involved in out of interest. But when it became a commercial activity, it didn't replace the original interest that I had in folk ways and wanting to do something for the betterment and improvement of the lives of the people. Chris Blackwell and I were at that time mostly doing the same thing; he started a little after I did. I could have continued along that route, and today I guess I would have been like him. But he chose the private life, I chose the public one.'

Meanwhile, developments on the Jamaican airwaves helped the island's homegrown music industry to expand. In September 1959 a second radio station was opened, the government-owned Jamaica Broadcasting Corporation (JBC); its premises were quickly used by entrepreneurs recording Jamaican boogie. In the early 1960s Sonny Bradshaw established _Teenage Dance Party,_ a weekday programme showcasing domestic product; but despite this show's extreme popularity, most radio airplay remained resolutely foreign.

It has been reported elsewhere that the first locally made song aired on JBC was Laurel Aitken's 'Boogie Rock', but Aitken says the song in question was 'Boogie In My Bones', the first hit single produced by Chris Blackwell. It entered the JBC chart in October 1959 and stayed at number one for thirteen weeks. 'Chris came to see me one day, he say, "You been doing quite a few things recently and I like them, I'v e got to do something with you," and I say, "Quite possible,"' Aitken recalls. 'When Chris came the second time, we went to a bar with a piano and I played him a few of the things that I have written. He said, "I'v e got a band I would like you to meet." They was five white guys – I think they were Canadian. We went to JBC with a friend of mine, [saxophonist] Caroll McLaughlin, and I did "Boogie In My Bones" and "Little Sheila".'

'Little Sheila' reached the Jamaican charts too, but 'Boogie In My Bones' was the more substantial hit. Aitken quickly tried to reproduce the formula with 'Boogie Rock', one of a handful of tunes cut with a group dubbed the Boogie Cats for aspiring producer Ruddy Abrahams, whose Downbeat label releases were pressed by Tewari's Caribbean Recording Company; other numbers included 'I'm Crying Over You' and the mento-styled 'Baba Kill Me Goat'.

Though Jamaican boogie may have been little more than a crude attempt to emulate the music of the American South, these early recorded efforts were exciting and retain a distinctly Jamaican feel. The Duke Reid Group's earliest instrumentals, such as 'What Makes Honey' and 'Joker', were fairly unadorned eight- or twelve-bar blues numbers set to a loping beat; Laurel Aitken's 'More Whiskey' and 'Low Down Dirty Girl' (featuring a hot sax solo from Trenton Spence) were much the same, though 'Daniel Saw The Stone' and 'Judgement Day' (backed by guitarist Ken Richards and His Harmonisers) drew from church music. Subsequent Duke Reid productions, such as 'Blackberry Brandy' and 'Yard Broom', used the shuffling boogie blues beat as a platform for expressive soloing by saxophonist Roland Alphonso, whilst drummer Arkland 'Drumbago' Parks' 'Duck Soup' was a somewhat more competent effort augmented by vibrant African hand-drumming. Such early attempts reveal an emerging style, as Jamaicans grappling with African-American rhythms sowed the seeds of a national genre. In contrast, many early Coxsone productions consisted of slower, softer, American-styled R&B ballads such as Alton and Eddie's 'Muriel' and Tony Gregory's 'Baby Come On Home', though other numbers like Lascelles Perkins' 'Lonely Robin' were also mutations of the shuffle mode.

Simeon L. Smith was another early entrepreneur who found success with ballads and mutated blues at the end of the 1950s. At his home in the Bronx, Smith says it was a regular source of records from New Orleans that helped him find a way into the music business. 'I'm from Manchester, a little district called Harry Watch; I come to Kingston when I was seventeen, had a lot of family to welcome me there. Me and a friend named Delfos worked at the wharf together and he has a sound system called Deltone in Mountain View Avenue. I used to be more a business person, so I used to get records from a fellow who come on the SS _America_ every two weeks: Fats Domino, Shirley and Lee. Then I opened a record shop, Hi-Lite Music Store, at the corner _of_ Harris Street and Spanish Town Road. I had a haberdashery also, a hardware store and a wholesale liquor store.'

Naturally Hi-Lite became a focal point for budding local talent, prompting Simeon to launch the Smith's label. 'My place full of young people. I buy an old piano and have a place next door where everybody practise. I put on a lot of shows, helping the artists to make some money. I used to be in my shop every day, have a Grundig tape recorder, and several [singers] would come by and say them have a tune. I would take them in my car up to a man in Hagley Park Road that have a old piano who can play good, practise with them and it grows. First session was with Owen Gray, at JBC studio with Sonny Bradshaw. The first big hit was Keith and Enid, "Worried Over You". They went to Federal records and Federal said they'r e singing too soft. Nobody wanted to record them, because recording business was like Laurel Aitken and heavy beats. But I love harmony and ballads, so I break with "Worried Over You" and make everybody worried.'

This soft duet, backed by tenor saxophonist Trenton Spence's group, topped the Jamaican charts in 1960. It was one of the first recordings licensed to Blue Beat, the subsidiary of Melodise records specifically designed for Jamaican product; the label would later be largely devoted to Prince Buster's output. Emil Shalit, an Eastern European Jewish immigrant, founded Melodise in 1949 as one of the first companies to issue black music in Britain; he was also involved in the pressing of the earliest Jamaican calypso discs. 'Shalit used to do the calypso business with Khouri,' says Simeon Smith. 'He came out and heard about ["Worried Over You"]; Sonny Bradshaw put him on to me and I gave him the rights to release the record in London. Then I start to do a whole lot.'

Smith's next substantial hit wras 'Fat Man' by Derrick Morgan, again backed by Trenton Spence, which blended the cadence of Cuban _bolero_ with the structure of American blues. Morgan recalls that 'Fat Man' was his first recording to gain an official release. 'After I do "Lover Boy" and "Oh My", I find Patsy, which is Millicent Todd. Her mum, Miss Kitty, meet me one day on Orange Street and asked me to come and listen [to] her daughter; she was living at Drummond Street, and I had to pass that street to reach Orange Lane where I live. Patsy start hum two lines of a gospel song and I like the voice I heard, so I sit right there and wrote a song, "Love Not To Brag", and I take her to Duke, and him record that. Then we do "Feel So Fine" off Shirley and Lee, "Feel So Good". Duke been playing these songs against Sir Coxsone Downbeat, play it for whosoever him come up against in contests, but it would be only [on] sound system. I also did "Leave Earth" and "Wigger Wee Shuffle" for Dodd in the early part, when we was just recording for sound systems. Then I hear about "Little Wonder", Mr Smith. King Edwards and Mr Smith is two brothers. I went to Smith with "Fat Man", and Monty Morris and myself do "Now We Know", so Smith was the first person to release. He put out "Fat Man" and people hark up the song; it pick up number one and Duke Reid was vexed about that. He sent some bad men to take me back to him, ask me why I do this, say I can't do that and so on. I said I didn't know it would be offending to him, I don't under no contract with nobody, but I just stay back in his stable for a while.'

Another notable Simeon Smith production was 'Album Of Memory' by the Magic Notes, a duo Smith recalls as 'Victor and Cecil, two guys from over Maverley'. Their hit was one of a handful of songs that Smith recorded with Drumbago. 'His beat was getting very popular,' Smith emphasizes. 'Drumbago was really good on drums.'

Diverted by other activities, Simeon Smith created very little music after 1963. Instead, he concentrated on his sound system, Little Wonder, and on promoting live events; he even wrote popular plays such as _Two Time In Bed,_ starring the rumba dancer Esmarie. 'Sound system, I had about six sets,' says Smith of Little Wonder. 'It was set up in '63 or '64.'

In addition to Trenton Spence and Drumbago, Simeon Smith singled out pianist Theophilus Beckford for particular mention in shaping his recorded material. Beckford's 'Easy Snappin', said to have been recorded in late 1956 but not issued on vinyl for several years, shifted the standard blues blueprint by emphasizing the after-beat of every measure. The song was a favourite on Coxsone's sound system, and was a precursor of the later ska style.

Though Beckford's cadence was original, the shuffle boogie was its most obvious root. Coxsone himself pointed out that Southern American music had the greatest influence on the earliest recordings he issued: 'At that time, my main emphasis was on rhythm and blues, boogie woo-gie, rhythm with a shuffle, and some slow pop music'

**Easy snapping: Theophilus Beckford [Beth Lesser]**

Once regular recording sessions were under way, musicians came together in various forms to impart a particular style for individual producers. Coxsone's earliest backing bands were Hersang and His City Slickers, led by pianist Herman Sang, and the Dew Droppers, led by pianist and organist Aubrey Adams. Clue J and His Blues Blasters, a slightly later combo, featured guitarist Keith Stoddart, drummer Ken Williams, bassist Lloyd Mason and Herman Sang, Aubrey Adams or Theophilus Beckford on piano; the teenage Monty Alexander also occasionally sat in. Duke Reid's early bands included the Duke Reid Group, Drumbago's Orchestra and trumpeter Baba Brooks' Band, which typically featured horn players such as trombonist Don Drummond and his Cuban-born protege, Emmanuel 'Rico' Rodriguez, saxophonist Roland Alphonso, drummer Aston 'Wackie' Henry, and any number of the players mentioned above. Popular music in Jamaica has always been a communal activity, and such groups tended to have overlapping and constantly shifting memberships, recording in various conglomerations under a variety of names.

**Cornell Campbell [David Katz]**

Some of these pioneering musicians are largely forgotten today, despite their important contributions. Singer Cornell Campbell says that during the mid-1950s pianist Herman Sang was a key man in Coxsone's operations, directly responsible for selecting him as a vocalist. 'I started recording at eleven years of age,' he says, seated inside the darkened offices of Freedom Sounds in Greenwich Farm. 'That was 1956, with a song named "My Treasure" for Sir Coxsone's Downbeat. In those days we didn't have reggae and ska – it was just rhythm and blues, not to mention the kind of boogie beat them had. A schoolmate urged me to be recorded. Someone told him Rico live somewhere on Gold Street, so we went to look for Rico and Rico tell us about Coxsone. I was in line for auditions, but Downbeat was very embarrassing. He call everybody "Jackson", and when Downbeat listen to the [singer], he say, "Jackson, which part you come from?" and the guy say he come from somewhere like Trelawney. Coxsone say, "But Jackson, you mean to say you come from so far to mash up my business?" I think the guy sound good, so when Downbeat say that, I just walk out of the line. The other week me go back again, the pianist see me and say, "But hold on, every week me see that little guy come up and he never sing a song yet. Come in, likkle youth, and let me hear what you have to do." I start to get nervous, and when I sing the song "My Treasure", my friend was supposed to harmonize with me, but him couldn't sing a note, so the pianist said my friend must stop. When Downbeat come down in the afternoon, he ask the musicians what's going on, and Hersang say, "That little guy is bad." Him say I must come back for the rehearsal Thursday.'

Cornell's fragile soprano was featured on a number of other sides cut for Dodd in the late fifties and early sixties, but disputes over payment ultimately drove him elsewhere.
