It was war downtown and our posse said, "Nobody can fight Uniques", so it was just a stand-off Both the Uniques and the re-formed Techniques kept the hits flying, notably with modified covers of Impressions' hits, such as the Uniques' version of 'Gypsy Woman' and the Techniques' 'You Don't Care' and 'Queen Majesty'.

'Curtis Mayfield was a major influence,' says Pat Kelly, whose cool, wounded lead is featured on 'You Don't Care'. 'We grewT up in that era: Curtis Mayfield, the Temptations, Smokey Robinson, Jerry Butler.' Kelly is on the veranda of his home in the lower middle-class Kingston suburb _of_ Patrick City, close to the homes of John Holt and Bunny Lee. Apparently a quiet, unassuming man, he says he was born Horatious Kelly in Kingston in 1944 and raised in the ghetto of Maxfield Avenue by his mother, a housewife, and father, a builder who became a church minister when Pat was seventeen. He became a temporary Techniques member when he returned to Jamaica on vacation while studying electronics in America. 'I had no intention of singing,' he insists. 'I went to school and studied as an electronic engineer. Firstly I went to Kingston Technical, and then I did three years in electronics at the Massachusetts College of Technology. It was during semester in 1967 that I came and did these things. Winston Riley asked me if I could join the group – he saw me first sing when I was at Kingston Technical. The group was Winston Riley, Junior Menz and myself, just the three of us. It took me about three months before we record "You Don't Care For Me At All". Directly it went to number one.'

Kelly returned to the States to continue his studies and was therefore not available to record further hits, but later rejoined the group for other recordings with Duke Reid. 'Junior Menz became leader of "My Girl" and "Queen Majesty" because I left back for college,' he explains. 'Junior Menz left, and then I end up with Winston Riley and Bruce Ruffin – they call him Bruce Ruf-fin, but I know him as Bruce Downer. We did "Day Oh" and "Run Come Celebrate", which was the Festival song.' When Kelly broke away from the group shortly afterwards to pursue a solo career, a modified Techniques remained aligned to Treasure Isle; Bruce Ruffin would later enjoy solo popularity in Britain.

**'No intention of singing': Pat Kelly [David Katz]**

As rock steady continued to rise, other less prominent producers, including Demck Harriott, enjoyed considerable hits. After he acquired his first record shop in 1966, Harriott's biggest production successes came with singer Rudy Mills' 'A Long Story' and Keith and Tex's 'Stop That Train' and 'Tonight', though Keith Rowe of the latter duo suggests that both songs were 'bigger abroad than in Jamaica'. A later hit, 'Don't Look Back', was recorded at the tail end of rock steady, but Rowe's subsequent emigration to New York and Dixon's to Canada cut short the duo's rise.

Ever in tune with the sounds of black America, Derrick Harriott also found success with rock steady adaptations of soul. His sensitive rendition of 'The Loser', arranged by bassist Boris Gardiner and boosted by thrilling drum rolls from Joe Isaacs, had an uncommon piano solo that made it all the more irresistible. 'The piano man, Lloyd Delprat, he was like a concert pianist,' Harriott emphasizes. 'He used to play with Boris' band, but he had that concert touch.' Other hits came with original material written by Junior 'Soul' Murvin, including the sensual 'Solomon'. 'That time you had things like "Baby Be True", the Heptones, "Penny For Your Song", the Federals and my "Solomon",' Harriott recalls.' "Solomon" was going for number one like a nonstop train, but Toots went in jail for a little while and was just coming out with this "54-46". Toots came on so hard that "54-46" went straight to number one.'

I asked Toots if it was true that, as has often been reported, he was busted for ganja in 1967. 'I didn't ,' he insists, 'not for ganja. I get arrested because people wish me bad. I was just bailing a friend who got arrested, and I get arrested for nothing, because it was planned. That's why I sing about it.' Is it true that 54–46 was his actual number while in jail? 'No, I just make it up.' Was the rhythm of this song borrowed from Marcia Griffiths' Coxsone-produced 'Feel Like Jumping', or vice versa? 'They come after me,' Toots grumbles. 'A lot of people in Jamaica copy my rhythm – and all over the world.'

Though Coxsone Dodd's stable was ultimately overshadowed by Duke Reid's in the rock steady period, Studio One had a number of formidable hits by a core of popular artists who included Marcia Griffiths, Ken Boothe, the Gaylads and the Heptones. Griffiths says she started singing professionally 'at the age of thirteen, in 1964'. Her ghetto upbringing was financially poor but spiritually rich. 'I was born and raised in Western Kingston. We were a family full _of_ love and godliness. My father was a carpenter, he made furniture. We had sweet parents: even when we didn't have food to eat, we had love – love was always on our table. I usually sing on the choir at Saint John's Presbyterian church and participate in concerts at school. Philip James of the Blues Busters was the boyfriend of my friend, she lived next door to us, and he heard me singing one night and was overwhelmed. I was twelve, still in school, and he was just knocked out, said he travelled all over the States and he's never heard anybody like me. There was a show planning at the Carib Theatre, Easter Monday morning, with Byron Lee and the Dragonaires. Blues Busters were appearing and he insisted that I appear on the show. I did Carla Thomas' "No Time To Lose", and I can honestly say that I brought the house down. Ronnie Nasrallah, the manager for Byron Lee and the Blues Busters, he immediately offered to manage me and I went on TV and I did two Nancy Wilson songs. From that time, I guess it was green light all the way. Then another friend who worked at RJR radio station, Linford Anderson, took me to Studio One. I didn't even do an audition, because they had already heard that I had performed at the Carib Theatre. Studio One is like Jamaica's Motown: that's where all the great artists pass through, where we all graduate. That's where I do my first recording, in 1964. I was accompanied by my father, 'cause I'm still under-age.'

Griffiths says Coxsone wanted her to be half of a male-female singing duo, but it was not until Bob Andy began writing and arranging her material that her career took off. 'When Mr Dodd found me, he coupled me with almost every male singer, seeking a hit song. Bob Marley and myself did a love song, "Oh My Darling", which I understand he's putting out on an album now – I heard it recently, and it's fresh as ever. Bob Andy and myself did "Really Together", and the late Free-I, Jeff Dixon, we did "Words", a cover version of the Bee Gees. I did a duet with Tony Gregory, "You'r e Mine", and [one with] another brother named Owen Boyce. So I did five duets with five different male singers, and then Bob Andy started writing songs for me: I did "Feel Like Jumping", "Melody Life", "Mark My Word", "Tell Me Now".'

**Love was always on the family table:** **Marcia Griffiths [Horseman]**

It was Bob Andy's sensitive vocal arrangements and visually oriented lyrics that drew the best from Marcia Griffiths' vocal capabilities, and Griffiths says their relationship was blessed by divine properties. 'Bob Andy and myself had an intimate relationship, and I can honestly say that all of this was ordained by the Most High God, 'cause Bob Andy was like a father figure. He was like a big brother – he was my guide, my everything. I met him at a rehearsal at Rockfort with the Paragons. After I went to Studio One, we hook up back, and it wasn't an accident, it was ordained, because he was a man with a lot of experience and wisdom and knowledge, and he was like a guide for me, because I was just a young girl then. For a young female singer in a male-dominated business with men surrounding you, God knows what could have happened, so I think God placed him there. He guided me through all the years, writing songs for me.'

The abstract vocalizing of 'Feel Like Jumping' and romantic optimism of#39;Melody Life' gave Dodd two of his biggest rock steady hits. Both are musically complex, with strong horn arrangements featuring Lester Sterling, Vin Gordon and trumpeter Bobby Ellis. Griffiths says Jackie Mittoo, who also provided backing vocals on the tune, determined the overall shape of 'Feel Like Jumping'.

**Bibi Seaton [Courtesy of Harris Seaton]**

Though Duke Reid had his greatest rock steady successes with vocal groups, Dodd tended to fare better in this period with solo artists. Perhaps the most successful Studio One vocal groups in rock steady were the Gaylads, who were mainstays for the bulk of the era, and the Heptones, who appeared at rock steady's tail end. The basis of the Gaylads was inspired songwriter Harris 'Bibi' Seaton and his talented singing partner, Winston Delano Stewart; the two first sang together in the Rhythm Aces, a Vineyard Town harmony quartet led by Boris Gardiner. In 1959 Seaton began recording solo for Coxsone, linking back with Stewart in 1962. They joined with Maurice Roberts to form the Gaylads, but disbanded when they failed to hit in ska. Seaton passed through the Carousels on the North Coast and fronted the Astronauts in 1964-5, before the Gaylads finally re-formed and broke through with Seaton's 'Lady In The Red Dress'.

'One night I was visiting a friend who live close to a cemetery,' Seaton explains in the busy offices of Sprint records in London. 'While waiting for him, I sat on a tombstone and this melody came to me, the lyrics and everything. The next day, I saw Winston and Maurice, and we rehearse it and went back to Coxsone.' The near-American feel of 'Lady In The Red Dress' was hugely successful, and the group scored Coxsone a number of further hits. Seaton was also an important member of Coxsone's team in this time, co-writing hit material for other artists and supervising auditions at Studio One through much of the rock steady phase. 'We had about twelve hits in that period, and Gaylads period was when I used to audition artists for Coxsone. I used to get three different salary from Coxsone every week: being a backing singer, being an A & R man and just being a friend.' He recalls with fondness auditioning 'some incredible guys like the Heptones, Melodians, Lloyd Parks who came as the Termites and this guy that came as Fred Locks, he used to sing in a group called the Bassies'. After 'Lady In The Red Dress', the close harmonies of the Gaylads brought Coxsone notable hits such as 'No Good Girl', 'Peculiar Man' (about a chap who would literally knock himself out when encountering local beauties) and 'Love Me With All Your Heart', on which the group played their own instruments.

Moving on to Leslie Kong, the group recorded a number of further hits. 'We did "There's A Fire" – it was number one in Jamaica for a good while. We did "My Love" and an album called _Fire And Rain'_ Seaton also co-wrote some of Ken Boothe's biggest hits for Kong, most notably the self-determined 'Freedom Street'. As part of Sonia Pottinger's growing stable the Gaylads enjoyed some of their biggest hits, but there was internal friction and Delano Stewart left the group in March 1968 to cut a series of strong solo sides before emigrating to America at the start of the 1970s. 'We went to Pottinger and did "Hard To Confess" and "ABC Rock Steady", and I wrote some serious songs for artists in that period like "Say You" and "Lady With The Starlight", by Ken Boothe. Delano left the group and then went to the States; it was Maurice and myself alone that did "ABC Rock Steady".' Seaton says the void left by Delano's departure was never filled satisfactorily; though Seaton continued to lead the Gaylads into the early reggae period, he chose to go solo in the early 1970s. 'We brought in a guy called Kesto Oakley and he did a song with us called "Little Girl". He wasn't totally committed, so we brought in Ricky Grant, a dreadlocks guy who used to hang around Franklyn Town with all of us. He used to do electrician work, and he sang on a song called "God Loves You Soul Sister" that we did at Beverley's . He went away too, and then I got disillusioned with the whole thing because the sound wasn't there any more. Delano Stewart, he's a pedigree. So that's when I went on my own.'

Though the Gaylads' departure from Studio One spelt a notable loss for Coxsone, the Heptones brought considerable gains from the success of the suggestive 'Fattie Fattie'. Leroy Sibbles, Barry Llewellyn and Earl Morgan were blessed with unrivalled harmonic abilities, and though it was Sibbles wTho most often took the lead he was the last to join the group. 'Heptones is not a one-man thing, Heptones is a three-man thing,' emphasizes founding member Earl Morgan. 'From [when] we get together, the three of we is like magic and that's what make we domineer the sixties and seventies.'

Morgan, is seated on the bonnet of a dilapidated Jaguar at his home in Duhaney Park as he recounts the Heptones' formative days. 'The group really started about 1958, but we never start recording until about the end of '66. A whole heap of people always did in there, man come and go. Glen Adams was part of the group at one time.' Like many of Jamaica's classic harmony groups, the Heptones, says Morgan, came together as teenagers on a street corner in West Kingston. 'I was born in 1945 at 12d Benbow Street in Jones Town, and I go by Trench Town when I was about four year old. Early on, I was listening to people on the radiofusion [Rediffusion], and in the evening everyone just go 'pon the corner and hang out and talk – you don't see that nowadays, because in the evening time everybody look 'pon the television. Barry grow up in my area, so me and Barry always rehearse as a duet, and people come and join up. One time Leroy have a group over Newland Town, that is the other side of Trench Town. Him did have a group over there with a girl named Claire and a brother named Winston, so me and Barry go there one day for a street contest and we dethrone them. It's from then on Leroy join our group.'

**Earl Morgan in Duhaney Park [David Katz]**

It was Sydney 'Luddy' Crooks, founder of the Pioneers and aspiring concert promoter, who arranged for the Heptones to audition for Ken Lack in 1966. 'Luddy Pioneers was in Trench Town, he did have a band down Crooks Street. We was rehearsing one evening and he say we must come down tomorrow evening. Them always had this likkle band going round the island, do likkle shows and t'i ng. We go down there and them say Ken Lack going to have a session Monday coming. From that session, Ken Lack released "Gun Man Coming To Town". It was going good on the charts, [until] Delroy Wilson come with [a cover of the Tarns'] "Dancing Mood".'

'After we did those songs, that was the first time we heard ourselves on the radiofusion – the only radio we had back then,' Leroy Sibbles says, swinging in a hammock at his Kingston home. Sibbles has a determined confidence that borders on arrogance; it is easy to picture the tall singer emerging as leader of the group in the Heptones' early days. 'That got our interest really tight on music and I started writing like hell,' he continues. 'Barry was doing mechanics and Earl were doing some _Star_ newspaper selling, so when we got together at night I would always be introducing new songs to them. I was writing like hell, because I had so much inspiration and it was flowing.'

Moving on from Caltone, the group signed a five-year contract at Studio One. 'We check Beverleys, Duke Reid, until we decided fi check Coxsone when all those people like the Wailers, most of them was leaving,' says Earl Morgan. 'We go there one Sunday, and Ken Boothe and BiBi Seaton take audition of "A Change Is Going To Come", "Fattie Fattie" and "Nobody Knows". "Nobody Knows" and "A Change Is Going To Come", people like it in the dancehall, but the big break come when they release "Fattie Fattie". From [when] they release, everybody need it. They ban it on the radio, but it was a number one seller.'

'Fattie Fattie' was built on a tremulous bass line from Wallin Cameron of the Soul Vendors house band; a sparse guitar riff from Hux Brown, who had been playing at Studio One for several years, accentuated the incorrigible rhythm. The continued popularity of 'Fattie Fattie' sparked several instrumental recuts, as would many other Heptones' Studio One creations. The first, Jackie Mittoo's 'Ram Jam', was almost as popular as the original. 'It's "Fattie Fattie" rhythm that really start Jackie Mittoo as a solo artist,' says Earl Morgan, who names Mittoo as the key figure at Studio One. 'When you'r e there in Trench Town and you hear all these artists over the radiofusion, like Bob Andy, the Wailers, Toots and the Maytals, then you'r e at the studio and you see them in person, it was wild. But the icon of all of the musicians who ever go through Coxsone for me is Jackie Mittoo, because he sing, him play organ, him do everything.'

**Lead Heptone Leroy Sibbles [David Katz]**

The Heptones ultimately recorded what can be seen as the most influential hits in rock steady, because the rhythms behind their voices would be returned to time and time again. Songs like 'Love Won't Come Easy', 'Heptones Gonna Fight' and the life-affirming 'Party Time' have become part of the canon of Jamaican pop - surrogate folk songs perpetually recycled over generations. Though Sibbles says 'Party Time' was inspired by 'some song that I heard the Wailers do', songs like 'Heptones Gonna Fight' and 'Equal Rights' came from observing the injustices suffered by black people in America. 'I was always involved in the black movement and in black history on a whole, so I was reading a lot of magazines about the way Blacks were being treated down South. I even tore out pictures from articles and had them in my little room, of black people being tarred and feathered and hung on trees. That's where the lyrics "Don't hang him on a tree, every man have an equal right to live and be free, no matter what colour, class or race he may be" came from: I was bearing my little bit of pain and anger from those things.'

This shift in subject matter was one of the many changes that came to the fore of Jamaican music once rock steady began to wane. The reggae style that followed it may have begun as another sound to fit a dance pattern, but conscious and politically motivated lyrics would increasingly become part of reggae's focus. As with the shift from ska to rock steady, the most immediate differences involved a particular instrumentation and melodic arrangement, with the bass retaining its crucial role.

**Winston Riley at Techniques records [Beth Lesser]**
**CHAPTER FOUR**

[Feel the Rhythm:  
Ribald Early Reggae](Davi_solidfou_9781596918269_epub_part6.html#d41151091)

_Ska music, that's where we start. We feel the energy and we start to ska, then we feel the energy change and we start to rock and steady, then the energy change again... we change and say that we want reggae._

Lee Perry

_Reggae music was taken from Poco music and calypso music. Poco was the African man's religion – we never know the European way of life. Their music was these carnival type of thing, and we bring the two in one to establish ourselves with the rest of the world._

Yabby You

AS WITH THE emergence of ska and rock steady, the precise birth of reggae is greatly contested. While some name Larry Marshall and Alvin Leslie's Studio One single 'Nanny Goat' as the first example of the new genre, others say it was the slow groove _of_ the Beltones' 'No More Heartaches', produced by former insurance salesman Harry Johnson. On 'Nanny Goat', Boris Gardiner's tight bass patterns and added rest stops simply made the strolling of rock steady seem passe; also moving the rhythm forward was a choppy organ break and intricate percussive cymbal work. Though somewhat slower and more in tune with traditional rock steady choral and horn arrangements, 'No More Heartaches' had a noticeably pronounced organ rhythm that would come to dominate the reggae form.

Many observers feel that reggae did not arrive properly until the end _of_ 1968, when a handful _of_ smaller producers truly brought the new sound forward. Though 'Nanny Goat' may have been the earliest song to show that rock steady was on the wane, it was the efforts of the upcoming 'ghetto promoters' that brought in the reggae sound with full force. Among the most notable contributors on the production side were Lee 'Scratch' Perry, Clancy Eccles, Bunny Lee and Linford Anderson. Together with Joe Gibbs, Derrick Harriott and a handful of others breaking into the business, like Alvin 'GG' Ranglin and Winston 'Niney' Holness, these close associates formed a series _of_ shifting allegiances and rivalries, in the process steering the music in a new direction. At Studio One, further melodic and technical developments also affected the shape of the new music.

Like its predecessors ska and rock steady, the reggae style had a dance that came with it. The most famous celebration of this dance in song came on the Maytals' 'Do The Reggay', which is often cited as the first to name the genre. 'I'm the inventor for the word reggae,' Toots insists. 'I'm the one who say our music called reggae.' According to Bunny Lee, Clancy Eccles, Niney and others, the name itself derives from 'streggae', a derogatory term connoting a woman of low morals, just as 'rock and roll' is said to have derived from black American slang for the sexual act. 'There's a lot of things me do inna this business,' Lee emphasizes. 'All reggae, our name that again. It was streggae, and the radio station wouldn't play streggae, so we change it to reggae.' The first tune to use the name reggae, according to Bunny, was one recorded by his brother, Don Tony Lee. 'Don used to work at WIRL records and he did radio business. Him and [announcer] Jeff Barnes grow up together. Him do the first reggae song, "Come Do The Reggae Now".'

Regardless of who was the first to name the new style, the faster, dance-oriented sound was often built around a rhythmic, two-chord organ shuffle that almost seemed to echo the word 'reggae' in sound. 'The reggae thing that time have some mad shuffle,' says Bunny Lee. 'The reggae is the organ, so when man talk 'bout reggae, they don't even know how it come in.'

The man known as 'Striker' because so many of his records hit is relaxing at his suburban home in Patrick City. Our first interview was delayed for several hours while he and singer Max Romeo went to fetch Striker's false teeth, which had apparently been left somewhere. He then spent some time repairing one of the many trucks in his yard, while his young children watched a frighteningly violent kung-fu movie. Stretching his rotund bulk over an easy chair, the man born Edward Lee has a bottle of Red Stripe beer clenched in each fist as he recounts how he got into the record business in the early 1960s. 'I was born in Kingston in 1941 – it's Greenwich Town now, but it was Greenwich Farm then. My father was a shoemaker and my mother was an ordinary housewife. Nine children follow me, but only six of us survive. After I leave school, I used to do electrical work, then I went into the parts department at United Motors, then I leave and go to Kingston Industrial Garage. From there, I been in the music business. I used to go round with Derrick Morgan, Max Romeo, Laurel Aitken, Jackie Edwards, Owen Gray and Slim Smith.'

Bunny Lee gained an early reputation as an able dancer of the Mashed Potato. He built up strong contacts in the industry and was known to insist with some force that certain music was regularly played. 'I used to go round Duke Reid a lot, and sometimes up by Coxsone,' he continues. 'Used to plug records for them and Leslie Kong on _Teenage Dance Party_ at the radio station. Sometimes Prince Buster and myself used to be very close too. Then when Roy Shirley come around, Roy Shirley bring Joe Gibbs to me.'

Lee was instrumental in ensuring the success of 'Hold Them', but spent so much time promoting records that he was fired from his garage job in 1967. Given free studio time by Duke Reid, Lee cut his first session with Lynn Taitt and the Jets, hitting with Roy Shirley's surreal 'Music Field' and Lloyd and the Groovers' 'Do It To Me Baby'. He then went to work for WIRL records, replacing Lee 'Scratch' Perry as in-house producer. 'Them fire Scratch because Scratch never make no hit,' Bunny Lee explains. 'It's Scratch cause himself to fire. I doing a session with Slim Smith and Scratch was the producer for WIRL at the time, but he didn't know Mr Rae did give me permission to run the session, and Scratch go and tell the musicians the session shouldn't go on. When them stop, I carry them up from the office and Mr Rae pay them, and so Scratch lose him work. The tune come out and was a big hit – "Let Me Go Girl" with Slim Smith and the Uniques.'

**Ghetto promoter extraordinaire: Bunny Lee [center]with fellow producer Prince Jammy** **[right] and friend**

Scratch then went to work for Joe Gibbs, just as Errol Dunkley was cutting his second big hit, 'Please Stop Your Lying'. He remained with Gibbs for several months, cutting his own witty vocal sides, often with startling sound effects, and arranging hits by groups like the Versatiles, the Overtakers and the Pioneers. He also arranged for Gibbs to handle Stranger Cole and Gladdy Anderson's self-produced 'Just Like A River', which turned out to be a sizeable post-rock steady hit, but eventually he left the partnership after disputes over pay, and his shoes were temporarily filled by Niney. Scratch then went back to WIRL and forged an alliance with engineer Linford Anderson – aka Andy Capp – who was then just entering the production field himself.

Anderson now lives in New York, where he is a sound engineer for the United Nations. When asked how he came to be involved in music production, he responds, 'That's something I can't figure out myself. All I know is we used to have a record player and a little radio attached to it, so I used to imitate things I hear on the radio, play parties with one turntable. From there I was an accountant, and I went to RJR as a log keeper in 1959. They took me back later as a trainee engineer. I worked there for a couple of years, and then Mr Nasrallah asked me if I wanted a job at West Indies Records – Nasrallah took it over and built a new studio.' He learned his mixing craft from WIRL's resident engineer. 'Graeme Goodall was the engineer when they opened, he's the one that taught me most of what I know. It was only a little console they had then, a little portable Ampex two-track.'

Anderson says he founded the Upset label in conjunction with Lee Perry and Barrington Lambert, WIRL's trainee engineer, in 1967, working at first with a female harmony trio called the Gaylets; the group featured a young Judy Mowatt, later one of Bob Marley's backing vocalists. Though such early material failed to gain favour, some of the later Upset creations would truly upset the predominant beat.

Around the same time, Lee Perry was also collaborating with Clancy Eccles, who had recently returned to recording after concentrating on concert promotion following his departure from Studio One. When Eccles moved into record production he retained Lee Perry in an A&R capacity, and converted part of his tailoring premises at 121a Orange Street to handle record sales. Eccles cut the rock steady love song 'What Will Your Mama Say' in conjunction with Perry, using Lynn Taitt's band at WIRL, followed by 'Darling Don't Do That'. The songs from their partnership that made the most impact were Monty Morris' 'Say What You'r e Saying' and Clancy's 'Feel The Rhythm'. Ernest Ranglin, who played guitar and provided arrangements on the session at Treasure Isle that produced both tunes, names these as the first reggae records; both have a bouncing pace and altered instrumentation, with 'Feel The Rhythm' in particular pointing to the style that would soon storm in.

Another monumental record from the same period was Lee Perry's 'People Funny Boy', recorded at WIRL with Eccles' assistance and released on the Upset label. 'I helped Scratch with one by the name of "People Funny Boy",' Eccles explains. 'I was the man who set the music, and I do the ghost vocal in the studio while Scratch was the man on the mixing desk. I set the rhythm for it at West Indies, late '68.' The highly charged intensity of 'People Funny Boy' was Perry's attempt to capture the rhythm of a Pukumina church service that he and Linford Anderson had stumbled upon the night before. With vocals that lashed out against his former employer, Joe Gibbs, and a melody line that parodied the Pioneers' 'Longshot', one of Gibbs' biggest production successes, Scratch recorded the cries of his infant son to drive home the song's message of the frustrations of an underpaid man. 'When Scratch's little boy was a baby, he took him in the studio and give him two spank on his bottom,' remembers singer Watty Burnett, who was in the studio when the song was recorded. 'That was the first sampling.' The song's popularity launched Perry as an independent producer, enabling him to form the Upsetter label and take over Prince Buster's former record shop at 36 Charles Street. It also sparked replies from the Pioneers and Joe Gibbs, including 'People Grudgeful' and 'Pan Yu Machete'.

The Pioneers, then the duo of Sidney 'Luddy' Crooks and Jackie Robinson, gave Gibbs some of his biggest hits in the days when reggae was first becoming established. They would subsequently score even bigger hits for Leslie Kong and then emigrate, but after nearly thirty years in Britain Crooks returned to Kingston in 1996 to become in-house arranger and engineer at Joe Gibbs' studio. Behind the mixing desk Luddy looks relaxed and comfortable, with a neat cornrow hairstyle and shockingly long fingernails. He came to Trench Town from the countryside at the age of seventeen, met (future session player) Earl 'Bagga' Walker and started singing at a neighbourhood roundabout. At Crooks Street in Jones Town, Luddy's group gradually took shape; various members passed through what was initially called the Spectaculars and later the Counts. The first version of the Pioneers to enter a recording studio consisted of Luddy, his brother Derrick (aka Joe) and Winston Hewitt; though self-produced, the material was issued on Ken Lack's Caltone label.

**The Pioneers [Courtesy of Jamphoto]**

'I get a little money from my mom,' Luddy explains, 'so we rented Duke Reid's studio with a band called Al and the Vibrators and made a song called "Good Nanny". We funded it ourselves 'cause we couldn't get recorded. We go to Duke Reid, we go to Coxsone, they push their head out and say, "Boy, time finish, you have to come back again and you have to practise hard." The song started to get one and two little plays on the radio, but it's nothing to speak about. We did "Running Back To You", an American song done over, and that one started showing a little bit better. After that, I started keeping stage shows around the country.

Luddy says that things began to fall into place for the Pioneers after he linked up with Joe Gibbs and re-formed the group with Jackie Robinson, a stranger in the right place at the right time. 'Winston Hewitt went to Canada and my brother went to work with the Alcoa bauxite company, so I was left alone. I met Joe Gibbs, bumped into him on Beeston Street where he have a shop, and I started working for him, carrying out records. I tell him that I want to make a record, so we went to WIRL studio one night with Hux Brown, Jackie Jackson, Gladstone Anderson, Winston Wright – the top of the line – and we made a song called "Give Me Little Loving". Outside the studio I saw a boy, so I said, "Me want you to sing a harmony." Jackie Robinson then became the lead singer of the Pioneers, and me and him started singing for Joe Gibbs: "Longshot", "Jackpot", "Catch The Beat".'

On some of these songs the group's humour really shines. The previously mentioned 'Pan Yu Machete', one of the retaliatory swipes at Lee Perry, called the upstart producer a 'two-edged sword,' with further insults coming in the sounds of a yapping puppy atop a slurring violin. 'Longshot', perhaps their biggest hit for Gibbs, was about a losing racehorse that 'busted' a bet, leaving the punter broke when it failed to break the tape at the finishing post. These and the celebratory 'Catch The Beat' all helped to move Jamaican music into the reggae dimension.

A number of important songs that helped to consolidate the reggae beat in late 1968 were produced by Bunny Lee, who says it was partly his link with the British Pama label that indirectly led to the emergence of the new sound. The Palmer brothers, three Jamaican immigrants who controlled valuable property interests in Northwest London, established Pama in 1967. It was set up as competition to Island's group of labels, the main issuer of Jamaican product in the UK since Chris Blackwell moved the company to London in 1962. Other concerns such as Sonny Roberts' Planetone, Emil Shalit's Melodise and Rita Issels and Benny King's R&B/ Ska Beat were also issuing quantities of Jamaican music. One of Island's subsidiary labels, Trojan, was established in 1967 to handle Duke Reid's product; the following year, it was relaunched to handle reggae from any producer by Lee Gopthal, a Jamaican of Indian descent who was originally the landlord of Island's office space. Gopthal was also the man behind the Muzik City chain of London record shops, and had founded the B&C distribution company. Though Pama initially beat Island in terms of reggae popularity in Britain, Trojan would shortly emerge as the most prominent reggae label in the country.

**Harry Palmer [David Katz]**

In the Pama camp, Harry Palmer was originally the brother most involved in record production. At his real estate office in Mandeville I note a close resemblance to his younger brother Carlisle, present head of the massive Jet Star record label and distribution company in London; both have a hook nose and Semitic features derived from their Jewish grandmother. Harry says that the family lived in Thompson Town, Clarendon, where his father was a Baptist church deacon and Justice _of_ the Peace with links to PNP founder Nonnan Manley. Harry's older brother Geoffrey left Jamaica for London in the mid-1950s, and Harry and Carlisle followed a few years later. 'Young people always looking for adventure,' he explains, 'and underdeveloped Jamaica didn't offer a lot of adventure. Geoffrey went in '55 to Willesden Polytechnic, he did engineering.' When Harry arrived in Willesden, he got a menial job 'in a factory, parcel packing', and eventually received a qualification in business administration. Carlisle came in the early 1960s and studied engineering.

Though Harry's chief line of work was in the insurance field, and though Carlisle would later be engaged in property, the Palmer brothers were involved in the music business as early as 1962. 'After studying I went into insurance, and from insurance we opened our first record shop, called Happy Sounds, at 827 Harrow Road near Scrubs Lane. That record shop was with a partner from the insurance business, but he was an electrician originally, so we stocked electrical fittings as well as records. By then Geoff had opened Club 31, at 31 Harrow Road in Stonebridge Park; it was a basement club, but it was very popular. All the great Tamla-Motown artists used to appear there: Stevie Wonder, the Jackson Five, Diana Ross, Martha and the Vandellas.' In 1968 Geoff opened a larger venue that he still controls today: the London Apollo Club on Willesden High Road.

Harry says that Happy Sounds began stocking rock and pop hits, but later carried Jamaican music, selling a phenomenal quantity of 'My Boy Lollipop' by Millie Small. 'We sold some four thousand copies of "My Boy Lollipop" in that little shop. We put a speaker outside, and it was [on] a bus route, and people would come from all over.'

Around 1966, a fire destroyed Happy Sounds. Geoff continued running his night club, while Harry and Carl set up an estate agency across the road from their former record shop. The brothers also began managing local bands, which led to a few tentative record productions issued by Island in 1967; Harry says the first was 'Do The Teasy' by Joyce Bond, backed by a band of young Jamaicans based in Kensal Rise called the Shadrocks, featuring singer Tim Chandell.

Though the Palmers thought Joyce Bond had commercial potential, they found the major labels had no interest in her style. 'We used to go to Phillips and Pye simply because Chris Blackwell went to them. They did Steve Winwood and the Spencer Davis Group, so we thought we could get in. We started thinking about putting it out ourselves, but then Island agreed to put it out.' As a follow-up to 'Do The Teasy' Island issued 'Tell Me What It's All About', written by Jackie Edwards, and some Shadrocks material such as 'Go Go Special', but then Harry Palmer travelled to Jamaica in search of undiscovered talent and resultant licensing deals saw the foundation of Pama records.

'By this time we had fallen out with Joyce Bond, and I had come down to find a replacement female singer. I stayed for maybe two weeks in Kingston, put an ad in the paper for female singers, and I went back to London with three mailbags full of applications.' He also attracted the attention of Jamaica's leading record producers: 'Coxsone Dodd, Bunny Lee, Scratch, everybody. We got this record by Clancy Eccles, "What Will Your Mama Say", went back with it and nobody wanted to put it out, so we formed Pama records and that was our first tune. We had to pay him a decent advance, maybe a couple of thousand pounds, which was a lot of money then. And we were new into the business, so he got a large percentage - larger than what we learned to pay later on.' Palmer also licensed material from Alton Ellis and brought an unknown singer called Beverley Simmonds to London to record. 'When we took her back to London, Otis Redding died, so we decided to make a tribute album. Same recording session we made an instrumental called "The Champ", based on a US soul thing. Although we put out the album called _Tribute To Otis Redding,_ the instrumental just took off. Atlantic licensed it and it was a hit in America – that was the first release on Cotillion.' Harry reveals that 'Champ', credited to the Mohawks on release in 1968, was played by 'all white guys, session people', with Alan Hawkshaw on organ.

Pama records was formed in a branch of their estate agency in Peterborough Road, Harrow, but moved shortly afterwards to a permanent base at 17 Craven Park Road, Harlesden – a larger building with room for proper offices and space to hold stock for distribution. Harry says the Bunny Lee link forged in the summer of 1968 placed Pama above the competition. 'Christmas '68 was really when it started to happen. Both Island and Trojan conceded that we had the Christmas – we had all the big things through Bunny Lee. In Harrow I had a phone call from Bunny Lee, but he was dealing with Island; he thought he wasn't getting such a good deal, so he wanted a little switch. He had tapes of his own, but we didn't want those, because they were already offered to Island. He asked us to come and see him and played us a tape called "Hold You Jack" by Derrick Morgan, but we knew [Island A&R man David] Betteridge and Blackwell and everybody, so we weren't about to take what they had. We gave him £2400 to come down and make records, and he did extremely well, but Bunny also wanted to outdo Clancy, because he realized that Clancy had a good deal, so he wanted to be in on it as well. Matter of fact, when he went back down to Jamaica I think there was some scuffling between them.'

Lee himself has a different recollection of his meeting with the Palmer brothers in 1968, claiming that his link with the company was forged after a dispute arose involving control of an album by London-based singer Dandy Livingston, who was then enjoying a big hit with an anti-rude boy song featuring Rico, 'A Message To You Rudie'. 'I go England the first time in February 1968, and I used to deal with Island. Dandy Livingston used to work for Rita Issels in Stamford Hill [North London], she used to pay him twenty pound a week as a producer. Me and Dandy meet and we turn good friends. I take Dandy round a Jackie Edwards' house and them start to be good brethren. Pama give Dandy some money to make an LP, but Dandy and me go to Island and me introduce him to Dave Betteridge. Dandy say him have an LP coming named _Dandy Returns,_ so Dave Betteridge then stay 'pon the phone and sell about twenty-five thousand right there and then, 'cause Dandy said him would give it to them. When the three Pama brothers find me over my yard in the East End, they start to talk about the Dandy thing. Them say they don't really want to lock up Dandy for we all are black men... them say Harry Palmer did come to Jamaica and give Clancy and Alton money and it didn't work out neither. Beverley Simmonds was at the Sheraton and him carry her up and do a whole LP with some Otis Redding tunes, but nothing didn't come of it. Them say them down 'pon them face and them no have no money, but he would give me three hundred pounds cash, that's all them can afford. I say, "I will give you some tune, we can work together." I say we have to make a label with a black hand and a white hand shake 'cross a mountain named Unity. I go back to Jamaica and Pama and Bunny Lee now amalgamated.' Unity was the first of several subsidiary labels controlled by Pama, most of which were created specifically to showcase the efforts of one producer.

With the money Lee received from Pama in London he ran a session in October 1968 that resulted in several early reggae hits, the most noteworthy of which were 'Bangarang' by Stranger Cole and Lester Sterling and 'Everybody Needs Love' by Slim Smith. ' "Bangarang" is the first reggae record make in Jamaica,' insists Stranger Cole. 'I was passing by Treasure Isle one day and I was told that Bunny Lee is making records, so I went up to see him because he's a good friend of mine, we go to Denham Town School together. He said, "I have a song here called 'Bongo Chant' [originally by Kenny Graham's Afro Cubist Group]. I would love to put some words to it, could you come up with some words?". I think for five minutes and come up with "Muma no want bangarang", and we went in the studio and it was a hit instantly. The melody came from some American song and I put new words to it. You could say that the melody belongs to somebody else, and the words belong to me.'

Striker Lee agrees that this was the first reggae song, naming the shuffling organ as its defining element. Slim Smith's 'Everybody Needs Love', an adaptation of a Motown soul hit, was also marked by a livid organ shuffle, played on both tunes by Glen Adams, then chiefly known as an auditions assistant and arranger at Treasure Isle.

**Glen Adams [David Katz]**

Adams started out as a singer and came to play organ on the session almost by accident. 'It all started from my mother,' Adams says. 'My mother was a dancer and a singer. She put on a show around 1957 – it was the Jiving Juniors with Derrick Harriott, so I saw them young men singing and it motivate me. I form a group when I was around twelve with some friends and I went to Palace Theatre where I auditioned for _Vere Johns' Opportunity Hour._ They didn't pick our song that time, so I went back by myself and I got selected.' Adams is reminiscing in his unofficial office: the local Dun-kin' Donuts in his corner of New York's Flatbush. Despite a pale and evidently mentally disturbed woman yelling at the top of her lungs about God's punishments from the table behind, he readily digs deep into memories of his early days in music. 'I usually do cabaret, places like Club Havana, Bungalow, Penguin Club. I used to do the show circuit alongside the Blues Busters and the Indian Rubber Boy. Mr Dodd had a talent show and during the rehearsal he heard this original song, "Wonder Thirst", and wanted to record it – it's a love lyric, written by my sister. I did that song for Mr Dodd. It never released, but I was popularly known for that song, because I got the dub and I gave it to a popular sound system [selector], Lord Comic. I just do one song for Studio One, but I was always a fan of Mr Dodd, and I have maximum respect for him for giving me the opportunity. Even until this day, Mr Dodd will remain my best friend.'

As a friend of Ken Boothe's , Adams was present at the recording of Stranger Cole's "Uno Dos Tres", sowing the seeds of a long association with Duke Reid. 'Me and Ken Boothe started practising and we did a couple of songs with Stranger Cole – some harmonies on 'Uno Dos Tres'. Me and Ken used to sing as Ken and Glen. We came second in the first Festival Song Competition in Ward Theatre with "I Remember" [in 1966].' Glen then went to work in tailoring; in his musical life, he passed through the Heptones and joined the Pioneers in time for some of their earliest recordings. 'Heptones was myself, Joseph Forrester, Jackson Jones, Earl Morgan and Barry Llewelyn; Jackson was the guitarist. Then we split up the group, and I go with the Pioneers – that time Winston Hewitt went to Canada, and I fill in for him about a year or so. I did "Shake It Up", "Good Nanny", and a couple more for Joe Gibbs and Ken Lack. Anywhere we travel, the Heptones would travel too. After Pioneers, I took Heptones to Studio One. Ken Boothe was there – he had "The Train Is Coming". And Jackie Mittoo was there, who I knew – I knew most of the guys because my sister Yvonne was a popular singer. I have a nice sister, so naturally the guys want to be my friend!'

Glen says he began playing piano on rehearsals for Duke Reid in the late ska era, and that Reid indirectly instigated his organ playing on 'Bangarang' for Bunny Lee. 'I usually practise down by Duke Reid – I actually rehearse and shape the song "Woman A Come" with Marguerita [Mahfood]. I selected artists for Mr Reid like Joe White, and [Reid] is the one who gave me the incentive to play. After the Pioneers, I was doing a lot of stuff for Bunny Lee, singing "Silent Lover", a song named "She" [aka 'She's Leaving' by Alva Lewis, recorded at Bunny Lee's inaugural session], "Taking Over Orange Street", "Hold Down Miss Winey". I sang harmony with the Uniques with Slim Smith on "Hooray" and "Give Me A Love".' Duke sent him to play piano on Bunny Lee's session, as Gladdy's band refused to play because they were still owed money from a previous session. 'Bunny Lee didn't know I play. I tried to play the piano on "Bangarang" but it didn't work out, so Lester Sterling asked us to switch, make Lester Sterling play the piano and let me play the organ. I'd never played the organ before.' On the same session, Lloyd Charmers gave Glen some pointers for the organ chops he supplied on 'Everybody Needs Love', with Charmers himself providing the exciting lead lines. After the immediate and far-reaching success of 'Bangarang' and 'Everybody Needs Love', Glen Adams has stuck to the organ ever since.

Just as Glen made his organ debut on this session, his singing partner Alva Lewis made his recording debut as a guitarist. Known as Reggie Carter, due to a resemblance to a local comedian of that name, Lewis was born in Manchester in 1949. Sitting in the waiting room at Tuff Gong studio, Lewis nowadays looks decidedly worse for wear. He had a long period of alcohol dependency, and walks with a limp; though still occasionally used on recording sessions, he is often out of work. Like many of his peers, Lewis came into music in the Greenwich Farm community. 'When I was sixteen I left Manchester, come to Kingston and stayed with my brother in West Avenue, Greenwich Farm. They wanted me to get educated, send me to college, but something is inside of me: me want to play music. My bigger brother was in America and he send a semi-acoustic Gibson, an electric Fender guitar and a small amplifier. You have a bar downstairs, but through I no hear the guitar them so much, [my] guitar never properly tuned. Derrick Morgan was living over [by] Bunny Lee, and the Melodians, they come one Sunday and Derrick asked one of the Melodians to tune my guitar. Them play the guitar and me observe them. Bunny Lee and Slim Smith carry me down to Duke Reid's studio one day and the first tunes me lick, "Everybody Needs Love" and "Bangarang", the songs was two number one. The backing band was Reggie, Family Man, Carly, Glen Adams and Lloyd Charmers play the solo with the organ.'

It was this backing band – usually without Lloyd Charmers, and often with lead guitarist Ronny Bop – that became one of the crucial studio combos that developed the reggae sound, first as the Bunny Lee All Stars, later as the Upsetters, and ultimately as backing to the Wailers. An earlier version of the group had been established on the live scene as the Hippy Boys, which Family Man and Carly joined before this pivotal session for Bunny Lee.

Aston 'Family Man' Barrett previously made an impact on record with his solid bass backing of the Uniques. His debut came on a single that adapted two American hits, Buffalo Springfield's 'For What It's Worth' (as 'Watch This Sound') and the Manhattans' 'I'm The One That Love Forgot' (as 'Out Of Love'). The session was arranged by Lloyd Charmers for Winston Lowe, a smaller producer of Chinese descent whose Tramp label was based in Greenwich Farm. Jimmy Riley explains that the refrain 'What's that sound?' from the anti-Vietnam War 'For What It's Worth' was transformed into 'Watch This Sound' because he misunderstood the lyrics when he first heard the song on the radio. 'I was sleeping one night and I heard the phrase "watch this sound". I couldn't make out all the words they was saying, so I just make "watch this sound" as the chorus. That was the first song Family Man played bass on. He was playing with a little band, and then we decided we were going to use him on the session. The drummer was Winston Grennan, Bobby Aitken on guitar, Ansel Collins on piano. We did "Watch This Sound" and "Out Of Love". Both sides hit, but "Watch This Sound" hit first.'

**Aston 'Family Man' Barrett [David Katz]**

Aston Barrett and his younger brother Carlton grew up at 26 Beeston Street, sharing a downtown Kingston yard with saxophonist Val Bennett. Nicknamed 'Family Man' (Fams for short) because of the children he fathered with different partners at an early age, Aston was an unruly teenager, sent to the Jamaica Youth Corps' Cobbla Camp for discipline. There he led a harmony quintet, the Jiving Crackerballs, which influenced his approach to bass. 'I love singing,' he declares in a deep, slow drawl, 'so when I'm playing bass, I always imagine that I am singing. I try to play a kind of melodic bass.' Barrett names Lloyd Brevett, Lloyd Spence and Jackie Jackson as his most immediate influences, stressing that 'one good artist always inspire from another'. The greater prominence _of_ Family Man's beefy bass parts, which often emphasized offbeat cadences through audible rest stops, would greatly elevate the emerging reggae sound. By the time of Family Man's debut session he and Carly were playing in the Hippy Boys, a live band that evolved after the demise of the Gaylads. Web Stewart was the group's lead guitarist when Fams and Carly were drafted in for a regular engagement at the Baby Grand. Their lead vocalist was then Maxwell Livingston Smith, former leader of the Emotions trio and better known to the world as Max Romeo.

In the late 1980s, Romeo was a short-haired Buddhist living with a white woman in New York. Having gone through many serious life changes, he would go through several more after returning to Jamaica and allowing his dreadlocks to grow back. He spent most of the 1990s living on West Avenue in Greenwich Farm, back on the same block where Bunny Lee was based when the new sound was coming together in 1968.

Romeo smiles as he recounts his early days. 'I was born in the parish of Saint Anne in Alexandria, an area about four miles out of Browns Town. My mother had to migrate to England in 1954. I was ten years old then, so I was taken to Kingston to live with my father. Me and my father's wife couldn't get along, so I had to run away from home. After drifting around for years, trying to find myself, when I was about seventeen I followed a friend to a place and saw this man looking at me. He called me over and said, "A brown man like you, brown complexion... ." Those days in Jamaica, high-complexion people were looked upon to live a certain standard, and I was suffering then, one pants all torn, shirt without sleeves, shoes with hole in the sole and the heel leaning. He gave me a piece of paper and a pencil and said, "I want you to write twenty things in life you think you would want to be." I wrote from mechanic, electrician, painter, carpenter, pastor, singer. I gave him the paper and he said, "I want you to narrow it down to two." I was trying to find the easiest of them, so it came down to preacher and singer. He said, "Now I want you to choose one of the two," so I choose singer. He said, "From now on, you'r e a singer. Don't let nobody call you anything other than a singer."

'I left with that in my mind and then I started working with Ken Lack, taking records to the record shop. He heard me singing one day and said, "Hey, give it a try." The first song I did was "A Rainbow", and it went to number two on the charts. I started with two other guys singing harmony – Lloyd Shakespeare, the brother of [bassist] Robbie Shakespeare, and Kenneth Knight – and we called the group Emotions. After a little while, there was disagreements in the group. I decided to work on my own, and I met Bunny Lee then. At that time, he was working as a desk clerk at Kingston Industrial Garage. He wasn't yet really in the [music] business, [but] he used to be around Duke Reid, take the records to the radio station, see to it that they play it by any means necessary. Duke Reid gave him some studio time, and Ken Lack and myself put together some money to buy the tapes. Tommy McCook and the Supersonics played for him and he came up with a hit: Roy Shirley, ' "Music Field". That was the starting point for Bunny Lee, "Striker". He did "Get On The Ball" with Roy Shirley. A set of songs was recorded then, and that launched the whole Bunny Lee era. I was around then, but I wasn't actually singing because I had to find a name.'

**Max Romeo [Adrian Thomas]**

His debut hit as a solo singer, once his determined courtship of a local girl earned him the name Max Romeo, came after Bunny Lee asked him to write a rude song to be sung over the rhythm of Derrick Morgan's 'Hold You Jack', a number already laden with minor innuendo.

'I started moving with Striker,' Max continues. 'That's how "Wet Dream" come into play, the first song with Bunny Lee. He came with this idea about doing this dirty song.' When Romeo had written something suitably suggestive, he found that Slim Smith, Roy Shirley, John Holt and Derrick Morgan all refused to sing it, forcing Romeo to do the honours on a session at Studio One. The resultant song, issued on Unity, stayed in the UK charts a full six months despite a radio ban, and brought Romeo to Britain for live performances. It also led the Palmer brothers to open a record shop in Kingston, above music producer and goods trader H. Robinson's Caribbean Disc company.

'Bunny Lee said we should open a branch of Pama Records in Jamaica, which we did, at 118 Orange Street,' notes Harry Palmer. 'It lasted for a couple of years, then Bunny Lee took it over as Bunny Lee records, because we can't manage Bunny for too long.'

Max Romeo's UK performances were preceded by the Ethiopians, who were brought to Britain in 1968 by a booking agency called Commercial Entertainment, run by former rock musicians Bruce White and Tony Cousins (aka Anthony Bautista). It was the first of many tours for Jamaican artists arranged by the company, and the pair would subsequently be involved in record production and artist management. ' "Train To Skaville" went off in England, was on the chart, and Bruce White and Tony Cousins, who later becomes Rhino Records, they request me up there,' Leonard Dillon recalls. 'We play all over England, three months this year and two months the other year. The audiences were mixed. I worked some places in Kent and in the audience is one black I see.'

Other popular Ethiopians' songs surfaced in this time on Doctor Bird, the label established by Graeme Goodall when he emigrated to Britain. 'Graeme Goodall engineered "The Whip", "Stay Loose Mama" and "Cool It Amigo" at West Indies with Linford Anderson. At that time, Lynn Taitt and the Jets was the star band at the studio, and I always hire the hornsman, like Trommy [Vin Gordon]. Doctor Bird records was the sole distributor [in the UK].'

When the Ethiopians returned from their first UK tour they began recording with 'Sir JJ' Johnson, the producer who drew the best of their abilities in the early reggae style. 'When I got back from the first tour, I met JJ at his record shop at 136 Orange Street. As a artist, you walk through these places, because them time some nice girl used to be in them places, and you have to go nice with them and buy them a lunch. When people come in to buy records, they'l l introduce people to your record, so you have to be around, niceing the people. I went up by JJ – he wasn't really in the business too tough, but he was a great man, my best producer. He just look upon me and say, "Write a song about everything crash." We went in WIRL studio and did it with Bobby Aitken's band, the Caribbeats. At that time, all essential services them in Jamaica was on strike – it's between them and them governmental system where pay is concerned. My politics is Africa, Rasta, and I don't go into them political business. I beat them every time musically, that's all I can do – when I see them doing a likkle thing, I write song at it.'

'Everything Crash' commented on the growing social unrest that reflected increasing dissatisfaction with the party in power. Jamaica had been independent for over five years, yet for the majority of its citizens, little had improved in real terms. The general strike was one of a series of widespread protests to grip the nation that year, and Dillon's song, despite his purported apolitical stance, captured the frustration and indignation with which the public experienced it.

Providing harmony on the song was Melvin Reid, who was featured on subsequent Ethiopians hits for JJ such as 'Hong Kong Flu' and 'Woman A Capture Man'. The former dealt with an outbreak of dengue fever in Jamaica, the latter with romantic situations, apparently written by a schoolgirl whom Dillon heard singing in a park. Both numbers, and further material such as 'Well Red', all feature upfront hand drumming, evidence of Dillon's longstanding awareness of his African origin and a coded signifier of his Rastafari faith.

'I always have drums in my thing,' he explains. 'I never leave the congo drum. I was taught about Africa by my grandmother and grandfather. They taught me the tribe that I'm from, Ashanti, and the name of my ancient African ancestor, the first man that reach to Jamaica: Quao.' He was drawn to the wisdom of Rastafari after attending services in the ghetto. 'It's a in-born thing, but there was a place in Waterhouse called the Ethiopian Reorganization Centre, [run by] two elder Rastas, Nasser King and Daddy King, and that's where we really spring from. We learned a lot from them where Ethiopia is concerned, and that's where we used to be, before I start sing as Ethiopians, but after the Jack Sparrow days.'

Enjoying a strong working relationship, the Ethiopians stayed with Sir JJ until he stopped making records in about 1971. 'He was very reluctant,' Dillon recalls. 'He's not a man who's really into a whole lot of competition and he could not take the fight. The radio station them never used to play the song and you have to let go a lot of money. Everything becomes corrupt after a while and he just ease up out of it and start meditating on his jukeboxes – he was a jukebox agent.'

Dillon notes the producer's wealthy origins, but has only praise for the man regarding his business practices and personality. 'His father is one of the biggest bus company magnates in Jamaica, and his brother had Johnson's drive-in restaurant and club up the top of Maxfield Avenue. He was a conscious black man, [with a] sensible manner, [an] irie man, cool. My best producer, and me wasn't on no contract. He say, "You no have to sign a contract, me live up to my word, you just have to live up to yours." That's what happened, so that's why I sticked with him so long – no producer had me that long.'

Roy Shirley, who recorded a few less successful tunes with the Uniques for JJ, is less flattering in his description of the producer. 'He was always a little guy in the production field. You couldn't put them man against Beverleys, Federal, Coxsone. JJ had a lot of money and his father had some bus company before that, but the guy look so simple – he always dress like them way-out cowboy and walk that way, but him was a money guy, so he could pay his way to promote his tune on the radio.'

**Rupie Edwards [Courtesy of Top Beat]**

The sad postscript to Sir JJ's career is that he was robbed and murdered in his Kingston home in 1973. 'It's just something intending,' Leonard Dillon states gravely, 'because it's three times them shot him, and he was living with shot in him. The fourth time they shot him, and then they cut him throat.'

Another producer based on Orange Street was Rupie Edwards, who opened his Success record shop just as the reggae beat was coming up in 1968. Edwards is a short, light-skinned man who likes to laugh. Early one cold Sunday morning, at the record shop in East London's Ridley Market that he has occupied since the mid-1970s, he and a young niece are gulping shots of vodka from polystyrene cups while gospel music blares at top volume. Rupie says he began his career as a singer and piano player. 'I used to sing in school, started beating drums and make a few little instruments out of bamboo and pumpkin vine, anything I can blow or beat and do a sound. I had a little band from them times. From there, I went on _Vere Johns' Opportunity Knocks._ After I left school at fifteen, I started training as a mechanic. I started recording in 1961 – that was for Hi-Lite, Little Wonder. "Guilty Convict" was the first record I did, and "Just Because" was the other side. I played piano in a night club during the same time: Kittymat, on Maxfield Avenue near Spanish Town Road.'

Edwards says the next recording he made was a sound system special for Count Bells, for which he was paid 'seven quid'. In the mid-sixties he formed a duo called the Ambassadors. 'Me and Junior Menz, who became the leader of the Techniques, we was singing together as the Ambassadors, we made "Amen" [an Impressions tune] and "Mother's Choice" for Harry J and got fifteen quid. Then I just started producing for myself, in 1966 at Coxsone, but I wasn't going to make a living from that. I recorded four songs on that first session, me and Junior as the same Ambassadors, live with the Virtues. Graeme Goodall was the engineer, along with Coxsone himself. I released the first on a little label I called Diamond. Then, by about '68, I toured Canada with the Virtues.'

Edwards says he first met Coxsone and other important music industry contacts while working in downtown Kingston garages. 'I was a motor mechanic and I used to work on his car and Duke's car; I started learning the trade at KIG [Kingston Industrial Garage], but I finished over United Motors – Bunny Lee was there.' While Edwards was working at Studio One, he recorded 'Let Me Love You' and 'If You Can't Beat Them, Join Them', material that Coxsone intended to release but which Rupie himself ended up handling. 'He wasn't releasing them quick enough for me, so he sold them back to me for twenty quid. He actually wanted an album out of me, but then I was also headed in his direction: to run my own company.' Hence the formation of the Success record shop and label, which took time to live up to its name despite a few early discs that made some local impact.

One of Edwards' notable acts was the Concords, a trio led on some numbers by Gregory Isaacs. 'Gregory came to me as lead singer with the Concords. The first song he did was "Don't Let Me Suffer". He was the lead singer in that, then the Concords went back and did "Buttoo" – or it could have been "Buttoo" first, I can't remember.' As Edwards could not recall the details, I went to the African Museum record shop to ask Isaacs himself.

In the late 1970s and early 1980s Gregory Isaacs was probably the best-known reggae singer after Bob Marley and Dennis Brown, but subsequent problems with substance abuse and unlicensed firearms have resulted in an uneven career marked by spells in jail. Gregory's best-known shop was downtown on Chancery Lane, a meeting point for musicians popularly known as the Idler's Rest; his current shop and Poor People's Recording Studio are located on one of the rougher patches of Red Hills Road, an area marred by recent gang violence linked to a turf war over control of the local drug trade. At the crumbling and nearly abandoned complex that houses African Museum a group of particularly intimidating young men are gathered on the staircase, scowling with an obvious contempt that is vaguely threatening. When a distinctly jumpy Isaacs eventually arrives he gives the impression of having a short attention span, but his recollections are fairly frank and highly informative.

'I was born 1950 in Western Kingston, grow in Denham Town,' he begins, 'grown by my mother. From early childhood, my friends used to tell me that I am good, like school concerts and those things. Sam Cooke, Otis Redding, those were my favourites.' His recording debut came in the late sixties. 'I started out single, with a song called "Another Heartache", produced by me and Winston Sinclair, a youth that I grow up with that like the music business too – he sing the next side, and we put it out on a blank. I did one song for Prince Buster, "Dancing Floor". He asked me to do it, because my friend was a close friend of his, Jim Brown.' Is he referring to the Jim Brown who was a political 'enforcer' and notorious gunman for the JLP? 'Yeah, same Jim Brown,' Isaacs replies in a matter-of-fact tone. 'We grew in the same Western Kingston. After "Dancing Floor", I start singing for Rupie Edwards – that's where the Concords come in. We did songs like "Don't Let Me Suffer" – quite a few.' Gregory names the other two members as 'a brethren called Bramwell and one named Pengro, they came from Western Kingston', adding that 'for Rupie Edwards, I did songs like "Each Day" and "Lonely Man".'

**Original rudeboy: Gregory Isaacs [David Katz]**

Though Rupie Edwards first travelled to England in 1969, licensing Concords' material to Trojan's Blue Cat subsidiary and establishing the Escort label with Pama, he did not have a substantial Jamaican hit until 1970, when Dobbie Dobson's sugary ballad 'That Wonderful Sound' reached the charts. Greater fame was achieved later in the decade.

Like Edwards' ironically named Success imprint, Alvin Ranglin's GG label took time to land a major hit. Ranglin's current base is near Kingston's Heroes' Circle, at the recording studio he bought from executive George Benson some years ago. The busy premises also house his pressing plant and offices, and, as Ranglin used to run a bakery from an adjacent building, the whole place smells of sour yeast and buttermilk. He is obviously an astute businessman, as he owns several choice pieces of property around the island and often cruises around town in a crisp utility vehicle, blasting gospel music on a high-powered stereo. He is a short, portly man with little hair left on his head, and he often breaks into a hoarse laugh as he speaks of growing up in the country. 'I was born in 1942 in a small district known as Eden – it's in the middle of Jamaica in Clarendon. In that area, most people does farming and cattle-rearing, and raising chicks has always been good for feeding people. Myself, I had to go and milk the cows in the morning. I leave from there to May Pen at age thirteen, because there was a better school in the higher part of the city.' It was as a teenager that Ranglin began performing, though his introduction to music came far earlier. 'In 1958 I started to sing on concerts, sometimes they throw up a penny for you, but from age three or four I was sent by my mother to a lady a few blocks from me that always play piano and organ for the church. I was brought up in the Adventist church, so from age four or five I sing in the choir.'

**Alvin 'GG' Ranglin [David Katz]**

When Ranglin came of age, he worked in a number of unsatisfying trades before venturing into music. 'I wanted to learn carpenter, then I started to get fed up, because the teaching from the elder gentleman was not up to date. Then I remove to mason, but I wanted to do something else again, because I believed I could do better. My mother put me into welding, and I get eye flash [an injury], and I decided again not to go back. Those times, people from here usually migrate to London. My mother said she's going to send me there, but I decided that I don't want to go. I decided that I would be an electrician, then I start learning at Yarmouth Estate in the South Coast of Clarendon, attend different schools and took course from National Technology, and I work as a radio and television technician. I used to build tube amplifiers, the KT88. Those used to get the music more to the people, so I always try to own a discotheque for myself, called GG. GG was a business name coming from family members, like Gloria and her sister, and another cousin "Girlfriend", so we just formulate it like that.'

Having a sound system meant it was an easy step to enter record production, especially as Ranglin was also involved in the jukebox industry. 'We start with the Maytones and we had Trevor Brown from Linstead. He used to sing some Christmas songs like "Every Day Is A Holiday". I usually sing with another gentleman, Emmanuel Flowers, as Flowers and Alvin: "Howdy & Tenky", it was a number one for many months. That was some part of the starting] point, but I went into so much business, because the jukebox business at that time was big, so that helped me to promote the songs them. Everybody didn't have a radio, only the middle class or the wealthy could buy that, but the poor persons could go and play with the shilling and ten cents into those jukebox, so I bought a few jukebox by the help of my mother. Then I was in the game machine business as well.' By this point Ranglin had already opened his first record store in May Pen, as well as a TV repair shop. His first Kingston store was at 45 Slipe Road, and he eventually opened shops in Half Way Tree and Old Harbour and abroad in Brooklyn and London.

GG produced the first recordings by the Maytones duo in late 1968 or early 1969; as both lead singer Vernon Buckley and his partner Gladstone Grant are natives of May Pen, their style is infused with provincial elements. 'I grow up with my grandmother, and I was compelled to go to a Seventh Day Adventist church every Saturday,' says Buckley at home in Montreal. 'I never really active in choir, but I used to do a lot of observation. A block down the road, there was a Church of God. Sundays, they would clap hands, so sometimes you get some good vibes. I used to do the bookwork for my dad – he was a government contractor. Gladstone Grant was driving a mini-bus. The town that I live in is May Pen, so we just give the group the name Mighty Maytones. Someone tell us about GG in our early days and we went down for [an] audition [at] his shop in May Pen. My first song never been released – we went into town, recording at Studio One, did "A Little Bit Of This" and "Land Of Music". A few months [later] we went to Dynamic, did "Loving Reggae" – wow, that was a hit. The next big hit was "Funny Man". We go in the studio more regular and songs keep coming, like "Bowee Wowee", "Serious Love".' The Maytones continued to supply Ranglin with hits until Buckley moved to Canada in 1980; he also assisted with the running of Ranglin's May Pen shop from 1971.

**The present site of GG records [Bernard Warin]**

Another notable record label formed in 1968 was Techniques, set up by Winston Riley at his home on West Street as a vehicle for the Techniques group; he began licensing material to Pama in 1969. Riley's first hit production was 'Warrior' by Johnny Osbourne and the Sensations, a song decrying the nation's growing polarity. 'That was the early time when this Jamaican political uprising was in its baby stage, and the people just starting to have political warfare,' Osbourne says, relaxing at a Boston hotel the day after a well-attended performance. Unlike the clear tenor that graces most of his records, his speaking voice is a low, raspy growl. 'Guns wasn't so prevalent, but the people were taking sides politically, and then it becomes a two-sided thing.'

Osbourne says time spent at Alpha set him on a musical path. 'I were born in 1947, grew up all over Kingston, but in the latter years it was more Western Kingston. I went to Alpha boys' school in my teens. I even join the Alpha band, learning to play the trumpet four or five years. Alpha was always teaching you all kinds of things: we have school choir, church choir, and the music teacher, Mrs Hall, always motivated us. I get to understand music some more, and I was really interested in music from then.'

**Vernon Buckley of The Maytones [Courtesy of Vernon Buckley, with thanks to Michael Turner]**

Osbourne subsequently began to record after mingling with local street-corner groups. 'There was a band called the Wildcats I used to sing with – Earl "Bagga" Walker used to be around that band. We used to come together in the evening time, practise these American songs and learn the harmony. The first tune that I recorded was "All I Have Is Love". That was for the band, but I guess the manager figure that he was just an independent producer, so seeing how it was done at Studio One, he gave it back to Mr Dodd and it [was] released on Studio One.'

**Winston Riley at work [Beth Lesser]**

**Johnny Osbourne [Beth Lesser]**

Osbourne subsequently began recording for Winston Riley with the Sensations, then composed of Jackie Parris, Buster Riley and Bobby Davis. 'Me and the Sensations, Techniques, Noel "Bunny" Brown, Tinga Stewart, we always run into each other somewhere, or somebody may say "Come here, sing this harmony", and then you just get involved. The Sensations was going on, so if they'r e making a record and I would be around, if I can sing a particular harmony, I might just chip in and sing that harmony, and if I'm recording a song, they do that. In 1968-9 I recorded "Come Back Darling" for Winston Riley, "See And Blind", "Warrior", "Fish Mouth", and by the summer of 1969 he compiled the first album, called _Come Back Darling.'_

All these songs were highly popular, the album's title track being a particularly big hit. ' "Come Back Darling" was about one of my little girlfriend when I was young,' Osbourne laughs. 'She moved to another area, I couldn't see her for a time, so I sit down with my guitar and the words just came up. But I happened to migrate to Canada right in that same month that I did the album, so I never even see that girl again.'

Despite the departure _of_ this rising star, Winston Riley continued to gain prestige as a producer. In 1970, Trojan launched a UK branch of the Techniques label after the incredible success of Dave and Ansel Collins' 'Double Barrel'; though the song itself was never popular in Jamaica, its success gave Riley the opportunity to open a record shop on Orange Street.

Whilst the smaller, ghetto-based promoters made their entry-level smashes in reggae, changes at Studio One were also bringing important elements to the new style. The most notable involved Leroy Sibbles' mastery _of_ the bass guitar and engineer Sylvan Morris' special effects, many born from budgetary restraints.

The Heptones' early work at Studio One featured the bass of various session players. Wally Cameron played on several, and Boris Gardiner, a veteran of big live bands such as Kes Chin's Souvenirs and Carlos Malcolm's Afro-Jamaicans, played on noteworthy hits such as 'Party Time'. But when Jackie Mittoo introduced Leroy Sibbles to the bass guitar, the course _of_ reggae history was radically altered.

'Jackie said he wanted to put a trio together to do a weekend gig at the Tit For Tat club on Red Hills Road,' Sibbles explains. 'He said he had the drummer Billy Wade, but he didn't have the bass man and he wanted me to play bass. I said, "Me? I never play bass!" He said, "Grab the bass guitar there", because the studio had a Fender jazz bass, and he hummed the line of one of the songs that he had out, and I find the line and start playing it. We start practising, played the gig and tore the goddam place up for weeks in and weeks out.'

Sibbles came up with memorable and lasting bass parts at Studio One, creating several of the best-loved and most-covered bass lines in the history of Jamaican popular music. 'The first song that I recorded, [the Cables'] "Baby Why", and the next side too, "What Kind Of World", those are two of the biggest bass lines ever recorded in Jamaica – "What Kind Of World", they just revived that rhythm for [Morgan Heritage's international smash hit] "Down By The River". I realized from that time on, the most-revived bass lines in Jamaica is the ones that I play.'

The bass of ska was an acoustic stand-up, frantically pumping to the beat; rock steady was an electric sound, with more audible and typically melodic strolling lines. As the early reggae bass is even more to the fore, its melodic features take a heightened emphasis, particularly at Studio One where augmented equipment boosted bass frequencies. In the hands of Leroy Sibbles the bass took on a quality of gravity; instead of the bright bounce of 'Nanny Goat', the patterns of songs like 'What Kind Of World' and 'Baby Why' seem emotionally laden, the sense of movement contrasted by subtle moments of rest.

Cornell Campbell notes that Sibbles played bass on 'Stars' and 'Queen Of The Minstrel', two landmark early reggae hits featuring Campbell's chilling falsetto, cut while he was leading a group called the Eternals. According to Campbell, the tunes were originally earmarked for a smaller producer, but changed shape when he brought them to Studio One. 'I formed Eternals with Ken Price and Errol Wisdom as a rival to the Uniques and the Sensations. "Queen Of The Minstrel", "Stars" and "Just Can't Find Loving" were three songs which I wrote. I went to Phil Pratt on Orange Street, but I tired out all the while. When I come to him for the recording, it's like he wasn't prepared – "Come back next week".
