In 1967 Coxsone selected the group, then still known as the Tempests, to record 'Pick Up The Pieces', an electrifying number that highlighted their harmonic excellence. But somehow Coxsone held back the disc, so Cousins recut it himself in 1971 with fellow postal worker and future Jays member Lloyd Forrest temporarily taking Errol Wilson's place. The success of the new number, issued on Cousins' Uhuru label, prompted Coxsone to release the original and led Cousins to have premier deejay I Roy voice 'Monkey Fashion' on the new rhythm at Tubby's studio. 'It's in King Tubby's we voice it, 'cause I used to live down the road from Tubbys,' Cousins explains, adding that songs like the Royals' 'Ghetto Man' and 'Sufferer In The Ghetto' were also voiced and mixed there.

'We start voice tune up a Tubbys,' continues Bunny Lee. 'The first set of tune, we go for Scratch again. Scratch do "Bathroom Skank" and all them tunes. We do the rhythm at Dynamic and voice it at Tubbys.' The sound-effects clutter of 'Bathroom Skank', a rude duet that Perry voiced with his common-law wife Pauline Morisson, was one of a series of heavily modified discs that Perry cut at Tubby's studio in 1972-3. Other notable examples include 'Black IP A', on which the horn parts were subjected to extreme phasing, and 'Flashing Echo', a drum-and-bass cut of Leo Graham's 'News Flash' given heavy doses of delay. Other producers drew equally exciting results: Derrick Harriott used Tubby's studio to transform the Ethiopians' 'No Baptism' into the spooky sounds of 'Dracula'; Keith Hudson, Glen Brown and upcoming producer Carlton Patterson all mixed the bulk of their dubs there.

**Picking up the pieces:**

**Roy Cousins [Dave Hendley]**

Tubby's studio was also a magnet for those from the very poorest levels of society who were seeking to break into the music business. A notable example was Vivian Jackson, a malnourished youth who was then scraping a living by giving betting tips at the racetrack. Jackson is obviously a deeply religious man, his conversation littered with references to the Bible; though his dreadlocks are long and matted, his rejection of Selassie as divine and his veneration of Jesus make him doubly ostracized, resulting in his title of Jesus Dread. Jackson says he was so poor in 1971, when he founded the Prophets group and record label, that he had to convince musicians to lay a few rhythm tracks for free; thus, 'The original Prophets was Chinna, Family Man, Horsemouth and myself Horsemouth says the studio fees were partly funded by friends whom Jackson sometimes lived and worked with. 'There was a big Rastaman on the gully bank in Waterhouse: Solomon Wolfe, he was the first man that lead the Rasta delegation with Mortimer Planno. These Rastas, Alric and Cleo, they have a furnace, gather aluminum from all over the streets, melt it and make pots. On the weekend they go to Ocho Rios, Montego Bay, and sell pots to make the sessions. [Karl] Pitterson was the engineer, a very roots guy, even though he was uptown. He knows we are from the ghetto, respects me and Family Man as good musicians, and just give us extra time.'

The following year, King Tubby gave Jackson free studio time when he was ready to voice the material with his harmony group. 'It was in 1972,' Jackson confirms. 'Alric Forbes and a brother who come sing as Bobby Melody carry harmony with me.' Tubby gave Jackson his peculiar stage name of Yabby You, stemming from the onomatopoeic chorus of his debut 'Conquering Lion'. 'When me start doing [music], I say me a go do it privately, and if it flop, me just take me losses and nobody won't know. I never know it would sell. I said I would just go around all of the Rastaman and beg them to buy one. When it did start to sell, here comes one of Tubbs' number one dubs. Me tell them it's Tubbys me sell the record for, so them always wonder who this group is. Then me do a song name "Love Of Jah", and it came up the same way. When me do "Warn The Nation", Tubbs tell them a me – him used to tell them the group is an American group named Yabby You.'

By 1974 Tubby's mixing artistry was officially acknowledged on version B-sides, notably on material cut for Carlton Patterson's Black and White label by artists such as Larry Marshall and Lizzy. Upcoming producer 'Prince' Tony Robinson also name-checked the sound man and mixer on an early disc called 'Tubby's In Full Swing', credited to Lloyd and Kerry (toasters Lloyd Young and Carey 'Wild-man' Johnson). The song was a bizarre cut-up blending the opening bars of the Staple Singers' 'I'l l Take You There' with a trombone instrumental.

Another important element in the development of dub is the partnership formed by Clive Chin and Errol Thompson at Randy's . 'Them time there, Tubby try and experiment more fi dub, but me and Errol start dub music,' says Clive. 'When I say start it, I'm not saying we going to take credit for any other man that put out a dub album, but we really experiment, because we had the time and the facility to do it. Another man like Phil Pratt or Niney, time was so important that you have to run in and run out – that's how studio used to run.'

**Jesus Dread: religious visionary Yabby You [Courtesy of Yabby You]**

As the son of the burgeoning studio's proprietor Clive Chin naturally spent a great deal of time in the premises at 17 North Parade, becoming more actively involved in record production at Randy's by the end of the 1960s. 'My first personal self-production was a tune called "Young Love", but I always had disappointments with vocal tunes in the early beginnings, because the artists never seem like they could sing. "Young Love" was cut with a schoolmate of mine, Douglas Boothe – I think he just took that name because him always trying to come off Ken's styling. Him just couldn't cut it, so we did it as an instrumental with Soul Syndicate – that was around '69.1 was basically brought in as an auditioner, because my uncle Keith was more in charge of production, but me used to do some wickeder tune than him, and them time him used to take credit fiit and me never used to like that. A whole heap of tune, my uncle Victor press them up and put "produced by Keith Chin", and it's really me do it!'

Among the tunes for which Keith Chin initially took credit was the wonderful Augustus Pablo instrumental 'Java', produced by Clive with Errol Thompson at the mixing desk in 1971. 'My uncle take credit for "Java" up here, and put the Chosen Few as the artist – it was called "East Of The Rio Cobre" by the Chosen Few on the New York Impact label. That really did get Pablo upset: Pablo didn't even get credit on that, and Chosen Few only said the intro and did the likkle chorus, but at the time Chosen Few had a name and nobody don't know Pablo.'

**Spicing the music: Clive Chin [David Katz]**

Clive reveals that 'Java' was originally intended as a vocal tune, but when the vocalist could not supply what Clive had in mind he got Pablo to reach for his melodica, providing the talented musician with his first big hit as a solo artist. It even received the accolade of Best Instrumental in 1972. 'The rhythm was a vocal tune that was not successful. The artist was another schoolmate of mine called Dennis Wright. It was some little love song, but him just couldn't cut it. Pablo was around, so I get Errol to cut a dub plate and he carry it home and work on it, come up with two melody lines – one for the version and one for the A-side.' Clive states that he decided to add the Chosen Few to counteract the novelty of Pablo's melodica playing. 'It was a new instrument on the scene, so I have to build around it and make it attractive, make people interested in it. I get the idea to throw in the Chosen Few because I had a tune with the Impressions called "East Of Java", and I wanted that song to be on a reggae rhythm. So I just have them come in saying, "From the East of the Rio Cobre to the West of the Rio Cobre hails the hottest Java". The Rio Cobre is a river in Jamaica where there are bamboo rafters. Again I just throw that in, just a spice, like when you'r e cooking food and you want to flavour it up a little bit.'

The song's tremendous success naturally spawned further versions, including 'Jaro', a Tommy McCook saxophone version, Dennis Alcapone's toasting cut 'Mava' and I Roy's surreal 'Hospital Trolley'. It also saw Pablo, Clive and Errol Thompson spending many a Sunday at Studio 17 with Family Man and Tin Legs, creating what would be issued in 1973 as _This Is Augustus Pablo,_ a stunning debut album for the young melodica master. 'The album was really something that me and Pablo sit down and talk about, 'cause doing work as a musician and doing work as an artist is two different things. Weeks we'v e been there mixing. There wasn't even a Sunday that I had to go out and mingle with my friends or check my girlfriend. We decided to dedicate to this mix, because I wanted it to sound so good.'

Two of the album's tracks, 'Lover's Mood' and the popular 'Pablo In Dub', were actually produced by Leonard 'Santic' Chin, a former singer with the Graduates. It was the sound of 'Java' that convinced Santic to seek out Pablo, and the fresh producer would often use his keyboard and arranging skills on future works, usually cut with Errol Thompson behind the mixing desk at Randy's .

Thompson was an old school friend of Clive Chin, and he became the chief engineer at 17 North Parade after completing a brief apprenticeship at Studio One. 'We had Bill Garnett, and then we had this other engineer from one of the radio stations – I think his name was Neville. A man named Mr Galbraith used to come to our studio to fix the organ – he w7as very good at the amplification and electronic part. He brought Errol down as an apprentice, because my father told him he was seeking an engineer. Errol came in when he had just come out of Studio One, but I knew him before that from Choir School out by South Camp Road and North Street. I had a good relationship with Errol, a very innovative relationship where we wouldn't just idle talk about things on the street or girlfriend business. We would talk more like how we could further the music, how we can do a different kind of fixture to it, spice it, rather than have the same old pattern of just Tommy [McCook] blowing a horn, Bobby [Aitken] playing a guitar, Winston [Wright] playing an organ.'

_Java Java Java Java_ was one of the first dub albums, issued in a limited pressing of one thousand copies in 1973. Thompson keeps the bass at the top of the mix for the whole of the disc, shuffling in reverb-treated keyboards, guitar, horns and the odd snatch of vocals or melodica. The final track, a version of Lloyd Parks' 'Ordinary Man' retitled 'Forward The Bass', incorporates a hilarious argument between studio personnel and an aspiring session musician in which the song's individual musical elements are highlighted. The album's concentration on drum and bass was a radical departure, and underlines not only the strength of the Randy's session musicians but, more importantly, the creative interaction between Clive Chin and Errol Thompson at the mixing desk. 'It was works done between me and Errol,' Clive emphasizes, 'and it was something new at the time. There wasn't an album out there that wasn't a mento, a calypso, or one of them uptown reggae albums by man like Byron Lee or Tomorrow's Children, so we wanted to do something different and say, "Boy, we a go give them a wicked dub album." We didn't commercialize it, we do it more like a dub plate special, so the market at that time wasn't great enough to keep the dub flow. People was just starting to listen to this new music, so we didn't consider it was going to be a big hit.'

A few other dub albums appeared around the same time, all vying for the title of'f irst dub LP'. Lee Perry's ground-breaking _Upsetters 14 Dub Blackboard Jungle_ (aka _Blackboard Jungle Dub)_ was mixed in true stereo with channel separation; Scratch often kept the drums and percussion upfront, and made greater use of reverb, echo and tape effects at King Tubby's studio. Prince Buster's epoch-making _The Message,_ mixed by Carlton Lee at Dynamics, kept horns and organ – processed through reverb – rolling over driving drum and bass; Joe Gibbs' _Dub Serial_ highlighted the strength of its minimal rhythms, as did Herman Chin-Loy's _Aquarius Dub,_ a set that mixed cuts of Chin-Loy's strongest productions (including Pablo's 'East Of The River Nile' and 'Cassava Piece') with a number of raw rhythms rapidly laid by the Now Generation. 'That was the first dub album, as far as I know,' insists Herman. 'Anybody that has a set always wanted a dub plate, and the dub plates those days were more or less just rhythm. Then the deejay would play the rhythm and talk on it, so I get Val Douglas, Michael Richards and Mikey Chung, about three or four musicians there, and about half an hour it take to do that in Dynamics' second studio. "Drum Song" and a couple others, "Home" by Ken Boothe, they were done there immediately. Whatever [rhythms] I had, like the Alton Ellis thing ["Alton's Official Daughter"], I just put on that too. Carlton Lee mixed the album – that's half an hour too.'

Another innovation from that period was displayed in Rupie Edwards' _Yamaha Skank,_ the first album entirely composed of different cuts of the same rhythm. In 1973 Rupie used a cut of the Uniques' 'My Conversation' to score two big chart successes with Shorty the President, a deejay who was born Derrick Thompson in the rural town of Trelawney in 1949. Shorty now lives in New Jersey, where he works as a telecommunications engineer; passing through London on business, now shorn of his locks, he explains how he came to record for Edwards.

'In the country where I come from is like plenty of farming: yam, bananas, Irish potatoes, cow, goat, so we always have something to eat. I leave the country and go to Kingston when I was nineteen. I was doing mechanic trade, but I didn't like too much of the mechanic work, because it's so dirty. I moved to New Haven when Duhaney Park was just built up. I was there with my sister, but I didn't too like the vibes of my sister, because she was living with a policeman – throw down his gun everywhere. I find a friend name Bongo George, he did have a small sound named Conquering Lion, so I always go down there and stay with him and make a little voice business live on the set. Rupie living over Duhaney Park, near the border, so he always come around. He is a rebel, he did have a girlfriend that live right beside us, so he hear me deejaying on the little set. One day we was under a tree, chanting, knocking some little pans, smoking a draw. They call me Stumpy them times, I didn't name Shorty [yet]. He just come up and say, "Stumpy, I'm going to record you today." '

This debut recording related the circumstances of a recent sound clash. 'How I get the lyrics "President A Mash Up The Resident", about three days before, we went to a dance with the sound named President, and that dance was crazy. The President a mash up the other sound, Stereo from Spanish Town – the front of the amplifier was like a jukebox, so they called it Stereo – and every man was talking about it. Rupie did carry U Roy Junior to studio to voice that track, but he couldn't manage it, so he have on the track "Here comes the pick of the past and this one's going to be the cream of the heart". After I hear that, I just come in and say, "Lit oh, you know, the President a mash up the resident, you rock so you come so you move so you go so... ." The lyrics was off of sound. You did have Bmgy Bunny and Bongo Herman in the studio. Rupie said, "What you could do, whistle something in there", so after I do it everybody go in for the second cut. After "President" it was "Yamaha Skank". Those two tune, it's the same rhythm, and I make it a number one, two of them in the one year. Me is the first deejay to do that.'

The popularity of Shorty's song of praise to the Japanese motorcycle made Rupie assemble the _Yamaha Skank_ album the following year, treating listeners to an endless succession of deejay, instrumental and dub cuts. The disc highlighted the infinite versatility of a good rhythm, and showed how any song has the potential to be endlessly transformed. 'He start to make a lot of version with the harmonica sound blowing, dubbing on,' Shorty recalls. 'Everybody always like to laugh after him, "You version too much, you do too much version!" But some of those version sell just like the original tune, because those times every sound man need version.'

Edwards himself says that it was easy then to get a cut of a rhythm produced by someone else, and notes that a popular rhythm could have a prolonged shelf-life when it was reshaped by various hands. 'Jimmy Riley sold me the "My Conversation" backing track, and then I did "Yamaha Skank" on that backing track. Then Bunny Lee come in and say he is the owner of the rhythm, and then I understand that Phil Pratt produced it, then I understand that Lloyd Charmers produced it... Bunny Lee sold me "Bangarang", then he sold me "Everybody Needs Love" and "Out Of Love" and some others, then I just do the versions. But that didn't stop Bunny Lee from using them afterwards.'

The appearance of dub and version albums greatly elevated the status of these Jamaican innovations, and they have both remained a constant feature of the island's popular music. As with the deejay innovations that were so closely allied to dub, in time the techniques of dub mixers were eventually adapted by producers in other countries, greatly influencing various dance music genres including house, garage, trip hop, trance and ambient. Dub's fruition, though almost incidental at the time, would prove to be one of the most significant of reggae's early 1970s' innovations, and the version phenomenon would help usher the music into other territories as the decade progressed.

**'Best Dressed Chicken In Town':**

**Dr Alimantado at Prince Buster's shop [Dave Hendley]**
**CHAPTER SEVEN**

Deejay's Choice: The Further Ascent of the Toaster

_You have other people that run the sound that don't want to give you the privilege 'cause them see that you have the power, but we bad boy of a the road so you have to demand._

Big Youth

_Working with Tubbys was a pleasure._

Prince Jazzbo.

AS JAMAICAN POP continued to wind its peculiar course, the status of the deejay was elevated further by a series of hit singles and album releases in 1973–4. Leading the way was Dennis Alcapone, who had recorded so much material for so many different producers by the end of 1972 that a glut of Alcapone albums hit the market at the same time.

'While I was at Duke Reid, me and Bunny Lee was friends, because Bunny Lee is not that serious a producer like Duke Reid and Coxsone, who is just business. Bunny would give you all two gal too – he is a man about town, if you understand,' the toaster reminisces. 'Me and Bunny get together and did the "Ripe Cherry" song off Eric Donaldson's "Cherry Oh Baby", "Alcapone Guns Don't Argue" and "It Must Come", a version of "Better Must Come". Bunny said Byron Lee was interested in an album, because Dynamic Sounds put out the "Ripe Cherry" and "Guns Don't Argue" singles. Word got around that I was working on that album, so Duke Reid wanted me to do an album, then Coxsone hear [about] it and wanted his own as well, and everybody was in a competition to get the first album out. I think Coxsone was the first of the whole of them with the _Forever Version_ album, then Dynamics with _Guns Don't Argue,_ then Duke put out _Soul To Soul, Deejay's Choice -_ in fact, when I came up to England on my first tour in 1973 Duke Reid actually gave me the tape to carry to Trojan records.'

'Deejay's Choice' was the first of many numbers to celebrate the unexpected rise to prominence of the deejay, and on this song Alcapone duly saluted contemporaries such as Big Youth, I Roy, Lizzy and U Roy by relating their distinguishing vocal traits. When asked about the song's inspiration, he was quick to admit that its theme originated from a rival deejay. 'When our sound system wasn't playing we used to go to Spanish Town to listen to Ruddy's , because Ruddy could afford whatever dub plates he wants. When Ruddy comes to Studio One or Treasure Isle, he would just tell them, "Put on plate what you made today." Ruddy's used to have a deejay named Mango, and Mango used to have that "Deejay's Choice" kind of thing going on the "Sidewalk Doctor" rhythm, so that's where I get the idea from.'

_Soul To Soul, Deejay's Choice_ has plenty of rapping momentum, with Alcapone and his spar Lizzy alternating places on the mike. In contrast, _King Of The Track,_ a further album that Dennis cut in this period for Bunny Lee, was voiced too hastily to be of much significance. 'When I was leaving Jamaica in 1973 Bunny Lee said, "Dennis, as you'r e going to England, I think you should walk with some material so that you can make some money. We'l l go by Tubby's tomorrow and make an album." He just run the track down and keep on running. When I said to him, "Striker, that's just a run down [practice run]", he said, "That tune there done, man. Next tune." '

**Drifter at the microphone:**

**I Roy the great [Beth Lesser]**

By the time of Alcapone's arrival in Britain, I Roy was firmly in ascendancy back home. Born Roy Samuel Reid in Saint Thomas in 1944,1 Roy operated a set called Soul Bunnies in the rock steady era, playing Friday nights at Rose Lane in downtown Kingston. A civil servant working at Customs and Excise, Reid had more ready cash than some of his peers and made a point of always being immaculately dressed. His microphone talents enabled him to move in 1968 to Son's Junior, a sound system with an extensive selection of Studio One dub plates based at the New Yorker club in Spanish Town. At the end of the sixties, when Son's proprietor moved abroad, I Roy became the star deejay of Ruddy Redwood's Supreme Ruler of Sound. When U Roy hit with 'Wake The Town', I Roy moved to the rival Spanish Town set called Stereo. His verbal skills drew the attention of producer Harry Mudie, who cut the toaster's debut disc, 'Hearts Don't Leap', towards the end of 1970. I Roy voiced further songs for Mudie, including a version of Dennis Walks' chilling 'Drifter', and helped distribute Mudie's product on a Honda motorcycle. As he started to branch out and record for other producers, the quick-witted toaster began chatting on the Clarendon sound V-Rocket, and ended up temporarily filling U Roy's shoes on King Tubby's Hi Fi in 1972. It was in the same year that I Roy voiced the arresting 'Sidewalk Killer' for Ruddy, making optimum use of Tommy McCook's sax-and-flute reworking of Phyllis Dillon's haunting cover of Marlena Shaw's soul hit 'Woman Of The Ghetto'; the disc was so popular that Roy voiced an alternative version, the atmospheric 'Dr Phibbs.'

**Puppy on the scene: Gussie Clarke [Courtesy of Gussie Clarke/Motion]**

I Roy's debut album, _Presenting I Roy,_ captured the deejay at his height of competence. The album was compiled by Gussie Clarke, a record importer and aspiring producer who was then so young that he was known on the scene as Puppy. Augustus Clarke is now a very busy man who presides over Anchor, the biggest recording studio on the island, complete with CD duplication and numerous other facilities. As with many of his peers, he came to record production through involvement with sound systems. 'I grew up downtown, Beeston Street and Church Street, with an adoptive mother, she usually work with the government – just an ordinary poor lady, nothing unique about her except her heart. Music was one of those things that was within you, and I just love music. In those days an amplifier was made by buying parts, so I usually buy parts for the amplifiers – save it from lunch money. I was kind of a born entrepreneur: I used to have a bicycle and sell rides to the school gates to get parts, and I usually ask for foreign records from the States and sell them to all the sound systems. I graduated from Kingston College in '71, so I was actually involved in having the sound system King Gussie's Hi Fi prior to '71. After that I had this dub cutting machine – I think we bought it from Treasure Isle. Get it working downtown at a home studio in a wooden building upstairs at 79½ Church Street – that's where we started the first productions, cutting dubs for all these sound systems. I swapped an old amplifier to Errol Dunkley for the use of a rhythm and we used the rhythm to voice U Roy's "The Higher The Mountain", that was the first thing.'

It was the close links he formed supplying exclusives to sound system operators and cutting dub plates for upcoming producers that enabled Gussie to hand-pick the choice rhythms used for I Roy's debut LP. Touching on a range of subjects, from the Rastafari-praising 'Red Gold And Green' to the social realism of 'Pusher Man' and 'Peace', Roy kept the pace consistently relaxed. Even when proffering a lyrical deluge as in his forceful castigation of freeloaders on 'Black Man Time', a witty reworking of Lloyd Parks' 'Slaving', the toaster made it seem effortless. It was this same laid-back confidence that characterized the follow-up _Hell And Sorrow set,_ which compiled work cut for various smaller producers. Thematically, Roy demonstrated on songs like 'Black And Proud' the influence of American black power movements, and on 'Buck And The Preacher' an enduring love of popular films. A distinguishing musical feature of the album is its generally increased use of echo and delay, much of which came courtesy of King Tubby.

The popularity of these albums in Britain encouraged I Roy to follow Dennis Alcapone to London in late 1973, where he remained for the better part of a year. He made several live appearances backed by rising local band Matumbi, and became a star feature of Lloydie Coxsone's sound system residency at the Roaring Twenties club in the West End.

I Roy's composure was matched by that of a tall, lanky dread with a deeper voice; known as Big Youth, he became something of a sensation through recordings cut in 1972–3. Manley Augustus Buchanan was born in the Southeast Kingston ghetto of Rae Town in 1949, and regards his religious upbringing as having been particularly formative. 'My mother is a Revivalist preacher and my mother was my father also, because she have to take care of everything, including my three sisters and one brother. I was born at la Water Street in Rae Town, and I move to 132 Matches Lane in the heart of the city, because my mother always have to be moving to get somewhere she can keep her church and preach. That wasn't the road I take, but that teach me Rastafari, because I was so biblical that the love was already in me. But I wasn't ready for Christianity and the condition of Christ.'

Before launching himself on a musical career, Big Youth repaired and maintained machines around Kingston. 'I was a mechanic at Harvey's Cab Company in Rae Town, that's where I learn trade. Then when the Kinston Sheraton was building, I used to do diesel mechanic work on the site. That was the last time I work.'

Though Big Youth had deejay aspirations from the time of U Roy's success, he found sound system operatives reluctant to allow him a chance to prove himself, and had to use intimidating tactics to find his way to the mike around 1971. 'You have other people that run the sound that don't want to give you the privilege 'cause them see that you have the power,' he explains backstage at London's millennial Peace and Love festival, 'but we a bad boy offa the road, so you have to demand. One night you have a sound named [Down South] the President [featuring I Roy]. Errol Scorcher and my brethren keep the dance and them say it's me going to have to go mash it. I go to the dance and just use the one song "Satta Massa Ganna": "A wiggle wiggle wiggle wonna, you no on-ya but you born-ya", and mash up the whole place the night. We nice it up, and it was a new star born.'

Big Youth came to the limelight through working on Lord Tippertone, a downtown set initially called Finger Tone that was based at Princess Street; his popularity enabled him to supersede fellow neighbourhood tough Jah Stitch as regular boss of the microphone. Big Youth says he consciously strove to bring the uplifting spirituality of Rastafari into his microphone chants, a direct reaction to the apolitical lyrics set as standard by U Roy at Treasure Isle. 'It just happened that I start to run the Tipper thing a spiritual way, and tell people must make love and not war, because war ugly and love lovely, 'cause me have a whole heap of brethren that me see just dead. Babylon or them friend or a next gang kill them through differences where they can't agree, so all those things teach me knowledge, and Jah himself. We used to hear about "Hit me back" and "Chick a bow, chick a bow, chick a bow wow wow", but I wasn't into the "Hit me back" and "bow" thing. I see where we need to say, "Live it up Jah", "Hit me front and never hit me back", 'cause we'r e moving forward. We'r e not into no bend down – we'r e getting up.'

Big Youth says it was West Kingston producer Ivan Radway – known as 'One Foot Jimmy' because he had lost a leg in his youth in the infamous Kendal train crash – who first approached him to record in early 1972 after he had a success with Errol Dunkley's 'Black Cinderella'. But Gregory Isaacs beat Radway to the draw and captured the toaster's talent first, placing Big Youth over Dunkley's cut of the oft-versioned 'Movie Star'. 'Jimmy Radway wanted me to come and toast on "Black Cinderella", but he was joking, and Gregory Isaacs already hear me working the sound, so Gregory just jump the gun, and that's where I do "Movie Man": "If you'r e coming from far in a bus or a car, man I'm begging you make love and not war, I'm so sad to say that when you deal with war you have to stay far. Makes things right to do things right in the middle of the night, and promise me you would do that." The same day I did the "Black Cinderella", and it goes on for a couple of weeks.'

Gregory says he came to produce Big Youth's first recording because _of_ 'the vibes of how I circulate in the ghetto. I see Big Youth as a youth with a lot of talent. Him come and blast off differently, seen? Everyone start take on to him – he's a very great deejay. We were friends, because him used to cook food for me.'

Errol Dunkley says the cut of 'Movie Star' that he recorded with Gregory Isaacs marked the formation of the African Museum label. 'I was at Coxsone – me, Dennis Brown, Horace Andy – until Wailing Souls started recording for Coxsone. I feel down all the time, because nothing ain't happening. Me and Dennis, sometimes we deh-deh and have no money, eat no food. I decide to move on after Coxsone and I fall out over something – I left Studio One with tears in my eyes, even asked Dennis if he's coming with me. Gregoiy Isaacs was singing for Rupie Edwards. Nothing wasn't really happening for him – he never really had a hit song. Gregory took me to Rupie Edwards and I did a medley of all my hit songs, because at the time it was a medley thing happening, but it was only a few that really hit, and mine was a hit. Then Gregory and I start working together. We form this African Museum label and we did three songs: wTe did over [Freddy McKay's ] "Picture On The Wall" rhythm and Winston Wright do a little keyboard. We did that instrumental song, then I did "Movie Star", then Gregory did "Look Before You Leap". We wrere working like half and half: we rent the studio and hire the musicians, and any amount of money that will come we split it down the middle. We did another session, and I did "Darling Ooh" and Gregory did "My Only Lover". After I did "Movie Star" I did "Black Cinderella" for Jimmy Radway, a good writer. He born and grow in the west of the city, same like us. He was a businessman, but he write songs like "Mother Liza" that Leroy Smart sing. I was supposed to sing that song, and another song called "Hell And Sorrow", [recorded by] Hortense Ellis.'

As Dunkley had licensed 'Darling Ooh' to Rupie Edwards, Edwards reaped the lion's share of proceeds when the song hit. 'Me sell it to Rupie Edwards and regretted it, because the song was number one for fourteen weeks in Jamaica, and it sell a lot on export. But I used to sell Sonia Pottinger "Movie Star" – that's how she get to know me. She said, "Let's do a thing" – that's how I did my first album.'

The LP, _Presenting Errol Dunkley,_ had the first cut of 'A Little Way Different', one of Dunkley's most popular tunes; Errol says the song was inspired by his rivalry with Dennis Brown. 'Delroy Wilson was the first kid in Jamaica doing recording, then I was the second kid. I was like Delroy's rival, then Dennis come, and Dennis was like my rival. They used to compare us together. Sometimes they say me and Dennis sound alike, and this is how this song come up. If you sing the song I sing, you can still hear the difference.'

Such work, and its proceeds, signalled the end of Dunkley's involvement in African Museum. 'Gregory and I split at the time, because we had an office in Orange Street, but them time Gregory were really short-tempered. Him and the landlord couldn't get on, so we had to pack the office up, and I get so downhearted with him I form my own label called Silver Ring. I had a store at the time in Honour Town and I had a couple of jukeboxes, 'cause [what] I wanted were to _go_ into the jukebox business. All my equipment was from money made from that album.'

Gregory continued his self-productions on African Museum, but the song that brought him widespread fame in Jamaica was 'All I Have Is Love', one of the first hits recorded at Channel One, a studio opened by local entrepreneur Joseph Hoo-Kim in the heart of the Maxfield Park ghetto. 'Joe Hoo-Kim call me one day and said, "The studio just done. Come and test out the studio – you are free,"' producer Phil Pratt recalls. 'So I brought my musicians with me and Gregory Isaacs. He arranged the horn section and the bass line and wrote the song as well. "All I Have Is Love", that was Gregory Isaacs' first number one.'

'We gave everybody a free try,' Joseph Hoo-Kim confirms at the pressing plant he operates today in Brooklyn, 'because I didn't understand anything about running a session and I want to get experience. So any producer that was in the business, it was a day we usually give them, just to let them experience it and maybe get their business.'

Joseph was the oldest of four brothers, all of whom would eventually be involved in running the studio. 'I was born in 1942,' he relates. 'Paul comes after me, then Kenneth and Ernie.' The Hoo-Kims are typically described as Chinese, but like most Jamaicans turn out to have a mixed heritage. 'My father is from China. He was going to Panama in '26, and he stopped off in Jamaica and never go to Panama. My mother, born in Jamaica, was half-Jew and half- Chinese – her [sur] name was Solomon. They were running a ice cream parlour and a rum bar at the corner of Maxfield Avenue and Spanish Town Road, and then we moved to 29 Maxfield Avenue in '66. It was a ice cream parlour and a wholesale liquor store, and the property belongs to us. My brother- in-law usually work with a company that operate iukebox, which was Issa's, and sometimes when he's going to the country he usually pick me up. Then Issa begin to sell out the boxes and I acquired some, and that is where I started from. I think the jukebox business started about I960.'

**Ernest Hoo-Kim [Beth Lesser]**

It was a fateful visit to Dynamic Sound that inspired Joseph to build Channel One. 'John Holt and I grew up together – we know each other from we was small. He takes me to Dynamic one day and I was fascinated with the sound in the studio – the sound just hold me right there, and I said, "This is what I want to be in." Bill Garnett is the one that select the equipment. I paid 38,000 dollars for a API console, one of the most expensive on the market, maybe 60,000 US dollars for the console and the two tape machines. The studio opened 1972, with just four tracks. Bunny Lee run the first session: it was "Can I Change My Mind" with Delroy Wilson, the musicians were Soul Syndicate – that was a trial session really. I started the studio myself, but Ernest came into it after we had a engineer problem – he usually fix jukeboxes and operate one-armed bandits. The original engineer was Syd Bucknor, he was there for about a year. During the period when we start up, Bunny Lee said, "The studio doesn't sound good", and I have in the most expensive console. I did an LP with Alton Ellis that I couldn't release. The bass was tied with the drum – it wasn't record[ed] properly. Then we make session[s] and they were all flops, and I decided to lay back and see what we are doing wrong. But the main thing was about the sound. After Syd Bucknor, we decided to go into the studio ourselves. The first record that we put out was "Don't Give Up The Fight", Gladdy and Stranger Cole. Then we came out with hits: [Delroy Wilson's ] "It's A Shame", "Call On Me", [Horace Andy's ] "Girl I Love You".'

**Syd Bucknor [right] with Lee Perry [David Katz]**

While Channel One was in the process of establishing itself, Big Youth was gaining popularity on vinyl. His second recording, 'Tell It Black', voiced on the emotive rendition of Peter Green's 'Black Magic Woman' recorded by Dennis Brown and Soul Syndicate for Phil Pratt, was the first of many Big Youth discs to make an impact. ' "Tell It Black" and "Phil Pratt Thing", those start to create a little heat,' the toaster recalls. 'Then the other producers want me – they all start coming. People have to wake me up out of my bed and we took a bike in the studio. We did "S 90 Skank" the same day we did "Chi Chi Run". The two of them went up to number one. I did things for Glen Brown too that - same day some great things happen on that Friday.'

It was 'S 90 Skank' that really drew the best of Big Youth's abilities on record, again aided by perceptive production from Keith Hudson. Youth says it was personal experience he spoke of on the record, warning about the consequences _of_ excessive speed. 'Me was riding a bike and crash at 12.20 in the afternoon, wake up at about 7.30 in the night.' He pauses momentarily to point out some cranial scarring. 'While they pronounce me dead, I was there visioning how I'm singing and Jah tell me me must go round the world and sing "Jah". So I say, "If you ride like lightning, you will crash like thunder" – I get some bad crash. I used to ride wild, you know.'

The success of that disc in particular, and of the Prince Buster-produced 'Chi Chi Run', made Big Youth in high demand on the Kingston scene. 'Everybody hearing this "Jah Jah Jah, Jah wah wah rock", so everybody want a piece, 'cause it's the science [magic or sorcery] that – is that open the key to reggae music. Then Joe Gibbs want me, Miss Pottinger and Derrick Harriott, and I had seven tunes in the chart at the same time, with five in the two top tens.' For Gibbs, Youth scored high with the boxing commentary 'Foreman Versus Frazier'; 'Cool Breeze' was a popular cut of Keith and Tex's 'Stop That Train', cut for Derrick Harriott; even the Wailers got in on the act, asking Youth to voice a deejay cut of their self-produced 'Craven Choke Puppy'. 'Them time Bob Marley them a do bad,' the toaster recalls. 'Them never have nothing! Me do "Craven A Go Choke Puppy" so Rita [Marley] can go buy some food, but them no treat me good.'

After such a steady stream of successful singles, a strong album consolidated his popularity both at home and abroad. As with I Roy's debut set, Big Youth's _Screaming Target_ was put out by Gussie Clarke in 1973. Youth makes full use of the disc's impeccably chosen rhythms, perpetuating the dejected message of Leroy Smart's 'Shame And Ambition' on 'Pride And Joy Rock', continuing the mournful determination of Gregory Isaacs' 'One One Cocoa' on 'One Of These Fine Days', and subverting KC White's recut of the oft-versioned 'No No No' for both the ghetto description of 'Concrete Jungle' and the more famous gangster-movie commentary 'Screaming Target'. 'This guy was firing his gun wilder than Clint Eastwood, so I said this one was ranker than Dirty Harry,' explains Youth of the cryptic introduction to the title track.

It was inevitable that such an individually minded artist would go into self-production to acquire greater financial reward and artistic control, so Big Youth launched the Negusa Negast and Augustus Buchanan labels. He later supplemented by imprints such as Tanasha and Nichola Delita, the latter named after one of his daughters. In 1974 he issued the double album _Reggae Phenomenon_ on, a portion _of_ which the toaster controversially branched out into the singing arena.

'That is when me get good,' he recounts, 'get conscious and start defend the African daughter them. Me take War's "The World Is A Ghetto" and turn it round and call it "Streets In Africa" with D. Brown – it give me much strength. Me do "Love Me Forever", "Hot Stock", "Downtown Kingston Pollution", "Hell Is For Heroes", "African Daughter" and [the Temptations'] "Papa Was A Rolling Stone", so that is when I start get versatile, but some of the fool them say me can't sing. That was the better part where I was achieving and seeing my career looking progressive.' And why, exactly, was Big Youth inspired to sing? 'Because in a lot of audiences, it's not everyone could understand the "dee dee dah dah dee dee dey", so you have to show them that Big Youth have something inna him – me can sing, so me a go touch some big song all of them know. When me come with [Dionne Warwick's ] "Touch Me In The Morning", God cry. Seeing the women them love me more, me have to start hide from girl. Me show them me versatility because me creative, me have a force to generate.'

Though Big Youth's singing technique seems largely inspired by the jazz tradition, he reveals that he takes his greatest inspiration from female soul vocalists. 'Me practise off of the woman them, like Diana Ross, and Dionne Warwick was my favourite, from early until now. Lady D now have the edge, because she take some wider notes.'

Another deejay to rise from live appearances on Tippertone was Doctor Alimantado, initially known as Winston Cool or Prince Winston. Born Winston Thompson in the early 1950s, Tado is a good-natured Rasta with a sharp sense of humour. Widely travelled, he is currently based in West Africa.

Tado says he ran away from his downtown Kingston home at the age of eight to live with rough people in the slums. His early experiences of hardship caused him to gravita towards Rastafari and brought about some conflict with his parents. His main deejay inspiration was Sir George the Atomic's Prince Rough, a toaster who always drew large crowds before he died in the early seventies; U Roy was another role model. One of Thompson's earliest recorded efforts was 'No Go On So' for small producer H. 'Bussa' Marston, but it was work for Lee Perry in 1971–2 that brought Winston wider recognition in Jamaica.

'I was on Tippertone, the sound that made Big Youth famous,' he boasts, 'and the Upsetter came to one of the dance and said I should come and do some songs for him. Jah Stitch used to be around my sound, and Jah Stitch used to play Upsetter's sound, so that's how it really came about. I did songs like "Macabee The Third", "Piece Of My Heart", "Tip Of My Tongue".'

Though his smooth embellishments of 'Piece Of My Heart' gave hints of the style he was to develop a few years later, it was in combination with Junior Byles that Winston's artistry first shone. Not only did he mutter ominous Rasta proclamations on 'Ital Version', the flip side of Junior's anthem-like 'Beat Down Babylon', but the thoughtful meditation on his African homeland delivered on a cut of Byles' heartfelt 'Place Called Africa' fully captured the reverence that alienated Rastafari accorded their ancestral continent. The toaster has also spoken of trying to voice a tune as a singer for Scratch, but a laughing Upsetter told Winston he should stick to toasting and as a result it would be several years before he dared to voice a singing disc.

In 1973 Winston founded the Vital Food label, renaming himself Doctor Alimantado 'The Ital Surgeon', with the self-produced 'Just The Other Day', a woeful comment on the crippling inflation that was then gripping Jamaica, voiced at King Tubby's studio over a cut of the Flames' great 'Zion' rhythm obtained from Lee Perry. From his base at 40 Beeston Street, Alimantado issued further stunners, sometimes on the Ital Sounds label, including the half-sung, optimistic plea for unity called 'Ride On'. Setting up his own label was partly a reaction to the negative attitude of larger entrepreneurs; though the bulk of his subsequent work was self-produced, he occasionally voiced material for smaller producers.

**Dr Alimantado: The Ital Surgeon [David Katz]**

It was the constantly echoing voice bouncing through 'Best Dressed Chicken In Town' that caused a storm on its release in 1974, sparking interest in the toaster in the UK; the song's cryptic lyrics referred to an advertisement for a supermarket poultry brand. According to Alimantado, the rhythm track for 'Best Dressed Chicken' was the result of a jam session at Dynamics funded by Lenroy Moffatt and a number of other individuals; though spontaneously created, the chord structure was later found to be similar to Bill Withers' 'Ain't No Sunshine', previously covered in reggae by Ken Boothe. Many cuts of Lenroy Moffatt's rhythm would be altered by a number of artists, and Tado himself used the rhythm for alternative cuts such as the religious 'Plead I Cause', the topical 'Oil Crisis' and the nonsensical 'She Wreng Up', but 'Best Dressed Chicken' is definitely the most inspiring. Tado explains that it was his close work with Lee Perry at the Black Ark that determined the shape of the tune. 'I'v e got an innovative mind where music is concerned, and so has Mr Perry. Put the two of us together and the result is "Best Dressed Chicken". It was an innovation I had in Randy's studio, but if you go to Mr Perry with an idea he doesn't just handle it as though he's an engineer, he handles it as though it's his thing – he puts his initiative behind it.'

**The Best Dressed Chicken [Bernard Warin]**

By the time Doctor Alimantado recorded 'Best Dressed Chicken In Town' Dennis Alcapone was already spending most of his time in Britain, though he returned to Jamaica for sporadic recordings; since 1979 he has been permanently based on the eastern edge of London. The toaster expresses some ambiguity when reflecting on his first trips abroad. When I came to London in 1973, I met up with the Pioneers. Luddy Crooks used to take me to the Q Club in Praed Street, Paddington quite regularly, because at that time Q Club was swinging. Pioneers and Nicky Thomas was living the high life, because they had tunes in the charts, so it was more or less the jet set that would go to that club. They used to take me down there and introduce me to some top-class whores. When I go back to Jamaica Harry J just built up his studio, so Luddy and I went to Harry J and did the album _Belch It Off._ I don't have fond memories of that album. It is a strange album, because that wasn't something that was planned.'

Dennis explains that his permanent emigration to the UK stemmed from various complications in his personal life. 'After my first trip to London I went back home and had a terrible bike accident, and it's like I lose something out of that accident. I lose two of my front teeth, because my head went inna this van, and I had a girl on the back of the bike and she drop inna the middle of the road. After I get better, I come back up to England with the Jamaica Showcase, which was Dennis Brown, Toots and the Maytals, Cynthia Richards and Skin Flesh and Bones with Al Brown. That's when I didn't have roots any more in Jamaica, 'cause from '74 I overstayed my time here. My father died, and I couldn't go back to Jamaica because my papers wasn't sorted, and the government said if I leave the country I wouldn't be able to come back in. So I got married, and that's how I really get caught up in the England thing. In Jamaica the violence was going on and the dance was being shot down, a whole heap of shooting going on. The runnings was quite different here – it was a cool operation compared to down there. Dance couldn't go on, because if the police didn't shoot it down the rude boy did, so that's one _of_ the reasons I guess that swing me a bit.'

Since he was away from Jamaica for such extended periods, Dennis Alcapone's influence naturally dropped at home. But before leaving the island he passed the deejay baton to Lester Bullocks, a protege who would gain greater fame as Dillinger. Born in 1953, he was raised by his grandmother on the edge of greater Kingston in Saint Andrew. 'My mother was living in America, so I was living with my grandma. She used to sell in the market on Friday,' he says, leaning against an oil drum outside Tuff Gong studio. 'Down the years I'v e been inspired by U Roy, Alcapone, King Stitt – deejays before my time.'

Alcapone distinctly remembers the thin young man's early enthusiasm. 'Dillinger was living just across the way, so whenever the sound is playing he always come right beside the amplifier. One night him ask me fi give him a talk over the mike.

He wasn't brilliant, but you could hear that there was something. Him used to come more often when I'm playing and work himself in.'

Dillinger subsequently became the leading deejay on a sound called Smith the Weapon, based on Payne Avenue off Spanish Town Road. Anxious to record, he began doing the rounds of Kingston studios: however, he encountered little interest and much resistance until Lee Perry decided to give him a chance at Dynamics in 1973. 'I remember Scratch voice him for about two days straight,' Alcapone recalls, 'because inna them days, when them man there get hold of a man, them make sure them get as much as them can, just in case the man bust out. But really and truly Dillinger wasn't in his peak yet, so those tracks didn't make an impact.'

As he was something of an apprentice to Dennis Alcapone, Lester Bullocks originally called himself Alcapone Junior, but Lee Perry deemed the name Dillinger more suitable and it has stuck with the toaster ever since. Though singles such as the topical 'Middle East Rock' and rebel-praising 'Headquarters' were taken from his initial session, much of the material was left unissued. This was perhaps due to Dillinger's uncertain delivery resulting from his lack of recording experience.

His first big hit, 'Platt Skank', was for upcoming producer Phil Pratt; the song decried the vanity of men who plait their hair. Dillinger then cut a few discs for spiritual melodica player and visionary producer Augustus Pablo, including the hard-to-fathom 'Brace A Boy', recorded in combination with a certain Clive; apparently the song refers to the braces that hold up little boys' short pants. He continued to record on a freelance basis, cutting several memorable singles along the way: 'I was campaigning, I was like a freelancer. I do a couple songs for Abyssinians, a version of "Satta Massa Ganna" [called "I Saw E Saw"] and "Crashie Project", and a couple for independent producers like Bunny Lee and GG records.' Dillinger also recorded extensively for Coxsone Dodd, who eventually issued the compelling _Ready Natty Dreadie_ album.

**Deejay for hire: Dillinger [Adrian Thomas]**

Another deejay to reach the Jamaican charts in 1973 was Prince Jazzbo, a toaster whose deep voice bordered on the menacing. Born Linval Carter around 1950, Jazzbo had a rough urban upbringing: he describes himself as a 'street boy' whose parents were engaged in 'badness'. Meeting the heavily built, domineering toaster face to face at Kingston's Countryside Club, it is easy to believe; he has several prominent facial scars and behaves in a manner that can only be described as intimidating. In his largely unsupervised youth Jazzbo naturally gravitated to the downtown Kingston music scene, gaining valuable contacts among artists and others in the industry. 'Me know Scratch, Lee Perry, from boy days, and Bob Marley and the Upsetters.' Jazzbo's deejay apprenticeship came on the Whip sound system in Spanish Town. In 1972 he talked his way into Studio One, where his first take of 'Crab Walking', voiced on Horace Andy's 'Skylarking', resulted in a major hit; Jazzbo's wild cut mixed dance-craze motivation with snatches of proverbs and Rastafari intonation, all delivered in a highly charged, low growl.

Other intense Studio One singles followed, including the stern 'Crime Don't Pay' and the devoted 'Imperial I' (over Burning Spear's 'Door Peep'), but Coxsone held back the bulk of his material. His debut album, _Choice Of Version,_ was not issued until 1990, eighteen years after it was voiced. Coxsone's hesitancy led Jazzbo to cut discs for the competition by the end of 1972, first on sides such as 'Mr Harry Skank', 'Meaning Of One' and 'Mr Want All' for Glen Brown. 'Inna the seventies, Glen Brown is the man who give me a lot of encouragement,' the toaster confirms. 'He get me to be self-reliant.' Despite Coxsone's reluctance to promote the greater portion of his work, Jazzbo insists he holds no grudge against him today. 'Coxsone is a nice producer and I respect him very much. Coxsone Downbeat's the best producer inna the whole reggae industry. Me never have nothing bad fi say about Coxsone, because Coxsone make me popular and make the music industry get so established that millions of people are eating food out of it.'

After hooking up with Glen Brown, Jazzbo made his first crude attempts at self-production by cutting further versions of Brown's 'At The Cross Roads' rhythm: 'Plum Plum' had sexually suggestive imagery, incongruously introduced by a religious phrase, whilst 'Crankibine' decried the negative tendencies of loose females, its instrumental B-side utilizing peculiar, out-of-time instrumentation. 'The off-beat piano is me,' Jazzbo consented, 'and I'm playing a melodica on it as well. It's just a natural mystic: music is a thing where you just see it before you even make it – you just put it together and put it out.' These versions were voiced at the studio of King Tubby, with whom Jazzbo often collaborated. 'Working with Tubbys was a pleasure. I work with Tubbys from 1972.'

**From street boy to star deejay:**

**Prince Jazzbo [Beth Lesser]**

After releasing sporadic singles on the Count 123, Mr Funny and Brisco labels, Jazzbo voiced his first single for Lee Perry at the producer's recently inaugurated Black Ark studio at the start of 1974. 'Penny Reel' had a cool stream of proverbs related by the deejay, and 'Good Things' warned of the dangers of truancy before Jazzbo lost his train of thought, rendering much of the song an instrumental. Later that year and into the next, Jazzbo's international fame spread as a result of his infamous feud with I Roy, charted on a series of clashing discs instigated by the owner of a record shop in Toronto with the assistance of Bunny Lee. As I Roy explained to radio disc jockey David Rodigan, 'It all started when George from Monica Records at 1553 Eglington Avenue West in Toronto came to Jamaica. He's a producer and he runs one of the biggest black record shops in Canada and he wanted some singles, one from me and one from Jazzbo, so we go round by King Tubby's studio and Bunny Lee, as the producer whe have the rhythms them now, he make Jazzbo go into the studio first fi do him track. Well, Jazzbo in the studio about an hour-and-a-half, just everybody a laugh, like we down a wharf. A murder me a tell you, him couldn't get the lyrics right! Him couldn't get the feel, so Striker Lee say, "What happen, Jazzbo. Come out of the studio, make I Roy go do him tune now, man." I go into the studio and say, "Tubby, roll the tape!" through I-man nah shape and them t'i ng there, and I just open my mouth and say, well, "Prince Jazzbo, if you was a juke box, I wouldn't put a dime in your slot. Move out of the way!" That was the first take. This tune lame him up bad, pure dub wounds to him head top, have him under tight wraps them time there. The whole thing was a joke, but it becomes a reality. About a week after, the record come out and start bubbling at the record shops all over the place. Bunny Lee take Jazzbo and say, "You no hear the one I Roy make? You know the man a take liberty with you? Come we go a studio, make a tune about I Roy!" and Jazzbo go straight to me head.'

The pair's first sorties were built around insults: on 'Straight To Jazzbo's Head' Roy accused Jazzbo of ugliness, whilst on 'Straight To I Roy's Head' Jazzbo claimed I Roy was an imitator of U Roy. The next round contained more serious allegations: in 'Jazzbo Have Fe Run', I Roy made reference to an incident said to have taken place near a downtown drugstore, in which Jazzbo was attacked by music promoter Trevor 'Leggo Beast' Douglas for plagiarizing the lyrics of another deejay. 'Big Youth a do a tune, "Wise Shepherd", and Jazzbo go in the studio and a listen Big Youth's tune. Before Jah Youth's tune come out a street, Jazzbo make a tune now, "Wise Shepherd", with the same lyrics as Jah Youth's own. So him no come a town now, because at that time, him live out in Cockburn Pen in a likkle pig pen. When him come town to North Parade, and him profile and come inna Randy's with him basket with him record them, and Leggo Beast go see him – that time Leggo Beast 'mongst Jah Youth – one lick with a bottle, and Jazzbo drop flat under a moving van! Head bust up and all them thing, and when the people see that, and the van a run over him head and Jazzbo get up... it's like a racehorse, when them move off and jump a hurdle. Him run past the drug store whe name Kincaid, and me just make a tune say, "Him run past Kincaid, him couldn't get no first aid, him get lick with a hand grenade." '

**The survivor: Jah Stitch [Beth Lesser]**

Jazzbo's retort in 'Gal Boy I Roy' had bite, but was ultimately weaker; his insistence that 'If deejay was your trade, you wouldn't talk about Kincaid' only emphasized the conflict with Big Youth's associate, and his claim that 'If I wasn't ugly, you couldn't call my name in your record to get promotion' was hardly an assertion of superiority. When I Roy struck back with 'Padlock' Jazzbo dropped out of the contest, and Bunny Lee had to convince singer Derrick Morgan to aim a volley at I Roy with 'I Roy The Chiney Come Round'; but Roy swiftly eliminated the competition entirely with the gloating 'Straight To Der-Morgan's Head'.

The feud between Prince Jazzbo and I Roy recalls the earlier vinyl battle waged by Derrick Morgan and Prince Buster; though their recorded attacks had a nasty edge, privately the parties remained on friendly terms, and I Roy would later record a series of tunes with Jazzbo in the producer's chair. These battles prefaced other recorded jousts in the US hip hop scene, such as that waged by LL Cool J against Kool Moe Dee and Roxanne Shante versus UTFO, as well as later Jamaican deejay battles between Beenie Man and Bounty Killer; a coffin was even paraded on stage when those two toasters clashed in the late 1990s. But such rivalry wras not always a matter of fun and games or symbolic conflict. In 1975 Jah Stitch was wrongly blamed for the death of one of the key members of Tippertone sound, as Stitch had left Tippertone to work for a new set called Black Harmony; since the two sounds were scheduled to clash, some people felt Stitch must have been responsible for orchestrating his rival's death. Stitch was subsequently shot in the face at point-blank range in the shop next door to the Wailers' record shack, but miraculously survived after being taken to hospital by I Roy and percussionist Sky High.

The deejay experimentation recounted in this chapter was among the most important elements of the evolving reggae continuum. The album successes of U Roy, Big Youth and I Roy showed that the art of toasting had reached a new high in the mid-1970s; since then the status of the deejay has never slipped.

**The Mighty Diamonds at Channel One [Dave Hendley]**
**CHAPTER EIGHT**

None Shall Escape The Judgement: The Flyers and Rockers Styles

_He pioneered the 'deejay' records through Hugh Roy, then brought in the 'version' flipside styling. His own unique engineering skills produced the almighty 'dub', which has now evolved into the very latest – the now sound – 'styling symbols' or simply flyers'... Tubby will always be at the roots of Jamaican sounds._

From the sleeve notes of _Dub From The Roots,_  
Total Sounds LP, 1974

_Channel One have a time when it used to run things._

Tabby Diamond

AS THE DEEJAYS were bringing more rants and chants into the vinyl arena, the dominant sound was shifting again as a result of rhythmic innovations from the Aggrovators, a Soul Syndicate spin-off utilized by Bunny Lee. Just as Lee had made Soul Syndicate the premier backing band at the start of the decade, so did his command of the Aggrovators bring about momentous change.

Bunny says the name Aggrovators came from an expression used by Eddy Grant, the Guyanese singer who was then leading mixed crossover group the Equals in London. 'Me used to have the Bunny Lee All Star, 'til you hear Joe Gibbs come with the Joe Gibbs All Star, and it never fair to the musicians neither, because me or Joe Gibbs can't play a note 'pon a guitar. Me and Eddy Grant start spar from inna the sixties, and every time you say something, Eddy say, "That's aggro, man! You'r e causing me aggro!", because you'r e aggravating him. Me start call my musicians the Aggrovators, and the Aggrovators tear up some music inna the seventies right back to inna the eighties.'

The Aggrovators had a loose line-up, typically featuring Chinna and Tony Chin on guitar and Bernard 'Touter' Harvey on organ. Santa was the mainstay on drums, but Carlton Barrett and 'Benbow' Creary sometimes took his place. On bass Family Man gave way to George Fullwood, occasionally supplanted by Earl 'Bagga' Walker, who in turn was replaced by Robbie Shakespeare, a young protege of Family Man from an East Kingston ghetto who had something of a tough reputation. Shakespeare's heavy bass lines gave the Aggrovators' material its solid anchorage, and he eventually became the band leader.

'Growing up from school, what I really like was drums,' Shakespeare says, leaning back in a chair on the balcony at Sonic Sounds studio. 'I used to have drumsticks and play on a bench, and then guitar got my attention – rock and roll lead guitar. When [I] hear the bass, the bass sound powerful and it make a difference. Jackie Jackson had an album where he stand up with a bass, and it always fascinate me – he was one of my greatest inspirations. Coming from school, I used to hear Jackie Jackson on [John Holt's ] "Ali Baba", every one of them Treasure Isle stuff. My older brother Lloyd, that time he had a group named the Emotions. In the group he have an acoustic guitar, and I used to trouble it. My first real encounter with a bass guitar was [when] Hippy Boys was doing some rehearsal – Family Man was playing. At that time, I didn't know it was really bass. Family Man started showing me the songs, so when Family Man left Hippy Boys I was the lad to come in. They didn't like to pay money, but I didn't care about the money then.'

The rhythmic style most associated with the Aggrovators was a 1974 innovation called 'flyers'. This was the 'flying cymbal' sound built on a mechanized high-hat adapted from the 'Philly Bump' soul beat developed by Philadelphia studio ensemble MFSB, whose song 'The Sound Of Philadelphia' (aka 'TSOP') was the theme of the popular American television show _Soul Train._ According to Santa Davis, the beat was first used on a song called 'None Shall Escape The Judgement'. 'MFSB, with "The Sound Of Philadelphia", they use a lot of that. The drummer was a favourite drummer of mine, and I said, "Damn, let's try that in a reggae sound." "None Shall Escape The Judgement" came up, and I started the song with that. Bunny Lee wanted that in every song. He was the one who name it "flying cymbal", which is an open-and-closed high-hat. Then the whole thing started ballooning.'

**Robbie Shakespeare [Beth Lesser]**

'None Shall Escape The Judgement' was written by Earl Zero, who voiced the song first for Bunny Lee with Chinna Smith, a couple of other Greenwich Farm amateurs and Johnny Clarke sitting in on harmony. 'Chinna was one of my best friends,' Zero says at his home in Santa Cruz, California. 'Chinna lived a couple blocks from me. His dad had a sound system, so every day we meet, play some songs and lick chalice [smoke marijuana in a water pipe]. Bunny Lee have a bar right there at Fifth Street and the empty lot, we used to round there and sing. Bunny Lee come and hear us all the time and say, "Me want to take you to studio to do something." 'Though the singer was bom Earl Anthony Johnson on East Avenue in 1953, Striker dubbed him Earl Zero at this session to avoid confusion with Earl 'Chinna' Smith. 'My dad is a fisherman,' Earl explains. 'He always dock him boat at a wharf called Zero Wharf, so them call him Fisherman Zero. When me and Chinna do the song, Bunny Lee just use the name Earl Zero.' Unfortunately for Zero, this recording was something of a false start. 'Me do "None Shall Escape", but maybe he never like my version, so he go in the studio with Johnny Clarke.'

**Earl Zero [David Katz]**

'None Shall Escape The Judgement' gave Clarke the major hit he had long been hoping for. 'Singing started from school days,' the tall, burly singer says in the lobby of a London hotel; he is a philosophical person who expresses himself without haste. 'I was born in Kingston in 1955, grow up in Waltham Park Road and I was in Trench Town for many years. I get a scholarship to Jamaica College. With Ibo Cooper from Third World, and Roger and Ian Lewis from Inner Circle, _we_ had a school band [to gether].'

While still at school, Clarke nearly became a Studio One artist. 'Me and Jacob Miller were close friends, and we used to go to Studio One and buy drinks for Leroy Sibbles and them man, through we want to stay inside. Bob Andy was supposed to write some songs for me, because Downbeat loved my voice, but because my parents wanted me to concentrate more on school, I never had the time like Jacob to go to the studio regularly. After school I started taking it more serious, trying to get some recording done. But it wasn't happening, so I started doing some talent shows with artists like Barry Brown, Mighty Diamonds and Sugar Minott who had the African Brothers, but we were young and nobody had any name yet.'

Clarke began recording in 1973, but his Federal debut was not released and an early effort for Clancy Eccles was not promoted sufficiently; 'Everyday Wondering', produced by Rupie Edwards, was the first single to make an impact. 'I was at the bus stop, wondering from which angle something's going to happen, so me just bring forth the song,' Clarke explains. 'It's all about me wanting to be successful. It was a hit in the dance, but it wasn't popularized country-wise among the population. That was my first time in King Tubby's studio – Rupie took me round there to voice. I made the rhythm at Channel One with Jackie Jackson and Hux Brown.'

'Wondering' eventually became relatively popular in London and New York, but Rupie's recasting of the rhythm as the abstract 'Skanga', in which the producer imitated the sound of instruments with his voice, turned out to be such a big hit in Britain that Edwards appeared on _Top Of The Pops_ in 1974 and became a permanent UK resident by 1977. Though 'Wondering' never brought Clarke that level of fame, it attracted the attention of other downtown promoters including K. 'Stamma' Hobson, an associate of Keith Hudson for whom Clarke cut the unusual 'Golden Snake'. 'He was more like a bad man? laughs Clarke, 'the first kind or man there who have them knife and ratchet. That's the reason he was around Hudson, because he was there like a protector.'

**Moving out of Babylon:**

**Johnny Clarke [David Katz]**

After covering Smokey Robinson's 'You Really Got A Hold On Me' for Glen Brown, Clarke finally broke through by linking with Bunny Lee. 'Striker was on the corner at Chancery Lane, right beside Salvation Army, where we all used to hang out: me, Gregory Isaacs, Stamma, Dirty Harry. Bunny Lee usually go a lot to England those days, so one day he came around and just create an excitement: "I hear a tune you do for Rupie in England, mash up the dance up there!" He wanted me to do a cover, "My Desire", one of those old type of Shirley and Lee songs, so I went to Treasure Isle studio, [where the engineer was] Errol Brown. Earl Zero and some other guys from down [Greenwich] Farm came there with this song, "None Shall Escape The Judgement". Bunny now want me to go with the group, because he had different ideas. When there was a session about to get underway, the musicians would have a little workout. So there was Santa just playing the cymbal, and Bunny came in and heard it – "Oh boy! That's what we want" – so he translate the little workout straight on to the real thing. Normally Santa was just doing it as a joke. It was a new sound, because that is the thing that really tip me now. We went to voice "My Desire" at Tubby's , and [Lee] said I should try to do back ["None Shall Escape"] in my way different than Earl Zero. I develop the vibes and run down the place, because it was all about that new sound and a new youth. From that it was strictly Bunny Lee right along, because Bunny Lee had the sound and everybody wanted to go to Duke Reid's studio now. Gregory Isaacs and _GG_ went there after to do "Love Is Overdue".'

Johnny Clarke stuck solidly with Striker for the next few years, 'through the cymbal, because that was the hit way, that was what the people needed'. The sound proved the perfect platform for Clarke's vocal style, which he punctuated with undulating slurs. Along with singers such as Al Campbell, Clarke was one _of_ the first to record the forerunner of what would later emerge as the dancehall style; his lyrics alternated between love songs and 'reality7' themes, and he was equally adept at cover tunes.

Clarke says his strongest influences were sound system culture and Rastafari. 'I grow up with a sound system next door to me, Veejay the Dub Master, that was at 31c Waltham Park Road. He's the type of soundman that get up at four o'c lock in the morning, and those days we have some steel horns up in the trees and you hear some wicked Alton Ellis – it usually wake me up to go to school. Veejay focus 'pon Rasta. We'r e really from the ghetto, and Rasta a focus from them time. My mom was a Christian, but I grow up with my bigger brother "Obadiah", James Clarke, a Rastaman, so after a while me start focussing on Rastafari.'

Obadiah founded the short-lived Clarke's label and produced some unsuccessful sides with Johnny after 'None Shall Escape'; another brother, well-known drummer Eric 'Fish' Clarke, also helped direct Johnny to music early on. 'He has a lot to do with my career too, but he never grow up with us. He ended up at Maxfield Park [Children's Home], then he move on to Alpha Boys' School. When he usually come [home] for holidays, he always deal with music, so I was more clingfing] to that.'

Johnny Clarke's partnership with Striker Lee brought the singer into the big time, and their association was responsible for some of the biggest hits of the mid-1970s. Like 'None Shall Escape The Judgement', 'Move Out Of Babylon Rastaman', 'Peace And Love In The Ghetto' and 'Rock With Me Baby' were substantial Clarke hits built on the flyers sound.

Cornell Campbell, who had moved to Bunny Lee's stable by 1972, emerged as Clarke's most consistent rival from the success of 'Natty Dread In Greenwich Town'. 'I became frustrated', Campbell says of his final departure from Studio One, 'when I went to Randy's record shop and hear songs like [the Bassies'] "Things A Come Up To Bump". The music a sell, and all the time my good tune them nah sell. I write "Natty Dread In A Greenwich Farm" [aka 'Natty Dread In A Greenwich Town'], because me say, "Let me sing pure reality tune, pure tune whe the people on the street them like." I went to Bunny Lee with that song, because I had a band named Don Cornell and the Eternals. Robert Shakespeare used to play with a band named Big Relation, and they used to play in my band with Carly [Barrett], so Bunny Lee came one night to hear the band play at Greenwich Town – that's the first time I know Bunny Lee, and him love how me sing. Him came to Robert Shakespeare and ask if him can play on sessions. I never like the idea, because if Robert Shakespeare leave, the band going go down. Robert Shakespeare left the band and go to Bunny Lee, so I use one of my singer partners, Ken Price, to play bass, but the band never up to the prestige of how we did have it. After a while me automatically start to sing for Bunny Lee, back-up artists with guitar and piano, and I play the bass with artists like Derrick Morgan. I said to Bunny Lee, "What you think about 'Natty Dread In A Greenwich Farm', you think it going to do anything?" Him said, "Yes, man, that is a bad tune." King Tubbys tell me, "Boy Cornell, I went a East [Kingston] the other night and hear the song a play, and somebody tell me police want to raid the place and shoot up the sound, because the way the song bad, whole heap of man get mad up there" – every time a song going to hit, some disaster happen. "Natty Dread In A Greenwich Farm" start to run the place big time.'

Cornell explains how his rivalry with Johnny took root. 'Johnny Clarke came before with a song named "None Shall Escape The Judgement". A brother named Earl write the song, and Bunny Lee give me the song to sing first, but through Bunny Lee owe me some money, me draw away a little bit, and Bunny Lee give it to Johnny Clarke to sing. That was him first big record. When me come back a town, Bunny Lee say, "You see if you sing the tune what would happen? Johnny Clarke sing it over and it gone." The tune start sell, so that's why I sing another version of it named "Gun Court Law".'

That song referred to Michael Manley's drastic response to Jamaica's escalating firearms problem. Opened in April 1974 at Camp Road, the barbed wire-encased fortress serves as a court of law and detention centre for those caught with illegal guns. Trials are conducted without juries, and with no opportunity for bail or appeals; the maximum sentence was initially indefinite detention, later changed to mandatory life imprisonment, and guilt could be established even by the possession of a single bullet shell. Though generally welcomed at its inception, the Gun Court was a surprisingly repressive creation from a man elected on a largely idealized platform of human rights.

Johnny Clarke addressed similar themes to 'Gun Court Law' on 'Joshua's Word', which also mentioned the call for citizens to 'bring in the guns' anonymously, without fear of being charged. The rivalry between Clarke and Campbell was now in full swing. 'It become a competition,' Cornell observes. 'Every man feel him bad, so I go write a song named "The Gorgon", and then Johnny Clarke come with "Move Out Of Babylon", and me come with "Dance In A Greenwich Farm", and Johnny Clarke come with [Barbara Lynn's ] "You'l l Lose A Good Thing", and me come with [Gene Chandler's ] "Duke Of Earl".'

Much of this material was given its final mix-down by Philip Smart, who was then resident engineer at King Tubby's . At HC&F, the studio he currently runs in Long Island, it is evident that Smart has something in common with most other sound engineers: his belly attests to untold hours seated at a mixing desk. 'The Bunny Lee era, that's when I came in the picture,' the chubby mixer says. 'All the Johnny Clarke, like "None Shall Escape The Judgement" – I convinced Bunny to put out Johnny's version. Bunny wasn't really too hot on the tune, but after Johnny sing it I tell him, "That tune there hot", and he say, "If you feel it, mix it," – just like that.'

**Prince Philip Smart [David Katz]**

Smart says he came to work with Tubby through the sound system circuit, and recalls his early studio experience in the company of Errol Thompson and Augustus Pablo. 'My mother says I was born with some turntable in me hand,' he laughs. 'I was always into music and collect records as a youth, used to play at birthday parties, keep coming up until [I] play at dances and clubs. I used to live at Harbour View, and Errol was living in Harbour View with his relative named Goff, an electronic engineer who usually repair and build amplifiers. He had a set named El Don, and all who was interested in electronics used to hang out over there. At that time, Errol was working at Studio One, used to bring home exclusives like "Nanny Goat". When him leave Studio One and go to Randy's , that's when I start going to Randy's after school to watch him work.'

It was in this period that Smart moved to Havendale and became friendly with Augustus Pablo. 'Myself and Augustus Pablo put together lunch money, book a studio session, and recorded some tracks with a band that we brought in, Reckless Breed, like six rhythm tracks to a two-track tape. Pablo was playing piano. Those tracks weren't hardcore enough, so we went back and did "Skanking Easy" [a melodica recut of the Soul Brothers' "Swing Easy", based on the theme _of Fiddler On The Roof\\._ Myself, Pablo and another friend put our money together and produced that record, pressed it and put it out on the Hot Stuff label. I was still in high school, but we use the top musicians at that time: Family Man, Carly, Tyrone Downey and Reggie. We did that on four-track at Randy's – that was like my first real production. I had my own sound, it was called Sound of Distinction, just a little neighbourhood set. We used to go to King Tubby's and cut dubs, that's how I first met him, with rhythms that Pablo would record – like Pablo and myself did "Cassava Piece" originally at Dynamics. I always was at Tubbs' and Tubbs was more receptive, he let me hang out there every night, until one day he just say, "Jackson, go and take that voice", probably about a year and a half from I was first going there.'

The flyers sound that sprang up in this era, with its uncanny high-hat emphasis, provided King Tubby with ample opportunity for exciting dub experimentation, as heard on the classic album sets _Dub From The Roots_ and _The Roots Of Dub._ On certain single B-sides mixed at Tubby's studio, Philip Smart came up with dub invention by subjecting the tape machine test-tone to short bursts of repeated delay. 'I put it on John Holt's "The Clock",' Smart explains. 'When it starts, you hear "beep-beep-beep-beep-beep-beep-beep-beep-beep..." You had to find stuff to be innovative – people always looking out for something new.'

Stereo mixing was another 1974 innovation. 'Those days, everything was mono.
