Pablo did [a melodica version of Ken Boothe's ] "When I Fall In Love" [issued as "Forever Love" on Brad Osborne's Clocktower label in New York], that was done in stereo, and "Black Is Our Colour", Wayne Wade – that was Yabby You's production, recorded at Lee Perry's studio.' The overseas popularity of such experiments saw Smart mixing his first dub album. 'England was very hot on some of the mixes I was doing, so after Bunny go to England, him say, "Philip, you are the man!" That's how me get to do the LP _Creation Dub,_ the one with Bunny Lee's picture on the front, but them no want to tell Tubbs that I did a dub LP, because they think that Tubbs will feel a way.'

Smart also notes that Tubby pioneered other innovations on his sound system in this period, despite police raids that would ultimately close it down. He mentions in particular an exciting event that took place in late 1973 or early 1974, after Tubby's sound had already been destroyed once by the police at a dance. 'It was twice them have it,' says Smart. 'The first time them shot up him sound, they had mash up the amp and he decided he wasn't going to play any more, he was just going to do engineering, the studio thing. But everybody said, "Tubbs, you have to bring back the sound", and him rebuild. The first dance that play was at Up Park Camp where they have the Gun Court right now, but Gun Court didn't exist then. Around midnight, U Roy ride in on him bike and tune up the sound. Nobody know that Tubbs put a secret thing in the amp: when they put on the mike, U Roy say, "You'r e now entertained by the number one sound in the land-land-land-land land-land", the first time people hear echo, "King Tubby's Hi-Fi-fi-fi-fi-fi... " A man lick a shot inside Camp, fire a shot inside the dance, the way that it sweet them! That was the first time him unveil echo in an amp and you can't see it, because he have it incorporated inside the amplifier.'

Not only was this the first time that echo was unleashed on the nation at a dance, it also marked the debut airing of the classic Ansel Collins rhythm track known as 'Stalag', created for producer Winston Riley; the song's forceful bass and vibrant horns have remained consistently popular, and the original rhythm continues to be used for new creations. 'Riley brought "Stalag" and give it to Tubby,' Smart recalls. 'That was the first time it played and that was the first tune that play that night, play for about a half an hour.'

In the mid-seventies Philip Smart went to New York to further his training in electronics: 'I left in late '74 or early '75, went to school, did broadcasting and engineering.' After Smart's departure, Tubby's sound was again destroyed by the police. 'The second one was some politics dance,' Smart details. 'Them shoot up the amps. Police spray the amps [with bullets] and took away the amps. Tubby was suing the police because them mash up his sound, shoot up the dub plates and a whole heap of thing. Him was very upset.'

With Smart out _of_ the country and Tubby in need of an assistant engineer, Pat Kelly returned for another stint at the Waterhouse studio. 'I work in Tubby's in three different sessions – left and come back,' he explains. 'I did about three years of mixing at various studios, like Tubby's , Dynamic, Joe Gibbs, Randy's .' Some months later, when Prince Jammy returned from Canada in January 1976, he took over most of the engineering duties, becoming a highly valued member of Tubby's team.

While King Tubby and his crew continued their dub explorations, Sly Dunbar dissipated the dominance of the flying cymbal with a new style called 'rockers', developed at Channel One with his house band, the Revolutionaries. Rockers revolved around the improved Channel One drum sound, which obtained a better depth of clarity once the studio upgraded to sixteen tracks in 1975; Sly made the most of its capabilities through a number of new beats. Often, his precision timing marked a 'militant' time on the snare; sometimes he doubled the rhythm to make better use of the bass drum and tom-toms. 'Sly would play for a whole day when we were just trying [things] out,' says Joseph Hoo-Kim. 'He was very helpful in that way. You wouldn't get another drummer who would want to do that.'

Often, though not always, the Revolutionaries made something new out of an old standard by recutting what had previously been a Studio One hit, giving favourite rhythms a thoroughly contemporary make-over, driven by Sly's modern rhythms and augmented by up-to-date keyboard, guitar and horn sounds. I Roy's residency at the studio, along with others such as keyboardist and arranger Ossie Hibbert, also assured a heavy measure of sonic excellence. 'He usually play sound,' says Joseph Hoo-Kim of I Roy, 'and we wanted somebody that have a sound knowledge. We figure that he knows the most popular rhythm that is being played in the dancehall.'

Sly Dunbar explains that the Revolutionaries evolved from various club bands of the early to mid-1970s. 'Ansel Collins was the first one who really taught me to do floorshows,' he recalls. 'He was playing in the RHT Invincibles and I was playing in the Yard-brooms. Ansel ask me to come on "Night Doctor", and he called me again to do "Double Barrel". I start doing some sessions, and then I was a member of RHT Invincibles, playing out at Tit For Tat, and then we play with Tommy McCook, Supersonics, for a little while. Ranchie used to play in Volcanoes, with Al Brown. They were seeking a new drummer, and that was a steady job: every Wednesday I play with the band, because Tommy was like every weekend.'

**Sly Dunbar [Beth Lesser]**

Though most people knew him as Charlie then, Dunbar says it was while he was in the Volcanoes that he received his nickname Sly. 'The owner of the band was a man called Mr Harvey. Every time he see me I'm talking about Sly, because I was a great fanatic of Sly and the Family Stone. Mr Harvey look at me and say, "Everything you say is Sly", and if I do something he would say, "Just like Sly", so the name just stuck.'

Eventually, the Volcanoes mutated into Skin Flesh and Bones. 'We used to play in Discovery Bay at Calypso Colony hotel. The hotel went under receivership and we came back in town. Ranchie and myself went back to Tit For Tat and spoke to Dickie Wong, the owner of the club, about getting back the band. We were trying to do a name like Earth Wind and Fire, then we call it Skin Flesh and Bone. We played the club for like five weeks, and we record Al Brown's version of Al Green ["Here I Am Baby"], and then a lot of recordings start coming in. We used to play at the club every Wednesday to Saturday. We had a weekly salary, so we never really have to depend on sessions, but we love to play on sessions, so Ranchie or Ansel or Lloyd Parks, when they get sessions they would call – that's how I think I end up playing on Tubby's "Psalms Of Dub", through Ansel for Black and White.'

By 1975, the bulk of Skin Flesh and Bones had become the Revolutionaries. As usual, the membership fluctuated. Keyboardists Ossie Hibbert and Robbie Lyn were sometimes featured. The horn section usually included Tommy McCook on tenor saxophone, Herman Marquis on alto and Vin Gordon on trombone. Robbie Shakespeare played bass on some of the later material. But the staple core members were normally Sly, Ansel Collins, Ranchie and Lloyd Parks. When Joe Gibbs, with engineer Errol Thompson, opened a new studio that year at 24 Retirement Crescent, in an industrial area near Cross Roads, it was this same core of musicians who most often provided backing, though Maurice 'Blacka Morwell' Wellington and Eric 'Bingy Bunny' Lamont of the Morwells also had prominent positions there, and members of Soul Syndicate and other musicians sometimes held court. Lloyd Parks eventually became the resident bassist at Gibbs' studio, after Robbie Shakespeare became a more prominent member of the Revolutionaries.

'When Skin Flesh and Bone start playing Tit For Tat again, Robbie was playing at a club next door,' says Sly. 'So when we get a break, we would go and check every band and they would go check us.' 'First time I see Sly was on Red Hills Road,' Robbie confirms, 'where there was clubjs] named Tit For Tat, Stables and Evil People. At Evil People Fab Five used to be their resident band, but Fab Five had some overseas engagement. The band I was playing with at the time was Big Relation from Spanish Town. Benbow was playing drums and the keyboard player left, which was Tyrone Downey. Touter Harvey, who later play with Inner Circle – originally Touter was a singer with Hippy Boys, and Touter only knew two chords, but we say him better play keyboards. He said he's going to check this band with Sly and Ranchie and a girl who sing named [Cynthia] Richards. Sly's band named Skin Flesh and Bones. I go over there and see Sly, and the way him sit down 'pon the drums, the drum set was way out here and it sound wicked. At that time, we was doing a lot of recording. We just finished Burning Spear's _Marcus Garvey,_ and Johnny Clarke and Slim Smith [material], and I was one of the main Peter Tosh bass players at that time, because I finish "Concrete Jungle" and "Stir It Up" for Wailers – I play on those tracks. I was one of the main man with Aggrovators, so me say, "All right, Bunny Lee, blood clot, see this little drummer here wicked? We have to do a session with him", so Bunny just call in Sly and we build a lot of tracks.'

**Joe Gibbs' studio, Retirement Crescent [Adrian Thomas]**

'I always like Robbie's bass,' Sly continues, 'because when we start meet in sessions down by Randy's , we talk and ask questions. In those time, every musician used to respect each and every one, so we go and check one another, sit down and work out some music, design a few chords and things. That was then in Jamaica – it doesn't exist no more. After I hooked up with Robbie we started some sessions for Bunny Lee, but a little before that Robbie and me was playing in the Revolutionaries, 'cause the first song he played on, he played piano on "MPLA" [an instrumental remake of "Freedom Blues" cut at Channel One]. He play a lot of guitars too, 'cause Ranchie was playing bass when Robbie came in. Because Lloyd Parks didn't turn up for a session Ranchie would play bass, but when Robbie came in and played a few guitars they change over: Ranchie play guitar and Robbie play bass.'

**Real Revolutionary: Lloyd Parks [Adrian Thomas]**

'The real Revolutionaries was me,' Lloyd Parks says on the veranda of his home in New Kingston, 'because that was part of Skin Flesh and Bones – we moved to Channel One and they call it Revolutionaries. After that, Robbie came in as the bass player, but we started it: me, Sly and Ansel Collins at Channel One.'

Parks started his career as a vocalist in the Termites during rock steady. 'Wentworth Vernon, he had a band in Waterhouse called the Termites. Bobby Kalphat plays keyboards. I never really like the name, but I wanted to record so I join him. Termites lasted about three years – we made this album called _Do The Rock Steady_ for Studio One. Then I join RHT Invincibles – it was a Rastaman who get it together for us, his organization was called Rainbow Healing Temple. That's where I really get my goal where I can go on an instrument and play as a guitar player. At that point, I played on a million-seller song for Ansel Collins called "Double Barrel".

'About a year after, I switch to bass. I was playing in a band called the Thoroughbreds" at the Stables. The bass player didn't show up one night and I tell Bobby Aitken, the band leader, "I can play that, you know." That night, first time I-man play a bass on a stage, a lot of people crowd around me, like you have the Go-Go dancers that used to be there, everybody crowd and say, "Hey hey hey! It's a new thing." From that night, I never stop playing bass. Because of the sound I had, I play a tune like "Nebucadneezer", Junior Byles [recorded in 1972], and the man say, "Boy, what's making that sound? It's just something different." In other words, when a Lloyd Parks song is playing you can tell it's Lloyd Parks on bass.' In those days, Parks used to break up his low bass patterns with cascading spirals of rapidly plucked high notes, pointing to his proficiency on guitar.

Parks became the in-house bassist at Randy's in this period, playing with the core of the future Revolutionaries. 'I used to do songs for Randy's Impact label, like a studio band. Whenever anybody recording, they call me, Ansel Collins, Sly and so on.' He also began cutting self-produced vocal work, including the moving 'Slaving', which Parks says was autobiographical. 'As a youth growing up, my parents migrate to England when I was thirteen, so I end up with my aunt in the country, Port Antonio, and I had a hard time. When I come back to town I just get that inspiration, "I'm slaving every day for my living", because I was really trying to make it. Tyrone Downey played on "Slaving" – he was a little schoolboy in khaki uniform, used to leave school and go to the studio, he was so young. Even today, when I listen to what he played, I can't believe it.'

Parks then joined Skin Flesh and Bones. 'We were in the same neighbourhood, Waterhouse,' he recalls. 'They produce a song with Al Brown, [who] sang over an Al Green song. It was a massive hit and I get a lot of raises for that bass line – even UB40 did a version using the same bass line.'

At Channel One, Skin Flesh and Bones became the Revolutionaries. With their new rockers sound, the group backed the bulk of the biggest hits of the mid-to-late seventies by artists such as the Mighty Diamonds with 'I Need A Roof, Leroy Smart with 'Ballistic Affair' and the Meditations with 'Woman Is Like A Shadow'.

The Mighty Diamonds' strong vocal harmony was equally suited to politically relevant lyrics and romantic material; whether voicing reverence of Marcus Garvey, singing of heartache or covering soul tunes, the Diamonds' soaring harmonies created a lasting impression. Their different tones are exciting when intermingling in unison, and lead singer Donald 'Tabby' Shaw's clear tenor is given pride of place when answered by call-and-response choruses from Fitzroy 'Bunny' Simpson and Lloyd 'Judge' Ferguson. Judge is the group's main lyricist, and his Garveyite upbringing ensures plenty of references to the Jamaican hero; his falsetto has also been put to good use on numbers such as 'Go Seek Your Rights'.

On a brief visit to London, where he always makes sure to visit his dentist, Tabby describes how the group came together in the late sixties when its members were doing welding and carpentry jobs and while Bunny ran a small sound called Soul Pack. 'We start out late 1969, come together in a full-force trinity in mid-December. When Bunny Diamond was a youth he used to sing with Jah Lloyd's group named Meditators, and them times he used to check me still, 'cause we used to live in the same street. We say we want a next man, search and search, and it takes about two years. We buck up on some different youths, but they weren't ready yet, until December '69 we buck up on Judge and the vibes start. Me know Judge from long time, but me never did see the music between me and him, because we used to go to the same school but different age group. The first song we recorded as a group was "Girl You'r e Too Young" for Rupie Edwards.'

After moving on to cut 'Oh No Baby' for Stranger Cole, they issued the self-produced 'Got To Live Some Life' and a cover of the Stylistics' 'Betcha By Golly Wow', before landing at Lee Perry's Black Ark, where they recorded 'Talk About It' and provided harmony on Susan Cadogan's 'Hurt So Good' – a chart hit in England but not particularly popular in Jamaica. 'Jah Jah Bless The Dreadlocks' and 'Carefree Girl' followed for Bunny Lee, but their true breakthrough came from their association with Jah Lloyd, a singer and deejay who had worked as a record salesman for Rupie Edwards and Lee Perry.

**Tabby, Bunny and Judge: the Mighty Diamonds [Beth Lesser]**

About a year before his death I met the man born Patrick Lloyd Francis at his regular hangout, one of the rougher street corners in the Kingston suburb of Washington Gardens; his matted beard reached his grimy T-shirt, and a miasma of stale rum surrounded him. Lloyd was proud of the role he had played in bringing the group to prominence. 'I was born in 1947 in Saint Catherine, a place named Point Hill. My dad used to love farming. At the age of twelve, I left school and come to town, because my mum die and I have to grow with a [step-mother], which was not so very smooth. I meet up on man like Theophilus Beckford, Eric "Monty" Morris, Toots and the Maytals, and trod on to a record business place named Success Records where I do a song like "Look Who A Bust Style" as Meditators, Paul Aston Jennings and myself- we all meet in Trench Town. I was a salesman for Rupie Edwards, selling tunes like "This Wonderful Sound" with Dobby Dobson, "Burning Fire" with Joe Higgs and the Gaylads' "My Jamaican Girl". Me and the youth them rehearse together, people like Ansel [Cridland of the Meditations] and Roland Burrell, and along Diamonds come. They was very good, and I take them to Coxsone, but Downbeat say them not ready: "Two months' time." Come to Rupie Edwards, they do "Girl You Are Too Young", I select that song there.'

**Shame and pride: Jah Lloyd [Adrian Thomas]**

Francis scored his first notable hit as a deejay at the Black Ark in 1974 with the self-produced 'Soldier Round The Corner', a song describing the tense feelings in the street brought on by emergency curfews; its success allowed him to bring the Diamonds back into the studio. 'I go back for them and do "Shame And Pride": Robbie Shakespeare on bass, Horsemouth on drum, Reggie that play with Upsetter on guitar and Errol Thompson, the engineer. That is the song that give them the go. After doing that, I take them to Channel One, telling Joe Hoo-Kim that he should record them. I couldn't manage them much more, because I was getting penalized by selling the record: people buying three or six copies, sabotaging, victimizing and jeo- pardizing. So this is when I have to carry them to Joe Joe, who tell them it's time fi do them album.'

'My best experience right now was Channel One,' Tabby emphasizes, 'because it's the first cut, the foundation. Channel One have a time when it used to run things.' They started at Channel One in 1975 with two more Stylistics covers, 'Hey Girl' and 'Country Living', the latter eventually hitting in Britain; causing a bigger impact was 'Right Time', a symbolic number relating Garvey's prophetic vision as a signal for black retribution. Other strong singles followed, including 'Back Weh', 'I Need A Roof, using a recut of Larry and Alvin's 'Mean Girl', and 'Have Mercy', using a recut of the Cables' 'Baby Why'. The group was among the first reggae acts signed to Virgin records, who issued their acclaimed debut album _Right Time_ in a gatefold sleeve.

The Meditations were another of the classic harmony trios to rise to prominence in this period. In the depths of Flatbush at Sir Tommy's studio, leader Ansel Cridland and fellow member Winston Watson are adding harmony to a Glen Washington track; the friendly Ansel describes the early hardships that led to his chosen career. 'Them say I man were born in '51 in Westmoreland, a little place they call Lambeth Pen. My father leave Jamaica when I was seven years [old] and my mother was in Kingston, so it 'was just me and my gran at that time. After my father leave, it was a [step-mother] that took me to a country [town] named Darliston, and they was mistreating me. Some lady next door see that and take I back to my people, and then my uncle take me to my mother in the Mountain View area. My father was a horse trainer and he had to live on the premises, this place named Red Hills. I want to be a jockey, but I used to listen [to] Delroy Wilson, and the man who really inspirate I at the time was the guy that used to fix the tractors. I hear those guys singing one day, that t'i ng amaze I so much, I say, "No, I'm going to sing." One of our brethren, horse kill him. From there, t'i ngs never look bright, so I told my friend, "We'r e going back to town – I'm gonna sing, you know." Back in the Kingston 11 area, as a young youth, I say I want to be a mechanic, I want to be a barber. But that wasn't the way, for all I wanted to do was sing.'

Cridland made his first recordings at the end of the sixties as part of harmony group the Linkers; by 1972 he was collaborating with Danny Clarke, with whom he would subsequently form the Meditations. 'I do "Sitting On The Sidewalk" that was when I just met Danny. I produced that song, I sing it and Danny blowing the melodica on it. I give it to JJ to put out and him send it to Creole [for UK release]. Danny was with the Righteous Flames, doing back-up harmony at the time.'

At home in Arizona, Danny Clarke picks up the tale. 'I was born in Trench Town in '53. My mom went to England in 1960 and I went to stay with my step-dad in Clarendon. At about eleven, I went back to Trench Town with my biological dad, in Fifth Street. I start sing with Winston Jarrett, had the pleasure of singing with Alton Ellis in Alton and the Flames. After they stop singing with Alton, I was one of the Righteous Flames. The first session I did was with Coxsone Downbeat, "Free Angela Davis" and "Working Time", but I don't know if it come out. Then I did two songs for Scratch: "Zion" and "You Can Run", Things wasn't going too right, so I leave the Righteous Flames and start out for myself. The first song I recorded was "Great Messiah" for GG, that was [as] Danny Clarke and the Meditations. Then I live in Madison Gardens [a West Kingston ghetto known as Baktu]. I met Ansel down there in 72, and Ansel teach me a few guitar chords. Me and him started to become good friends, so we started trying a thing together. Like when I'm rehearsing, Ansel would come around and help me out harmony-wise, and when he's rehearsing I would go around and help him. We used to hang in a group called the Hombres, one of the first groups in Madison Gardens.'

**Winston, Danny, and Ansel: The Meditations [Courtes** **y** of Robert **Schoenfeld/Nighthawk]**

'Winston Heywood and the Hombres, they asked me to follow them for an audition to play guitar,' recalls Ansel. 'They was doing this song "Never Fall In Love". They were singing it into a ska and I change it to the one drop – I actually do the harmony in it also. I do a song named "Bongo Man" on the session, but my group never maintaining the harmony.'

'We wanted to form our own little group,' Danny explains. 'Winston was a good singer, he's got a good voice, but he couldn't control it. Whenever I would go check Lloyd Forrest, I would bring him with me. We were going to use him, but Lloyd couldn't wait – he was going to go with the Jays. So me and Winston start walk together, teach him more and more. We come back up by Ansel, so all of we just sing. We start to rehearse together, not as a group, but if Winston find somebody who wanted to record him, he can rely on me and Ansel to give him harmony, and if Ansel wanted to record, me and Winston would do backing vocals.'

Winston Watson's soprano provided a strong contrast to the deeper tones of Cridland and Clarke; he says his inspiration came largely from soul singers of both sexes. 'I was born in Trench Town in '54; mostly grow in Kingston 11. My daddy was a writer and performer and I think I get the inspiration from him – he used to perform magic, turn newspaper into an ice cream. Along that journey I used to go to _Opportunity Knocks_ and win, not even near my mind being in a group. Ansel was a "face-boy", bell-foot pants and soul hair and t'i ng. I guess I was about the same. I hear a lot of singers and everything I heard I sing – female singers too, like Diana Ross.'

'I see the potential in him,' Ansel continues. 'Him say him want to be a singer, so when we sing the Impressions' harmony it sound good. At the time, I was about to do "Woman Is Like A Shadow". This brethren named Calvin was to come and sing the harmony for us – we waiting on him and he didn't come. So Winston come along and say, "I will sing that tune." When I hear Winston come with the harmony, I say, "Winston remind me of Bunny Wailer's harmony, and this is the harmony we want."'

The group recorded the song in late 1974, but Joseph Hoo-Kim did not release it for well over a year. When he finally chose to do so, the song was a major hit. 'I went to see if I could get somebody to do it good,' Danny explains, 'so I went to Channel One. Ossie [Hibbert] used to be up there taking auditions, and I know him from way back with the Righteous Flames days. I said, "Ossie, we have some tunes", and sing a couple of songs. He said, "Come back Friday", and when I went back I carry Ansel. As he opened his mouth, Ossie said, "Don't sing no more. Come back Monday!" After we did that, Joe Joe said he didn't like the lyrics, so we had to change it about three times and he still didn't put it out.'

'I didn't like the lyrics,' confirms Joseph Hoo-Kim, 'I didn't like "A woman is like a shadow and a man is like an arrow" – it never sound practical to me. We sell a lot of it on dub, but I didn't like the song.'

'I was on Marcus Garvey Drive at Federal and met Dobby Dobson,' Ansel continues. 'I know him from long time in Flames days. He said he's the one taking the auditions over there. I went there and the first song I sing he likes it, second song he likes it. I saw Winston coming down the road, so I said, "This guy have some good songs too. I want you to listen to him." Dobby say we have to come back, so we went to Dobby Dobson and it was "Babylon Trap Them", "Longest Liver" and Winston did "Man Piabba, Woman Piabba", and Dobby loved those songs. After he released those songs, he said he wanted to do an album. "Longest Liver" was the first song that came out as Meditations. After that, Joe Joe released "Woman Is Like A Shadow", and that was the song that really break the name Meditations.' It was also _one_ of the biggest songs to boost the Channel One rockers sound.

Though the advent of rockers stole the fire from Bunny Lee's flying cymbal, the Revolutionaries' habit of adapting Studio One rhythms naturally caused most offence at Brentford Road, particularly after many of Coxsone's artists defected to Channel One. Coxsone's greatest weapon in the war of styles turned out to be Sugar Min-ott, a man with his ear constantly on the pulse of Jamaica's dancehall scene. 'It was a living war with Channel One,' laughs Minott. 'They used to call me "Coxsone's Boy". When they made "I Need A Roof for Channel One, I immediately know what it was, because I'm an expert in music and rhythm from [when] I was a kid. So I went to Coxsone and said, "Look, it's "Mean Girl". We went to buy a flask of rum, so I was hyped up, did over "I Need A Roof. Me and Tabby them was friends, but I didn't care because I was like "Channel One? I hate Chinese." That was my thing in them times – I was young that way: "I'm not singing for no Chinese." There was a next one called "Woman Is Like A Shadow". Coxsone called me and said, "I want you to sing this music, listen that tune," so I thought it was an old song from some old group that never came out and he wanted me to do it over, but I didn't know it was a Meditations song that never even came out yet. I did over "Woman Is Like A Shadow" and it came out before the original, because the original used to play on the sound. When my version drop in, the whole of Baktu was looking for me – it was a war with Channel One. Every time they try to do a Coxsone song I go and tell him, so they came and fling bottle and stone to mash Coxsone's studio up. They had the force – everybody was following the Chinese. Somehow Coxsone and Joe Joe got in some fight and that was that.' Perhaps unsurprisingly, Joseph Hoo-Kim contests Sugar's version of events.

**Studio One warrior:**

**Sugar Minott [Beth Lesser]**

As Minott grew up next door to one of Kingston's most popular dancehalls, music has been a central element of his life. 'My house was right beside the dancehall, Champagnie Lawn, at 82 Chisholm Avenue, run by a man called Champagnie. We get to know the music from them days, like Delroy Wilson, Derrick Morgan, and you used to see man like King Stitch. As small kids we couldn't go in this dancehall legally, so we have to make holes in the fence. And we used to imitate the dance, keep our own dance afterwards, with cardboard box and condensed [milk] can. I grow up with my mom, four brothers and three sisters. My father was a man that sells meat, like salt beef, in the ghetto on the street, but he had a rough time. He couldn't take the pressure, so he went back to the country where his family was and start to do some farming.'

Lincoln Minott has a sweet voice, but his nickname Sugar stems from another source. 'Chisholm Avenue was a rough ghetto, 'nuff bad things was happening, so my mother was trying to take us to a better life. She save up a little money and rent this house in this middle-class area, Waltham Gardens. That's where I got the name Sugar, from my friends as a little boy in the street. I was kind of a fat kid, porky, so they always say, "You must have eaten all your mother's sugar, Sugar Belly!" After a while they just say, "Sugar."'

Like most of his peers, Minott got his start in a local amateur group. 'While I was in Waltham Gardens there was two big girls, Beverley and Trudy, and we try to form a group with some likkle boys, named Telstar. That wasn't working out so good, so these two big girls, they said they wanted to sing with me, but they didn't know harmony, and I didn't know nothing about that myself. We were trying to be in the Festival competition, but it was rubbish! We didn't even pass the first level.

'My mother was struggling. She used to sell at the market, go with all these big loads on her head, so she had it rough with eight kids. Then she had to move from that area, because we couldn't afford the rent no more and the landlord didn't like us so much, because we were people from the ghetto trying to live in some kind of posh area. So we end up back in Kingston 11, where we came from, and that was a good thing because that's where I met Tony Tuff [aka Winston Morris] and Derrick Howard [sometimes referred to as Eric Bubbles].

'At Delamere Avenue, there was a big yard where all of us used to meet and burn herb, learning to build a spliff as a little kid, in time to go to school. That's where we meet Tony Tuff, a likkle man on an old car, playing a guitar – he's our godfather. He was singing around with people like Bop and the Beltones, trying to carry two harmonies, around 1969-70. I was like fourteen, going to first form in school, and we meet a youth called Derrick Howard from the country. He say his group was the African Brothers, say he want us to join in it. He took us to Rupie Edwards and Micron Music, but it was Dessie Young, Tony Tuff and that youth first, because I was just a little kid in beanies. They did one song, "Behold I Live" – I think that was for Micron Music – and somehow it wasn't happening. Dessie Young wanted to sing by himself, so I was in the group officially.

'We enter the Festival at the Ward Theatre, and in the second run we dropped off. Tony Tuff did write the song, saying we'r e not going to follow the English no more, so I guess that was too heavy. We started recording for people like One Foot Jimmy. The group did "Lead Us Father" – this came on our own label called Ital through Micron Music. We did a couple songs for Rupie Edwards, like "Mysterious Nature" and "Party Night", and I did a song called "Youths Of Today", the first song I get to lead, and "Righteous Kingdom" for Micron Music. We used to hang out at Randy's records – that's how I get to know Clive Chin, Gussie Clarke, Leonard Chin, they were the king of the road for selling records. For Clive we did "Hold Tight", and we do "Torturing" for some man on our street named Duke Thelwell, but he never gave us no money. That was one of the first songs that made an impression for us.'

Sugar says the group passed through much tribulation before it split and he aligned himself with Coxsone. 'First of all, Derrick Howard was the leader for the group. We was going to stage shows and people boo, because this guy couldn't lead. Tony Tuff start to lead, so the songs start to get better. Then he was giving problems in the harmonies, but all of it was experience. Ronnie Burke and Mike Johnson, they were Micron Music. They had Pete Weston working there, they had Merritone, and Merritone them didn't like my music. We were singing Rasta, we sound like the Abyssinians, that's our kind of sound – real cultural roots, and they didn't like it. It was American [style] music they was promoting, like Beres Hammond, Cynthia Schloss, Rudy Thomas, and they was all singing Stylistics. They always say our music was no good, and then the company somehow wasn't working right, so [Mike Johnson] just gave us back our tapes, and Merritone went to do Turntable club, Ronnie Burke went to do Sunsplash, Pete Weston went to Canada.

'On Delamere Avenue, in the rough ghetto where we used to meet, there was a big bar in a back yard, play sound system. Coxsone used to pass there and take a drink. One time the group round the back and he came and listened to us and say, "Yeah, it sound good." We did a song at Studio One, "No Cup No Broke", but them say, "Coxsone's thieving". I'm saying, "Don't you hear the music, man? That's where I want to be. I don't care if I get no money, because that's the music I want to sing to." We decided this group's not working out, 'cause we kinda make an impression but the music not going anywhere.'

Heavily involved in sound systems, Sugar Minott knew exactly what Studio One rhythms he wanted to sing on and approached Coxsone with the idea of updating the label's earlier hits. 'I used to be a selector, play sound from [when] I was likkle – that was my first job. First sound I played was my brother-in-law's sound, and in Maxfield Park there was Sounds of Silence and Keytone. I used to play those sounds. I end up having a sound called Jah Live, and then from Jah Live to Black Roots, and from Black Roots to Youth Promotion, so it was always sound – I was a sound system man. Many people say, "I start singing at the church," but I start singing in the dance. I was born in the dance, never been singing in the church – I was raggamuffin style, so sound system was my beginning. I was playing sound system, so that's how we got to be singing on the Studio One records, because we only had to play the version from a Studio One record and make up our own song. That's how it started, and nobody was doing that before us.

'When I went to Coxsone, I already know what I wanted to sing because I taped off "Love Me Girl" with Leroy and Rocky, and I taped off "Going Home" with Bob Andy, and I made my own song to it, because I figured these rhythms are so bad I don't need to make new rhythms. I could make a song to this, and if I go to Coxsone and do it quick he doesn't need to get no musicians. I went to this audition – it was a long line of people, and I was the last. It was Coxsone himself, say, "Man, what you used to do before?" "I was just in the country, planting some food." "I think you should go back there... You, come back next year." I say, "Look, sir, I know you'v e got this rhythm already, I can make a song for it." When he heard he don't have to go and make no music, he took me round there same time. He said, "Come back tomorrow", and that was it. I signed a contract to be at Studio One for one year, and I was there for six years without no contract, voicing every day, doing back-up, shaking tambourines and whatever.'

Together with fellow singers Johnny Osbourne and Freddy McGregor, Minott played an influential role in shifting Studio One towards the evolving genre which by the end of the decade would be known as dancehall. In the election year of 1976, however, Studio One had to fight hard to counter the exciting developments coming from Channel One and Joe Gibbs. It was at these studio locations, especially the Maxfield Avenue hotspot of Channel One, that musicians and vocalists were best able to translate the feeling of ghetto suffering into music.

**Culture [Robert Schoenfeld]**
**CHAPTER NINE**

Natty Dread Taking Over: The Ultimate Rise of Roots Reggae

_If you listen to reggae music, you don't need to buy the paper. Reggae music tell you everything wha' happen in Jamaica._

Roy Cousins

_I eat, sing, sleep, drink, live, red green and gold music._

Joseph Hill

THE LATE 1970S were a particularly turbulent time in Jamaican politics as the nation became ensnared in the opposing ideology and rampant demagoguery of the Cold War. Michael Manley embarked on a series _of_ ambitious projects to better the lives _of_ the underprivileged, but ultimately these weakened the Jamaican economy, ironically most hurting those whom he sought to help and alienating middle-class supporters into the bargain. He was also vociferously attacking neo-colonialism and imperialism, sentiments that were noted with dismay by the conservative United States government.

In September 1974 Manley announced a dramatic shift in policy, steering the nation further left with a new era of 'Democratic Socialism'. Though the concept seems to be an ill-defined marriage of the free market to Marxist values, much of Manley's new campaign was clearly against capitalism. He nationalized most _of_ the bauxite industry, which was central to the island's economy, and proposed an international alliance by which the industry's electricity would be generated by Mexico and Venezuela. Most importantly, in May that year he had changed the way that fees were paid by the multinational corporations which purchased Jamaica's bauxite. By replacing the low fixed rate with a percentage levy, he aimed to ensure that these fees would be in keeping with the actual price of the mineral. The result, unfortunately, was that the multinationals turned to other Third World nations for a cheaper supply, greatly reducing Jamaica's annual income.

Henry Kissinger, Secretary of State in the right-wing government Gerald Ford inherited from Richard Nixon, was displeased by the new bauxite levy; more troubling were Manley's growing links with Cuba and his backing of the presence in Angola of thousands of Cuban soldiers, who were helping the Marxist MPLA government fight off an invading South African army backed by the CIA. Kissinger came to Jamaica in December 1975 to pressurize the leader into silence, threatening to cancel a billion-dollar trade agreement if Manley voiced support for the Cuban intervention; as a prominent member of the Non-Aligned Nations group, Manley refused to be dissuaded and the trade deal evaporated.

Naturally Kissinger found an ally in Edward Seaga, who had assumed leadership of the JLP in November 1974. In February 1975 Seaga began an offensive against Democratic Socialism by requesting a State of Emergency; the number of politically motivated murders had increased in recent months, and Seaga aimed to show that Manley was not in control of the situation. Unsurprisingly, the request was denied, and the violence continued to escalate. The following month one of Manley's bodyguards, PNP heavy Winston 'Bury7 Boy' Blake, was killed; during his ostentatious state funeral, eight people were injured by sniper fire as the coffin passed the JLP stronghold of Tivoli Gardens.

By the start of 1976 the violence had significantly worsened: in January JLP gangs started a gunfight in Trench Town, which culminated in the fire-bombing of twenty homes. The following day a PNP picket of an International Monetary Fund convention in New Kingston was infiltrated by JLP saboteurs who started a riot; dozens were killed, including four policemen, and shops were looted and burned. In May further politically motivated violence resulted in dozens of deaths, notably when a JLP gang fire-bombed a West Kingston slum yard. Apart from the dead, over five hundred people were left homeless.

By June there had been 163 known murders, including those of nineteen policemen. Manley had no choice but to call a State of Emergency, the first since the one implemented by the JLP in 1966; this time the nation was so unstable and the levels of violence so high, with automatic weapons and fire-bombs being used with increasing frequency, that the Emergency would be in place for a full ten months. Mindful of the way the progressive Allende government in Chile had been obliterated by a military coup in 1973, with scores of human rights abuses and terrorist activities orchestrated by the CIA, Manley feared a destabilization campaign had been initiated in Jamaica with the aim of toppling his leadership. Such ideas were given credence by ex-CIA officer Philip Agee, who visited Jamaica in September 1976 to speak out about the Agency's destructive presence and its overall control of the region.

When asked about what role the CIA played in the political power struggles of this era, Edward Seaga gives an ambiguous response. 'It's much of a myth to say that the CIA played any great role,' he insists. 'I think they may have done certain things in the background, but out front we never saw them. If they were here, they surely never introduced themselves to me, and I can't tell you of anybody that we knew who was even invited abroad to be trained in anything. Maybe people were here who were agents who I have been introduced to, but not as operatives. I just don't know where they would have participated. Certainly we wouldn't have needed their help anyway, because people were reacting in an extraordinary way. They just didn't want to have anything of Manley's concept of Jamaica being socialistic. [The PNP] were doing a wonderful job stumbling over themselves, telling people they are not wanted in Jamaica – "There are flights leaving every day for Miami", that sort of thing. [Manley] did everything that was wrong to do, destabilizing himself.

Though Seaga is unwilling to identify any concrete training or CIA assistance given to the JLP or its supporters, he suggests that Manley's 1975 _brigadista_ programme of sending unemployed Jamaicans to Cuba for a year's training in construction techniques had sinister undertones. 'Manley was having Jamaicans trained in Cuba as _brigadistas,_ and they were very active in making the country far more militant,' he stresses. 'Dress militant, act militant, so they put the country on almost a wartime footing.' Additionally, senior Jamaican police officers were training in Cuba and PNP ministers were having meetings with the Castro government. Cubans also came to Jamaica to build the Jose Marti Technical School in Spanish Town, opened shortly before the general election of December 1976.

The election campaign was resolutely violent: in October, gunmen sheltering in a local PNP office fired at Edward Seaga and former Prime Minister Shearer in the settlement of York Town. After a lengthy gunfight, in which ten people were shot, the office was burned to the ground. Then, twelve days before the elections were due to be held, an attempt was made on Bob Marley's life.

By then, of course, Marley was an international star, and though certainly not the most popular singer at home he was definitely the best-known Jamaican artist world-wide, and one seen as wielding significant financial and political power. After signing with Island for the _Catch A Fire_ album in 1972, the Wailers underwent massive changes. They were marketed as a Jamaican rock band led by Marley, whose 'rebel' image was further cultivated; their material was subjected to overdubs by Island's rock musicians and remixed for foreign audiences. Peter Tosh and Bunny Wailer left the group after the 1973 set _Burnin_ to pursue solo careers; they were replaced by the I-Threes (Rita Marley, Marcia Griffiths and Judy Mowatt) for the acclaimed _Natty Dread_ album, cut with Sylvan Morris at Harry J. Then Marley recruited American guitarists Al Anderson and Donald Kinsey to broaden the group's overall sound. Despite such personnel changes, Marley's message was still rooted in the rebelliousness of Rastafari; when it was reported that Emperor Haile Selassie of Ethiopia had met his physical death in August 1975, Marley refuted the notion with 'Jah Live', a song expressing eternal devotion.

Though the Wailers often toured abroad and based their Tuff Gong organization in Chris Blackwell's large former home at 56 Hope Road, close by the Governor General's residence, they had not lost touch with their Jamaican audience nor the ghetto communities they came from. In October 1976 the group was asked to headline a concert for the people, due to take place in December; called 'Smile Jamaica', it was a government-sponsored event. The group agreed, but found themselves pawns in a political game: Manley set the election date for a few days after the concert, linking the two events in people's minds and insinuating that the group were tacitly endorsing the PNP. 'That was a crazy time,' remembers Family Man. 'They was going around with the campaigning for who wanna be in position, and who's going to be opposition, and not everybody was really into that change, but that's how our country is run still. We were getting a lot of rumours that things were happening, both sides at the war path, a lot of gun smoke... Shortly after we get our date, the campaign was set for the same time, so people looking at the thing differently. In the middle of one of the rehearsal[s], it was ambush in the night!'

The band had received telephone threats not to appear at the event, but Marley insisted. An armed group of PNP supporters, known as the 'Echo Squad', were despatched to guard 56 Hope Road around the clock. Marcia Griffiths wisely chose not to stick around, using the pretext of a non-existent concert engagement to retreat to her sister's home in New York. 'I told him that I didn't get the vibes to stay back and do it,' she recalls, 'because I was just so sure that something was going to happen and because sister Judy had a vision plain-plain. I told him, "Please don't do it – something terrible is going to happen." I begged him. The same day that I left Jamaica, they had the shooting.'

**Eternal: Bob Marley**

Seven gunmen, supposedly from one of the downtown ghetto strongholds stormed 56 Hope Road on the night of 3 December. Amazingly no one died in the attack, although Marley's manager, Don Taylor, took several bullets and had to be airlifted to Miami for emergency surgery. A bullet grazed Marley's chest and ended up in his arm; another penetrated Rita's head. Despite their wounds the Marleys and most of their band, supported by members of Third World, went through with the performance, sending a message of defiance to whoever was behind the attack.

Various theories have been put forward as to who organized the shooting. The most widely held belief is that the orders came straight from the top of the JLP, though others have suggested PNP involvement (because of Marley's links with major JLP enforcers); some say it was not connected to politics at all but due to a racetrack scam gone wrong, or a mishandled drug deal carried out by people who based themselves in Bob's camp. 'Nobody has ever found out who the gunmen were,' remarks Edward Seaga. 'We don't really know. People at the Bob Marley level would always have a string of hangers-on who are close to them. In that sort _of_ mix any souring of relationship may take place, causing that kind of reaction.' If the JLP was indeed behind the attack they miscalculated badly, as Manley won a second term of office and his PNP acquired a comfortable majority when the election finally took place on 16 December.

The events that culminated in the 1976 general election were widely commented on in music: Pablo Moses sang 'We Should Be In Angola', the Meditations cut 'Running From Jamaica', Max Romeo sang of 'War In A Babylon'. Even the instrumentals of the day, typically led by a vibrant horn section, spoke of Cold War politics: alto saxophonist Herman Marquis and trombonist Vin 'Trommie' Gordon created 'IMF'; Ossie Hibbert arranged 'Kissinger' at Channel One, where an instrumental remake of 'Freedom Blues' was titled 'MPLA'; over at Joe Gibbs' the Professionals house band, led by Lloyd Parks and Sly, cut an album called _State Of Emergency._

A headline declaring the imposition of the 1976 State of Emergency was used for the front cover of the Gladiators' debut album, _Trench Town Mix Up,_ produced by Prince Tony Robinson and released internationally by Virgin. 'Them time was when we were going to give up,' remembers Albert Griffiths. '[Producer] Enos McLeod get me in to play on a session, but he didn't have any money to pay us, so I lay a little rhythm that I didn't even finish plan, and when things split up I go to Ossie at Channel One and say, "Beg you a little time", and we voice it [as] the song "Know Yourself Mankind". When we go down there Prince Tony say, "Who really write for the Gladiators? It's a long time me wrant to find the man." I said, "What you mean? A me named Albert Griffiths", but him say him no believe me, because he look to see man who look more fussy. That's how me and Prince Tony get linked up. We signed to Virgin, do _Trench Town Mix Up,_ then _Proverbial Reggae.'_

Recorded at Joe Gibbs' with Lloyd Parks, Sly and Sticky, _Trench Town Mix Up_ was a stunning debut. Though the title track had originally been cut at Studio One as 'Bongo Red', the Prince Tony version had a palpable tension mirroring that which engulfed the Kingston slums as Natty Dread made an increasingly visible stance against 'Babylon'. Aside from a couple of gratuitous Wailers' covers, cut to make the most of any vocal similarity between Griffiths and Marley, the rest of the disc, and virtually all the Gladiators' subsequent work, used proverbs and folk sayings to describe the everyday reality of ghetto life. 'They used to call me the parable man,' Griffiths explains. 'Me sing these songs that really teach you to be wise and clever. Me have a thing that says, "Looks Is Deceiving", because you don't judge a book by the cover. It was all that kind of thing that me sing: songs that say don't under-rate people, mind what you talk, don't talk too much and chat bad things about people. Me no really sing no song yet that doesn't have a meaning.'

When asked about the line 'Cow never know the use of him tail 'til the butcher cut it off in 'Looks Is Deceiving', a slight smile passes Griffiths' lips. 'The cow use him tail to swat flies, mosquito, anything that will bite him,' he explains. 'When him lose it, mosquito and flies start to bite him and him no have nothing more to fight it. Him have to sit down in a ground and rub them off, so him never realize his tail was so important. I have another one where I say, "A sailor-man never know the value of his piece of knife 'til it break." Those things teach you to be wise and think twice. That is mainly what we grew up on, old-time proverbs, things whe your grandparents teach you.'

While the Gladiators' Virgin releases drew critical acclaim in Britain, Griffiths and Clinton Fearon continued backing Yabby You, notably singing harmony on his third album, _Deliver Me From My Enemies,_ issued in the UK on singer and promoter King Sounds' label, Grove Music. 'A whole heap of songs me have with Yabby You,' Griffiths confirms. 'King Sounds set up Yabby for money to produce and we used to play most of them songs.'

The dominant overseas labels were now Island and Virgin, battling to control the best of Jamaica's new music. It was Island, of course, that spearheaded the international marketing of reggae through their widespread success with Bob Marley. Third World signed with Island in 1975, and eventually became the second most successful reggae act on the label. The original Inner Circle group, active since the late 1960s, included key members who would later form the core of Third World: guitarist Stephen 'Cat' Coore, keyboardist Michael 'Ibo' Cooper, vocalist Milton 'Prilly' Hamilton and percussionist Irvin 'Carrot' Jarrett. William 'Bunny Rugs' Clark, who has been Third World's lead singer and figurehead since 1976, says he first performed with these musicians in Inner Circle when he returned to Jamaica from the USA towards the end of 1969.

**Third World [urbanimage.tv/Adrian Boot]**

'I went to New York for about a year,' an exhausted Rugs explains after an appearance at an outdoor festival. 'My mother and father had moved there before me.' Clark grew up in the harsh streets that make up the heart of downtown Kingston, but came across Inner Circle closer to their base in a more genteel area uptown. 'I went to this night club, the Tunnel – that was off Hope Road above Matilda's Corner, you have some Chinese restaurants there. I heard this band and told them that I wanted to do a number with them. Their singer was Bruce Ruffin, and Bruce Ruffin went to England, so I took his place.' Clark stayed with Inner Circle until 1971 when he returned for a longer stint in New York, where he worked with expatriate bands Hugh Hendricks and the Buccaneers and the Bluegrass Experience.

Meanwhile, Inner Circle continued to evolve. Cat Coore had joined the group in 1967 at the tender age of twelve. In 1973 exam pressures forced him to leave, and in July of that year he formed the first incarnation of Third World with bassist Colin Leslie, who would later be the group's business manager. Inner Circle was now being fronted by Jacob Miller, whose stammering vocals brought a contract with Capitol.

**Bunny Rugs [urbanimage.tv/Adrian Boot]**

Cat Coore has said his group's original name was Sons of the Third World, and that its underlying concept was directly related to Michael Manley's programme of Democratic Socialism – perhaps an unsurprising sentiment from a musician whose father was then party chairman and Minister of Finance in Manley's government. Though Coore took the role of musical director from Third World's inception, members drifted in and out of the group in the early years.

Like Cat, Ibo, Prilly and Carrot found their way into Third World from Inner Circle. Drummer Carl Barovier, also ex-Inner Circle, was briefly a Third World member, with former Tomorrow's Children and future Zap Pow drummer Cornel Marshall filling his shoes for a period. Bassist Richard Daley also joined after the disbanding of Tomorrow's Children. Like Inner Circle, this group was associated with the uptown section of Kingston society, but was competent enough to back Toots and the Maytals on their 1972 North American tour. At the start of the 1970s Daley was a member of the Hell's Angels night club band with multi- instrumentalist and future producer Clive 'Lizard' Hunt, plus Wayne Armond and Allah, who would later found the group Chalice. Before that, Daley played in the Astronauts with keyboardist Earl 'Wire' Lindo and drummer Mikey 'Boo' Richards; his recording debut came on Ken Boothe material in the late 1960s.

In 1975, a stabilized Third World played some gigs in England. They caught the attention of Chris Blackwell and signed to Island records for their self-titled debut album, a strong collection of roots material flavoured with rock, jazz and soul elements. The following year William Clark came into the Third World fold. The singer received his first taste of the Jamaican recording scene as a solo artist in 1974–5, after being brought to Lee Perry's Black Ark studio by Glen Adams. Clark subsequently returned to New York, hoping to land a contract with Atlantic; instead, he unexpectedly joined his old Inner Circle band mates in a truly professional Third World that had already supported Bob Marley at the famed Lyceum concert in London. 'I knew this Jamaican who was working for the Vice President of Atlantic records, and I spoke with her several times,' Clark explains. 'Then I went to New York and started to write songs, trying to put together a demo tape to present to Atlantic. Third World was on their first United States tour – that was 1976. They were already signed to Island and they were playing in the [Greenwich] Village [at] the Bottom Line. I went to the concert, and I'v e been with them from that day until now.'

With Clark as lead vocalist, the group's sound grew markedly stronger. Joining the band at the same time was Willie 'Roots' Stewart, a solid drummer tutored by Winston Grennan. Third World's resultant second album, _96 Degrees In The Shade,_ is considered by many to be their best. Songs like the emotive 'Human Market Place' showed their awareness of social injustice; 'Jah Glory' was evidence of a deeply felt spirituality; '1865' (better known as the title track) was a slice of Jamaican history that was a major hit abroad. 'That is strictly about Paul Bogle, who was hanged in Jamaica for leading a revolution against the English for equal rights and justice for his people,' says Clark. 'That was a true story, and the year for that was 1865. Paul Bogle is one of Jamaica's national heroes, so the same way Burning Spear sings about Marcus Garvey, we sing about Paul Bogle.' The band would remain with Island to the end of the decade, scoring their biggest hit in 1978 with a funky cover of the O'Jays' 'Now That We Found Love'.

Once Winston Rodney teamed up with Jack Ruby in 1975, a contract with Island brought Burning Spear to the attention of overseas audiences with the release of _Marcus Garvey_ the following year. 'Jack heard about me, and he happen to check me out,' Rodney recalls. 'We were talking musically, but Jack was more like a sound system man – he never get involved in recording business before. His argument did sounding strong and constructive to me at that time, so he decided to get involved in the recording business and we did two albums, _Marcus Garvey_ and _Man In The Hills.'_ For these recordings, Burning Spear took the form of a trio. 'When dealing with Jack Ruby, I need some harmonies,' Rodney explains, 'so I draw for Delroy Hines and happens to give back [Rupert] Willington some work, use both of them to do background vocals.'

It was in this phase that Rodney began the veneration of Marcus Garvey that has marked the bulk _of_ his work. 'Marcus Garvey is from the town where I-man is from,' Rodney explains. 'There were elderly people wTho knew a lot about Marcus Garvey, but not so much of the young people. [I] go around, listen to various people saying different things about Garvey, and each person know something different. Then I start to run a research, musically, to see if anyone were there singing about Marcus Garvey, but no one was really hitting that point musically, so I decided to get into that. I think the message from Garvey really stands out, not only for me, but for a lot of other people. I think Garvey did have a strong plan, and if more people did pay attention to Garvey and his work it's possible things would be more easier for folks like we today. More people has forgotten Marcus and his philosophy and his doctrine, 'cause people get carried awray.'

Equally moving were songs that examined the legacies of slavery, such as 'The Invasion', 'Tradition' and 'Slavery7 Days'. As Rodney sees it, 'Those songs was part of my history, part of my heritage, the struggling of my people. Knowing that I am an African descendant, an African-Jamaican, those songs based upon the struggling of black people overall – not only [in] Africa. Those songs carrying a different concept, sending a different message.'

Similar ideals, though with more overtly religious overtones, were explored on _Blackheart Man,_ Bunny Waller's debut solo set; also issued by Island in 1976, the album was an artistic triumph, though less successful in terms of sales. It was also partly through the Marley/Wailers connection that Chris Blackwell made a licensing agreement with Lee Perry, issuing in 1976-7 a series of adventurous albums, most of which rapidly achieved cult status. Perry was in the midst of what many feel was his most creative phase, and the new equipment that Island's funding enabled him to install allowed him to expand his sonic horizons, particularly on his superb 1976 creation _Super Ape,_ a disc perhaps rivalled only by Augustus Pablo's _King Tubbys Meets Rockers Uptoivn_ for sheer brilliance in the realm of dub.

Richard Branson's Virgin company entered the reggae arena at a particularly opportune time. When Virgin began to sign artists connected to Channel One, Trojan records was experiencing severe financial problems which ultimately resulted in voluntary liquidation. Smaller labels such as Klik and Vulcan spun off from Trojan's demise. Klik's first release, the highly successful _Dread Docks Dread,_ was a Big Youth set put together by Prince Tony in 1975, but Virgin eventually took control of the disc after Klik failed to maintain momentum. The album's outstanding tracks include 'Marcus Garvey Dread', a powerful recasting of Burning Spear's monumental 'Marcus Garvey' that highlighted certain prophecies attributed to the Jamaican hero of black self-determination

The Pama group, too, ceased producing in 1975, within a year of Harry Palmer's return to Jamaica to begin farming and preaching to a congregation in Mandeville. 'It was a blood-and-sweat business,' Palmer says of the music scene. 'I think I suffered a lot dealing with the artists.' He recalls being physically attacked by singers and producers he was dealing with in Jamaica: 'One broke a bottle and said that he's going to cut me up with it. He just want money – his royalties not enough. Then one of them got me and bucked me with his head. They made up their minds that the records sold millions, and you know nothing didn't go like that. He too wanted a cheque, so when they were finished I said, "I'm going to give you guys this, but I don't want anything more to do with you." I wrote out a cheque, but the bank said, "We can't change it", because I had called. I wasn't going to give them that money. They came back and threatened me, said Mr Seaga told him they'l l take it up. I said, "I don't have to be afraid of anybody, because I'm straight."'

Palmer also reaffirmed his religious commitment at this time. 'My wife being a devout church lady, I had to keep dropping her [at church] and picking her up back, so one day they persuaded me to come in. I thought, "Gosh, I'm driving a Rolls Royce", and I sat where I could see the car. Then something came to me: "Why are you worshipping a bit of tin? Don't ever look out there again! Change your seat and listen to what the preacher is saying. These material things won't get you anywhere." I went to Nottingham and got baptised, and that's when I went into agriculture. The reggae thing was conflicting with my religious thing, because by then the records were getting really rude.'

Pama had typically aimed their releases at Britain's black population, while Trojan targeted the white working class; instead of reaching for either group, Virgin followed Island's lead by going for university students and the hipper end of the rock audience. The Channel One connection brought Virgin highly successful albums by the Diamonds and I Roy, plus dub works featuring the Revolutionaries; with Prince Tony, Virgin scored the Gladiators and U Roy. HowTever, some have suggested that Virgin had a tendency to place too much emphasis on appearances and reputation in this era.

'I heard that Virgin never sign us because I never carried a Rasta image,' says Vernon Buckley of the Maytones, whose finest and most spiritually oriented work was handled outside Jamaica by Burning Sounds, a smaller independent. 'Those days we used to sell a lot, but we never really saying "Rastafari".' In contrast, Keith Hudson had the right look when signed to Virgin in 1976, but the poor response to the soul mode of _Too Expensive_ caused his contract to be terminated early, forcing Hudson to issue the excellent dub set _Brand_ on his Joint label in New York; its vocal companion, _Rasta Communication,_ was released by UK independent Green-sleeves. Virgin fared better with Peter Tosh's 1976 solo landmark _Eegalize It_ – acquired from CBS, who apparently picked it up from Island – and its strong follow-up, _Equal Rights,_ but Tosh was later lured to Rolling Stones records by Mick Jagger, whose attempt to bring pop stardom to the reggae firebrand drew predictably inconsistent results.

Though aspects of such companies' involvement in reggae has sometimes been viewed with scepticism in retrospect, certain acts clearly drew artistic benefit from the patronage of major labels. Sly Dunbar says the live tours that Virgin organized brought dub into the performance arena, as heard on a U Roy twelve-inch recorded in London in August 1976. 'The Lyceum ballroom with the Diamonds and U Roy, I think this was the breaking point that the drum [became] such a dominant figure within reggae music,' Dunbar explains. 'They said that dub wise couldn't be played live, they said it was the engineer doing all these tricks, so when we went to drum and bass and work it the people said, "No, I can't believe they'r e really playing it!" We go to bass and drum and go to rhythm, and one time there was a cue that somebody miss and I was playing the drum alone, and I start rolling the tom-toms around and the band came in. It was really a miscue, but we work it out and the place went in a frenzy.'

The Channel One sound was a perfect platform for deejays, and the one to hit the biggest on rhythms cut there by Sly and company was Dillinger. 'I come to Channel One where I do "Ragnumpiza", "CB 200", "Buckingham Palace" and "Cokane In My Brain",' the toaster boasts.

'Ragnumpiza' utilized the bare rhythm of a hard Channel One recut of Bob Andy's 'Set Me Free', with Sly Dunbar's militant drumming style and vibrant horn breaks providing a solid backdrop for Dillinger's jargon-filled lyrics and lengthy rolling r's ; he explains that the song is about ghetto sufferers, as 'Ragnumpiza come like ragamuffin'. 'CB 200' was a major hit that shifted Gregory Isaacs' uplifting cut of Bob Andy's 'Sunshine For Me' into a tale of dreadlocked motorcycle bravado. The atmospheric 'Caymanas Park' (aka 'Race Day') was a further success that captured the excitement of an afternoon at Kingston's racetrack. 'That was about two of Jamaica's champion race jockey, Emilio Rodriguez and George Ho-Sang,' the toaster explains. 'They was two great jockey that used to win a lot of races – they was so popular that you'v e got to put them in a song.'

It was the Yankee-styled tale of substance abuse 'Cokane In My Brain' that launched Dillinger to international stardom. 'I used to be on the North Coast at the Playboy Hotel in Saint Mary where a lot of hippies used to hang out, and we used to jam in the moonlight,' he recalls. 'I used to watch them get high, and they sing, "I'v e got cocaine in my brain", so I get the inspiration from there.'

The rhythm track of 'Cokane' was adapted from American funk hit 'Do It 'Til You'r e Satisfied' by BT Express, its bouncing grooves allowing Dillinger to explore better his concept of what life was like on the streets of New York City. His half-sung delivery and clever, rhyming lyrics brought the greatest success a deejay had ever achieved when the record was chosen for overseas promotion by Island records; the song was a smash not only in the UK and USA, but also in Germany, Holland, France and elsewhere.

The rising popularity of 'Cokane' and the _CB 200_ album in 1976 somewhat overshadowed another creative deejay work released by Island – the densely textured and esoteric _Colombia Collie_ by Jah Lion, the toaster better known as Jah Lloyd. It was the first Island-issued album to be produced by Lee Perry, with whom Jah Lloyd worked solidly for much of the mid-1970s, but financial disagreements led to a rupture by 1977 and the toaster subsequently signed to Virgin. 'I move away because there was a dispute between me and him,' Jah Lloyd explained. '[Perry] wanted to take me _on_ a tour after we done _Colombia Collie -_ never get much happiness from it. We start another album, do quite a lot of songs, but I left and do _Humble One_ for Morwell as Jah Lloyd the Black Lion, and I do _Black Moses_ for myself

Perry had better success writh the vocal material that Island released, first with Max Romeo's _War Ina Babylon_ album and then with Junior Murvin's _Police And Thieves._ Murvin is a singer with a wide vocal range, but is best known for a searing falsetto largely patterned on Curtis Mayfield. In person he is a distinctly humble man, still living in the modestly furnished home of his youth in Port Antonio.

'I was born in Swift River,' he explains, 'and my mother took me up here to my great-grandmother when I was one week old, so I grew up in this same house. From six years old I usually imitate Billy Eckstine, Wilfred Edwards, Nat King Cole, Louis Armstrong, Sam Cooke. I sing in every voice, but I mostly use the treble voice they call falsetto. When I was thirteen my great-grandmother died and I went to live with my grandmother in Montego Bay, pursuing a career in mechanics. After I left school, my auntie sent me a guitar from the States. The first band I sang with was the Hippy Boys – that time I usually sing as Junior Soul. About three times I sang with the band. After I left Hippy Boys, I went to sing with Derrick Harriott and Miss Pottinger. The first song I wrote was "Solomon" – it was a hit for Derrick Harriott.

While living in Trench Town, Murvin cut further material for Harriott and Sonia Pottinger before Noel BrowTn _of_ the Chosen Few brought him into the Falcons. Frustrated by a lack of concrete success, Murvin spent a few years back in Portland in the mid-seventies. 'I was singing in a band named Young Experience in Port Antonio,' he recalls. 'Bobby Ellis was the arranger. We went to Cuba, play right round Jamaica, play at National Arena for the Prime Minister's wife's birthday, Beverley Manley. When the band went defunct, I wrote "Police And Thieves".'

**Proverbs: Junior Murvin and friend** **[urbanimage.tv/Adrian Boot]**

'Police And Thieves' captured the tense feeling of a Kingston riven by political and social differences; the song translated perfectly well to London, where black citizens were experiencing untold levels of police brutality. The song stayed at the top of the British reggae charts for nearly six months and was named 'Reggae Single of the Year' by _Melody Maker_ and _Black Echoes._ 'Police And Thieves' was eventually covered by the Clash – a direct expression of the way the British punk movement was enthralled and influenced by the rebellious spirit and musical individuality of reggae.

Junior Murvin's acclaimed _Police And Thieves_ album was issued by Island in April 1977; much of the disc, and other recordings like 'Cool Out Son', a 1979 single cut for Joe Gibbs, made use of folk wisdom and parables. 'I never know that I was singing in proverbs until [disc jockey] Winston Barnes from RJR started calling me "Proverbs",' laughs the singer. 'That mean I'm spiritually oriented, along with the line of David.'

Although the British record companies were spreading reggae's liberating message, there was sometimes an artistic price to be paid. For instance, Virgin executives tried to steer the Mighty Diamonds into crossover territory, cutting the bland _Ice On Fire_ with Allen Toussant and American session players in New Orleans in 1977, followed by the marginally less commercial _Planet Earth._ As Tabby Diamond recalls, 'Virgin records, we sign up with them in 1975 with an album named _Right Time. Right Time_ was a four-track album, and them say them need a twenty-four-track recording now, and through we sign with the company we no have no option, so we went to Nassau, Compass Point studio. It finish decorating and fix up, but we were the first to start record in that studio, and we did an album named _Planet Earth. Planet Earth_ come in a different kind of vibes, 'nuff things a gwan with it through Virgin that we never bargain for. We have the blame, but the whole album was like a test. The company want a crossover thing, so there was a little bit of contrast with this album, but it was a good album too.'

Similarly, after the long gap caused by Leroy Sibbles' emigration to Canada the Heptones sprang back into action with the _Night Food_ album, released by Island in early 1976. The disc coupled classic singles from 1973-4 with recut Studio One material and other new songs, all cut at Harry J and assembled under the direction of Island staff member and music columnist Danny Holloway. Unfortunately the album was altered overseas, with cheesy overdubs and entirely gratuitous strings added to certain tracks. Though such elements were theoretically included to bolster mass-market appeal, the end result was something of a throwback to the late 1960s, when pop-oriented reggae had been given a philharmonic sprinkling in the hope of being played on _Top Of The Pops._ 'We have some good songs on that,' says Sibbles, 'but I don't like the production – it too watered-down. But I was willing to do whatever necessary to get a breakthrough. I was hoping that these guys know what they were doing and were capable of getting the right sound – I was hoping that they know what it took to break. But I was wrong again.' Their follow-up, _Party Time,_ was artistically more successful, created without foreign input in the rootsier confines of Lee Perry's Black Ark. However, its issue marked Sibbles' return to Canada and his ultimate departure from the group.

Some of the more challenging productions in this period were created by Geoffrey Chung for Tropical Sound Tracks, often in conjunction with Clive Hunt. Taking a break from a Tuff Gong session with Ivorian reggae star Alpha Blondy, Hunt says that the company had high-ranking connections. 'It was a communications office where they do advertisement for the government,' he explains, 'and they involved with the government. They hired people like Geoffrey Chung, Bob Andy and some of the guys from Corporation of Love – these were talented people who were university graduates on the edge of the music business. Geoffrey Chung tell them, "You need Clive Hunt", because me and Geoffrey Chung were always a team: in our day we used to be Psyche and Trim, Bonnie and Clyde.'

Like many of Jamaica's great horn players, Hunt first learned music in reform school. 'I born in 1952 in Linstead. My father was a cane farmer and herb farmer – he was a Rastaman. I have eighteen brothers and sisters, and I'm the oldest one. I was a real bad boy at school: they took me to family court and I end up going to Stony Hill Approved School, a reformatory. Everyone had to do a trade: I was a tailor, and I was at the top at thirteen. They said they was going to send me home for Christmas, but I felt really bad going back to the community 'cause you made your parents feel like you were a disgrace to the family, so I didn't feel good enough going home with the surprise of being the first in tailoring. With the music class I wasn't involved, but I stay outside and watched through the windows. One day I went inside to join. The teacher, Joe Moore, one of the man who involved with putting together the national anthem, he say, "Today is the day they are taking the test", so I start tell him all the notes, the bass and the treble clef, and the position of the trombone, fingering on the trumpet – I learn all of that from outside. We went and did the test, and I came first – went home with both prizes. When I came back, he decided that I wouldn't do anything else – no more tailoring, just music – and it's been like that from that day.'

Clive Hunt at work [David Katz]

When Hunt graduated from Story Hill, a lack of employment opportunities brought him to the army, and it was while he was a soldier that Hunt began recording. 'My first session, I was a trumpeter – Fabulous Five asked me to do something. The first real session I did was Lorna Bennett, that album she had, _Breakfast In Bed_ at Harry J Studio. I remember I thief some time and go over the fence, [as] the studio is next door to the military [camp], and Geoffrey Chung was [camp], and Geoffrey Chung was the one who asked me to come to play. I remember I was sitting and reading, I wasn't really interested – I didn't know that these were the top musicians in Jamaica. I was helping out the musicians, so they discovered that I knew a lot about music and asked me to come back the next day.'

Hunt's partnership with Chung shaped the most notable Sound Tracks material, including Max Romeo's challenging concept album _Revelation Time_ and the Abyssinians' debut album _Forward On To Zion;_ Hunt also arranged prime material for other producers. 'Whenever Geoffrey Chung would sign some project, I would get to do that,' he recalls. 'When I was working on Abyssinians, I was doing overdubs at Joe Gibbs' and it sounded so good that I just started to work a thing with Joe Gibbs. I said, "I work on your stuff and then you give me studio time." 'At the same time, Hunt was cutting solo work credited to Lizard, Azul or occasionally the In Crowd. 'Me is the first man in the studio making things by meself with no other musicians,' he boasts. 'Me did "Satta I", do everything almost, and "Milk And Honey Bees", me did all of that. The original credited In Crowd band, but it wasn't In Crowd band, it was just me.'

Hunt has a keen sense of musical arrangement as heard on the _Legendary Skatalites_ album (later reissued as _African Roots),_ a 1975 reunion set of Niyabinghi instrumentals instigated by Lloyd Brevett. 'I did work with an Australian group,' Brevett explains. 'Somebody send for them to work between the Glass Bucket Club and Myrtle Bank Hotel. Then I start a group with Ras Michael on vocals, but he take the tune there that we write, go behind our back and start to sell them, so I just back out and start my own thing. Then I make an LP out of that named _African Roots_ [with] Brother Jack, Sidney Wolfe, I Marts. I use them for the Rasta drum and it end up with Skatalites play on it: [Lester] Sterling, Tommy [McCook], Ernest Ranglin.' The album was cut between Lee Perry's Black Ark and Herman ChinLoy's recently opened twenty-four-track Aquarius studio in Half Way Tree.

Some of the most politically relevant music to benefit from the Geoffrey Chung/ Clive Hunt association came from Pablo Moses, a highly inspired singer with a wide range and very individual approach. 'From [when] I was a kid, singing was a thing that was in me,' he says after an enthusiastic Sunsplash performance. 'I was born [in] a little district in Manchester named Culloden in 1948. My mom was a singer in the church choir and I went to church choir too. My mom usually have a shop, and when I was five they put me on the counter to sing for everybody that come in. It just happened that from [when] I was a kid I can sing falsetto in a certain way, and I can sing natural baritone, so I usually practise along with Ray Charles and Sam Cooke. My mom migrated and I was raised by my aunt, and then my mom brought us out to Vineyard Town. I went to Excelsior High School on Mountain View Avenue, then I went to Jamaica School of Music for two and a half years, did guitar and theory of music'
