After an unsatisfactory period in singing groups, Pablo broke out as a solo artist with 'I Man A Grasshopper', an unusual number relating the conflict the singer experienced when smoking herb. 'Most of the guys I formed a group with, they don't want to stick to being original. Maybe they would make a quicker money [copying others], but I was telling them it's not just the money, you have to establish a foundation. All the groups got broken up, so I became a soloist. The name that them give me is Pablito Henry and you know that not an African name, so it couldn't be my name – it's a slave-master name, so that's why I call myself Pablo Moses.

'In 1975 I did my first song, "I Man A Grasshopper", at the Black Ark studio, with Lee Perry as the engineer and Geoffrey Chung as the producer. In Vineyard Town I met up with Geoffrey Chung's brother Mikey, he was the first one that introduce me to some chords on guitar. We usually hang out together and he was an accomplished musician, so being around him was like an honour. Seeing him playing, and listening to the music and observing how they do things, I just adopt those principles. Then I get introduced to Geoffrey Chung, we start to move together and he liked the song. He said, "This is something new, it's unique." Mikey Chung played rhythm guitar, Michael Murray from In Crowd played the lead guitar, Clive Hunt played bass, Robbie Lyn and Geoffrey Chung was on the keyboards. We had synthesizer, organ and piano. Everyone had a jam session – they all enjoy that type of chord change in that song, so immediately in the studio it was a buzz.'

The song made an impact on both sides of the Atlantic and led to the recording of further material. His exquisite debut album has a very particular sound, marked by rock guitar and synth lines and with echoes of jazz in its horns. 'Sound Tracks wanted an album, so I started to write songs. Every set of new chords that I learn I write new lyrics, and that's how the album _Revolutionary Dream_ came about. "We Should Be In Angola" was done at Aquarius studio, [and] we did the rest of the songs, "Give I Fe I Name" and "Blood Money", at Joe Gibbs' studio.'

Though such material was well received overseas, it was rockers and the sound of Channel One that really ruled Jamaica in the mid-seventies, reigning largely unchallenged in 1975 and early 1976. Unfortunately, a violent incident caused Channel One to lose its dominance temporarily. 'What really get us down is when my brother Paul got killed, that's '77,' says Joseph Hoo-Kim remorsefully. 'He usually sell motorcycles and parts from the same premises, and the Channel One sound system, he get involved with it. Kenneth, the next brother, he was involved in the motorcycle thing too. We was playing domino, three o'c lock in the morning, on the beach in Greenwich Town. It was a robbery – they rob him for his gun. I get a bit down when Paul got killed – we weren't doing anything for a long little while. That was the main thing why I came abroad, went to Brooklyn and opened a record store: J and L, at Utica Avenue, with a fellow by the name of Lowell.'

Back in Kingston, the next to lead the field was Joe Gibbs' sixteen-track studio in Retirement Crescent; Gibbs followed Channel One's lead by revamping Studio One and Treasure Isle rock steady rhythms for hits with a pool of singers and deejays and a series of sublime dub albums. Different sets of musicians, typically composed of members _of_ the Morwells and Soul Syndicate groups, built rhythms for Gibbs; similarities to Channel One were achieved by regulars such as Sly Dunbar and Ossie Hibbert, plus horn players such as Vin Gordon, Herman Marquis, Tommy McCook and Richard 'Dirty Harry' Hall. By late 1976 the Professionals were the in-house band, featuring Lloyd Parks on bass; on keyboards, Franklyn 'Bubbler' Waul diversified the sound with bubbling clavinet and synthesizer licks, augmented by players such as Errol 'Tarzan' Nelson. Gibbs' product was given international issue from 1977 on Lightning, a subsidiary of the Warner-Electric-Atlantic group; later material surfaced on Laser, another sub-label of WEA. Gibbs' dub albums, expertly mixed by Errol Thompson, were also highly popular in the UK. The Professionals' _African Dub Chapter Three_ was particularly successful: the first two chapters in the series were fairly sedate Soul Syndicate dubs, but the third had ringing doorbells, banging gongs and myriad other '3-D' effects.

The most exciting new vocal group to get their break at Joe Gibbs' was Culture, a trio that favoured religious material. Joseph Hill, the group's leader and composer of most of their material, uses his distinct soprano to shape lyrics in the vernacular of the countryside, the songs made fuller by the able harmony of the other two members. At a London hotel, following an inspirational performance, Hill has a refreshing enthusiasm, though one red, gold and green false tooth and a lazy left eye are visibly peculiar. Joseph says he got his start at Studio One. 'Myself was part of the band Soul Defenders,' he stresses, 'and in the early days of Freddy McKay I helped arrange a lot of his harmony, like "Picture On The Wall". I did a lot of percussion in Coxsone's studio as well, and I did my first song, "Behold". That was back in 1972, upwards to about '73. Three years went by, then I start doing my own thing.'

He formed Culture, originally known as the African Disciples, with his cousin, Albert Walker, and Kenneth Paley [aka Lloyd Dayes] in 1976. 'It was during the season of Easter,' Hill recalls. 'I used to play instruments in other bands on the North Coast, and I came home to catch a little rest. I was at my uncle's house and I saw Albert appear. He proposed, "Let us do something in the studio." That same Friday, we saw the politicians came with a band. I ask one of the [band] members what's happening in town, so I say, "I'm going to the studio on Monday morning." We went there and a lot of adventurous things take place, visible or invisible, wherever we went. It was a joy to be there, because we gain acceptance.'

**Joseph Hill of Culture [David Katz]**

Before the group's successful audition at Joe Gibbs', Hill was given a helping hand by Jach LloyedI buck up on people like Joseph Hill of Culture,' Lloyd proudly related. 'Him carry me way down in the bush and say, "Hear this, Jah Lloyd. Me can sing, you know." Him come a town for me to record him a couple times, at Chancery Lane corner where Gregory Isaac's shop is, and I financially embarrassed, couldn't afford to produce them, because the business taking up more cash. [Blacka] Morwell was up at Joe Gibbs' doing a thing for Gibbo, so I said, "Go to Morwell, and tell him Jah Lloyd sent you." When them go there, they wasn't named Culture, they was named something like the Israelites – it was four of them. When we select them, we lay the rhythms like "Jah Jah See Them A Come" on "State Of Emergency".'

The song to bring the group widespread attention – particularly among a punk audience when issued in Britain – was the apocalyptic "Two Sevens Clash", a song recounting Garvey's many prophecies. 'It was a Saturday morning,' Hill explains, 'when lightning and thunder, which is a natural disaster, chopped down a cotton tree right before the police station at Ferry. That was a bad sign, but they take no heed of brutality. Marcus Garvey was imprisoned at Spanish Town by the Jamaican government, and on his way out of the prison, when the sentence was over, he prophesised and said, "That gate that I walk through, no other one shall use it again." It has been paste up, locked, and if they wished to speak the truth about cell number eighteen in that prison, for a number of years it could not be locked, because that was the one that Marcus Garvey was in.'

Hill says he was inspired to sing about Garvey because the leader's message remains relevant. 'When they see their back is going up against the wall, that was the time we reminded them to remember what Marcus Garvey say,' he continues. 'That was the time they start getting awake. If I and Burning Spear and even Mighty Diamonds did not start making suggestion of Marcus Garvey, and keep making suggestion of Marcus Garvey, they would be there still sittin' on their same butts, making their same mistakes all over again.'

Along with Dennis Brown, Culture was one of the key acts that boosted the popularity of Joe Gibbs' stable; and although a number of other hits followed, such as the triumphant 'Natty Dread Taking Over' and faithful 'Zion Gate', their time with the producer was brief. By 1978 they had begun a relationship with Sonia Pottinger, who took over Treasure Isle on Duke Reid's death in September 1976 following a long battle with cancer. The resultant material, including songs first recorded at Joe Gibbs' and cut afresh at Treasure Isle, was issued abroad on Front Line, Virgin's reggae subsidiary.

**Lead Twinkle Norman Grant [David Katz]**

Much of the Front Line roster was chosen by Johnny 'Rotten' Lydon, who flew to Kingston in February 1978 after leaving the Sex Pistols. One of the most important acts to sign to the lable before Lydon's involvement was the Twinkle Brothers, a group from the dusty country town of Falmouth east of Montego Bay. The group began as a vocal duo in the ska years. 'My brother Ralston and myself, from '62, we actually got the name,' says lead singer Norman Grant, taking a break from rehearsals at a dingy South London studio. 'We make our instrument out of cans and fishing line, and people always gather around and listen, so one night a Rastaman named So-Mi-Say say, "I'm going to give you guys a name – the Twinkle Brothers." It was in the evening, so I guess he see the stars. When Jamaica got independence, they came with this pop and mento Festival song contest, so we enter at our parish level and won every year from '62 to '68.'

'We are from the ghetto area of Falmouth,' adds the soft-spoken Ralston, the harmony specialist of the group, 'and we started very young: Norman was about six, I was about eight. Inna them early days, we tune into the American music on the radio like Otis Redding, James Brown, Aretha Franklin. We get a little from the R&B, blend it with a spiritual, gospel thing.'

'We usually took part in church concerts,' continues Norman, who has something of a cockney accent after twenty-five years in London. 'We used to go to the Anglican and other churches. You had the Pocomania church in the week where they play drums.'

**Ralston Grant:**

**Twinkles harmony specialist [David Katz]**

From 1960, when he was ten, Norman was performing in North Coast hotel bands such as Schubert and the Miracles and the Cardinals; he cut his debut single in 1964. 'My aunt was in Kingston, close to the university hospital. She said, "You guys are talented, come to Kingston and go to the studios." But it wasn't easy, 'cause it's far. Me and my brother were going to Prince Buster, Treasure Isle, JJ, when we get school holidays. The day we went to Coxsone they were just packing up – they said, "Youth, if you guys came earlier, we would a record you." Then my brother went away to a camp where they teach you skills, so I went to Kingston on my own and made my first record at fourteen for Leslie Kong, "Somebody Please Help me".'

'My mother is a higgler [market trador or street vendor],' adds Ralston, 'so that day, Norman went to Kingston to buy some stuff for her to sell and went to Beverley's , and Roland Alphonso tell Beverley's , "This youth have a nice tune." Then we bring in another guy that live in the same yard with us, Eric Bernard, and go on for a while. Eric went to Montego Bay, and we bring in my brother-in-law, Karl Hyatt, then we do the first group song on the Treasure Isle label with Duke Reid.' The song, 'Matthew Mark', made reference to the apostles.

'From [when] I was sixteen, I was putting on stage shows,' Norman adds, 'bringing down all the top guys from Kingston: Desmond Dekker and the Aces, John Holt, Ken Boothe.' Both Norman and Ralston gained further live experience working in the North Coast hotel scene.

In the same period, the Twinkle Brothers were augmented by the various additions of Karl Hyatt, Eric Bernard and Derrick Brown. 'When one guy wasn't around, we'd use another,' Norman notes, 'so it was from two to three, then to four.' Meanwhile, the group progressed to higher levels of the Festival contest. 'In '68 we won a gold medal in the group section, and I won a gold medal as a solo artist. In '69 we won again, and 1970 we took part in the professional side and came third, got a bronze medal, with "You Can Do It Too". Being in the Festival, we were getting more exposed, so we met up with Bunny Lee. He recorded the Festival song and "It's Not Who You Know", "Miss Labba Labba", "Sweet Young Thing", "Miss World", "Too Late". Soul Syndicate, the first session they played on as a band, it was Twinkle Brothers they backed. We also do some songs for Phil Pratt, Sunshot label, like "Do Your Own Thing". "No Big Thing" was a hit in Jamaica at the time.'

By 1973, the Twinkle Brothers _were_ a fully self-contained unit. 'Norman said, "This group is too big, we have to learn to play some instruments,"' Ralston laughs. 'So he buy some second-hand.' Ralston handled guitar, Eric Bernard played keyboards, Derrick Brown played bass and Karl Hyatt was on percussion.

'Nobody would play the drums,' Norman chuckles, 'so I end up playing them. Norman continued to be employed by hotel bands and toured Mexico and Guatemala with Sonny Bradshaw in 1975, but became chiefly concerned with Twinkle self-productions. 'We were recording with other producers, but about '74, '75, I stop singing for other people, just deal with the Twinkle. That's where we came with the first album, _Rasta 'Pon Top.'_

_Rasta 'Pon Top_ addressed liberation struggles and the African identity of Rastafari, a notable shift in the group's focus. 'All over the world, there were changes,' says Norman. 'You have groups like the Temptations singing some heavy lyrics, so myself as a writer, I decided I'm going to do things like that. Plus, with humanity, I sing what I believe. It's not even a matter of the sales, 'cause the music help us also to see and take us around different people, and you hear of countries that you thought weren't even in the world. When I was growing up, the spiritual part of the Bible tell me about Jerusalem, and I used to believe it was somewhere in the sky. But being in music opened my eyes very early to know that these things was on earth, that it wasn't a dream thing,'

'Most of our songs say something about Africa,' Ralston adds. 'We have the love side too, but we'r e more into the deeper roots, what's going on around the world. Things that we feel from the ghetto, that's what we put out. We like to go in some chords that nobody else touch – that's how we feel it. We'r e from the countryside and we feel it more spiritual. We are not the city person, we are more laid back and with God.'

With the band now on solid footing, Norman opened a record shop in Falmouth's Water Square and began producing material with local talent. 'One of the guy in the band was a cabinetmaker. He build me a shop – the little shop with the big stock. When we started producing ourselves, if we have like five songs for the Twinkle Brothers to record on a session, I would carry like five other artists, and record one-one song with them.'

_Rasta 'Pon Top_ and these accompanying singles were issued in Britain on Grounation, a roots subsidiary of Vulcan records. 'I was putting out lots of stuff in Jamaica on my label,' Norman recalls, 'and it was very hard. We were doing everything ourselves, driving round the island. Every other week you go to a shop and they tell you, "We have that one. You have a new one?" I couldn't be putting out new songs that often, so I say I have to find a new market. A lawyer friend of mine was going to England and he said, "You could stay where's I'm staying", so that's how I came. I went to Vulcan and they wras interested in all those material.'

When Vulcan folded about 1977, executive Chips Richards set up Carib Gems with former Pama employee Joe Farquarson and his colleague Adrian Sherwood, who had previously run the J&A distribution service together. Sherwood would shortly go into record production, notably working with Prince Fari on his splendid _Message From The King_ set and mixing the _Cry Tough Dub Encounter_ series. The Twinkle Brothers' second album, a less-focussed set called _Do Your Own Thing,_ was licensed to Carib Gems, and a couple of singles, including a recut of 'It's Not Who You Know', surfaced on the Tops label, based at a record shop in Brixton's Acre Lane.

The disc that gave them the broader recognition they deserved was _Love,_ initially issued as a ten-inch mini-LP on Virgin Front Line; half the material was cut with Sly and the Revolutionaries at Channel One. 'The ending of '77, I did a session in Jamaica and came up with five songs. I went to Island and they told me to come back in two weeks, but them time I come to England only for like four weeks, so you have to do things quick. Chips Richards told me that Virgin is looking [for] artists, so I went down there with "Solid As A Rock", "Free Africa", "Watch The Hypocrites" and the version to "Free Africa" with Sir Lee. I had this other track, "I Love You So", which I recorded at Treasure Isle studio, but I didn't think it was up to standard because the volume was very low. When I play it to them, they say "Yeah, we like that", so they got the six tracks and they came out with it as a ten-inch – it have a different look in the shops.'

The strong harmony arrangements and pertinent message of _Love_ brought the Twinkle Brothers into the overseas major league; at home in Jamaica, they continued to run their grassroots record shop and label in Falmouth. Similarly, though Dennis Brown's material for Joe Gibbs was given major-label promotion from 1978, the singer remained strongly aligned with his core roots audience on self-produced work. Brown also played a major role in raising the profile of Junior Delgado, an old friend whose deep, rough voice carries spine-tingling portents of anguish. 'I know Dennis Brown from I was a kid,' the burly singer chuckles. 'I and D. Brown have around two decades tied up in music, because Dennis Brown was like a whiz kid. Everybody used to love him – they even used to love him more than Bob Marley.'

Delgado was born in Kingston in 1954: '48½ Luke Lane, I grew up there until the city was getting combustible and my people move out to Rockfort. The first time I was singing is at Ward Theatre. Me and my mother went to watch the movies, and I went up on the stage and they throw money at me. My mother was a dressmaker, a merchant tailor also. My dad used to work on tower cranes and heavy-duty equipment like JCBs and E25 Caterpillar. It's really singing [that] take me away from that area.'

His career started with the group Time Unlimited. 'I and Orville Smith and Junior Marshall was Time Unlimited, but this guy that was bigger than us, Glasford Manning, he used to sing for Coxsone Downbeat and he used to live over in Trench Town, Ninth Street. Over Trench Town, they was evacuating some people to Callalloo Bed, near to [Washington] Boulevard. We meet up Glasford Manning in that swamp over there with a lot of mosquitoes. We were rehearsing and Trevor Douglas take the group to Scratch to do an audition. Scratch says, "We have to record him right now." He wanted to do it right there, same night, because he's a genius – don't let them tell you nothing, that man is a genius.'

A few crucial nuggets surfaced from these early sessions: 'He and Joe Gibbs release "Reaction" on a label called Reflection. "African Sound", Glasford Manning was doing lead, and "23rd Psalms", I was doing the lead.' Because Scratch held back additional material, the group cut sides for Rupie Edwards as the Heaven Singers; they then cut some tunes for Tommy Cowan that remains unreleased.

In 1975 Delgado went solo, recutting 'Reaction' for Larry Lawrence with the Wailers band at Harry J; this was followed by 'Thinking' and 'Every Natty' for Niney the Observer, which brought him back into close contact with Dennis Brown. 'Niney used to have us in his hand, me and D. Brown. We lived together, move up and down together. Me and Dennis Brown went away and recorded [a version of the Heptones'] "Party Time". He did put it out on DEB first and we did sell forty thousand out of a car back, just like that. We start making songs like "Trickster". A lot of hits keep coming.'

The earliest Delgado hit to surface on DEB Music was 'Tition', a critique of politicians set to an infectious rhythm. 'It was me and Chinna who do that song in Channel One studio. Chinna was playing bass, Keith Sterling was playing keyboards and Santa Davis was on drums – only three instruments.' A later hit, 'Devil's Throne', was subsequently licensed to Joe Gibbs. 'Those tunes was done in Channel One,' Junior recalls. 'It was Ossie Hibbert done that song with me. He gave it to Joe Gibbs and they gave him a car, a Ford Cortina L.'

Junior's debut album, the 1978 set _Taste Of The Young Heart,_ was highly popular in Jamaica and the UK. Tastefully assembled by Dennis Brown, the album was issued on DEB Music and distributed in the UK by EMI; one of its outstanding tracks is the fearsome 'Storm Is Coming', an apocalyptic number produced by Augustus Pablo. 'I and Pablo go back from kid days, like Dennis Brown,' Delgado reminisces. 'We didn't come in this business as big people. I remember when we used to go to a club to sing, they were hiding us from the naked women because we were little.'

While DEB Music helped Delgado's star to rise, Augustus Pablo was gaining international prestige through the overseas issue of ethereal productions on the Hawkeye label. Some of the product had been recorded several years earlier. 'Everything in Pablo's camp happens very slowly,' says Carlton Hines, founder of the group Tetrack. 'Money was always in short supply.' Though only a backing vocalist in the group, Hines was the writer and arranger of their material; he is a down-to-earth individual who now runs a restaurant in Queens, New York. Over an excellent lunch of steamed fish, Hines recalls the group's rise: 'I spent my formative years in East Kingston. In Mountain View there was a sound system called Tetrack, run by a guy called Owen Archer. Tetrack is really the title of a provincial ruler in the Roman Empire. That set was one of the baddest sets in the East – many artists would come by and leave dub plates. With Paul Mangaroo and Dave Harvey, we form a group as Tetrack. They were guys in the neighbourhood – we used to meet under a mango tree. A guy called Denzil would sit in on the rehearsals sometimes – he used to go to KC with Pablo. He said, "I'l l introduce you to Pablo", and we were in awe of Pablo because of "Java". The group started '72, so we had been rehearsing for almost two years. We went uptown, check out Pablo and discover Pablo always wanted a group, because Pablo love group singing. We went to a place called Black Ants Lane, off Red Hills Road – a have-not community. Pablo's playing some tracks and he picked up "Let's Get Together". I forgot the name of the group, but it was an obscure [foreign] song, and he gave it to us to rehearse.'

This debut recording was executed at the Black Ark with the assistance of Lee 'Scratch' Perry. 'When we went into Black Ark studio, it's like three scaredy-cats – they had to turn off the lights in the studio. We ended up positioning ourselves in such a way that Scratch could not see anybody's face. It's '74, probably about August. A few months afterwards, it came out.'

Tetrack came to the attention of the outside world a few years later, when Hawkeye picked up an original number called 'Let's Get Started'; the label later issued a remixed 'Let's Get Together' on a disco with Jacob Miller's 'Each One Teach One'. As Hines recalls, 'We did a couple of other nondescript songs, then we did "Let's Get Started". That song did not include all three Tetracks, because Dave wasn't there. We went to Tubby's to record that, and a number of Rockers people were to record that day, like Ricky Grant, Locksley Castell, Hugh Mundell. Pat Kelly happened to come along and Pablo said, "Come sing a harmony". We look in _Black Echoes_ and saw it at number ten. When I looked back later it was at number one, so Pablo said, "Let's do an album", but because of finances the album came much later than it should.'

The bulk of Augustus Pablo's work never made it on to a major overseas label, but it is among the most moving and individually spiritual music ever recorded. The Phase One catalogue, which also remained outside the majors, was likewise composed of enthralling roots material. Roy Francis, the label's founder, is on the balcony of Mixing Lab, the perpetually buzzing studio he currently runs near Half Way Tree. Despite the hectic atmosphere, with his two cell phones constantly ringing, the mustachioed Francis is relaxed, cheerful and very accommodating. 'I was born in Trench Town, 1945,' he says, his elongated vowels betraying the many years he spent in New York. 'My dad used to sell things on the street like mangoes – all kind of hustling he used to do. My mom used to sell downtown on the sidewalk. When I was around ten I went to Spanish Town, and I go to America when I was around seventeen. My dad went on farm work, run off and hook up with some woman, get his green card and sponsor me up there in Brooklyn. I go to Peterborough School of Aeronautics in New Jersey. I'm a certified airline mechanic, work for Delta and Pan Am.'

Though he lived in Brooklyn Francis'j ob enabled him to make frequent visits to Jamaica, where he went into record production in 1977. 'As I used to work with the airline and fly free, I used to [be] in Jamaica every week. I met this guy named Derrick Smith, his alias is Gurr. He used to work for Jimmy Radway, selling his records. I was fooling around with his cousin, so he say, "You want to invest in some music?" I decide I'm going to put some money into it, so he bring this group to me named the Chantells, tell me that they'r e wicked singers. They record a couple songs with Duke Reid before I met them. I don't know nothing about producing, so Gun-was the one. We went to Randy's downtown – he do the rhythm, produced four tracks with the group: "Children Of Jah", "Natty Supper", "Blood River" and "Stumbling Block", with Horsemouth Wallace, Robbie Shakespeare, Ansel Collins. How I get the name Phase One, I copy it from Channel One – Channel _One_ at those times was having nothing but big hits.'

Phase One rapidly became a label noted for its quality, with the work of the Chantells outstanding. The group styled themselves after the soul acts of Philadelphia and Chicago, with lead singer Samuel Bramwell's soaring falsetto backed by responsive harmonies from Tommy Thomas and Lloyd Forrest, but much of their repertoire dealt with Rastafarian retribution, as is evident from the titles named above, which Francis says initially failed to hit. 'They wasn't being successful,' he recalls. 'I left Gurr in Jamaica to be doing the business and I went back in the States. The first four recordings sold maybe a thousand, two thousand, and that was like a drop in the bucket – I barely make back my money. Those days you could sell thirty to forty thousand records in Jamaica as a hit song, because then you didn't have much cassette player, no CDs, so eveiybody have a little turntable in their house. It's not like now, when a hit song in Jamaica is maybe two thousand.'

It was the Chantells' 'Waiting In The Park' that established the group and the Phase One label in 1978. 'That's when me and Gurr break off and I said I'm going to do this on my own,' Francis explains. 'We went to Joe Gibbs' and did two songs, "Waiting In The Park" and "Man In Love", and "Waiting In The Park" was a huge success. The group was really big in England. I fly to London and stay at my sister's house in Walthamstow, and Pama [back in business since 1977 as the Jet Star Phonographic distribution company] used to send a taxi to my house to buy those records. Then I was spending so much time in Jamaica I get fired from my job, but the job was only paying me like five hundred a week, and I could make that in a hour, so I didn't mind losing my job – the only thing I miss is my flying benefits. I decide I'm going to be a producer full time, and I start recording all kind of tracks by Keith Boswell and Lopez Walker. I was at Channel One doing sessions and saw [Walker] standing outside – he say he want to do something for me, start sing a song by the name of "Jah Jah New Garden". I take him right in the studio and record the rhythm, voice him a week after and put it out. He was a baldhead and then he turn Rasta, start to come to me with some story, so I didn't bother with him no more.'

'Jah Jah New Garden', cut in the vibrant, danceable style known as 'steppers', was a vision of Rastafarian Utopia. Francis also used the rhythm for a fine deejay cut by a baldhead known as Jah Berry, another artist from his growing stable of unknowns. 'I met him at Joe Gibbs' when we do "WTaiting In The Park", says Francis. 'He's a guy who come from somewhere down Maxfield Avenue. I had other artists like the Terrors, Leroy King [who] did a song named "Mash Down Babylon", the Untouchables [with] "Sea Of Love" [a cover of the Twillights' Cajun pop song, previously cut in reggae by the Heptones], and I dig out some of the wickedest rhythm I had [for] _Phase One Dubwise.'_

Though Francis allowed a second dub album and some singles to be issued by an associate in the UK, he essentially kept Phase One as an independent label, resisting offers from established reggae companies which wanted to license his material. 'When I come to London,' he proudly recalls, 'Greensleeves and Trojan, all these guys looking for me.' Unfortunately, the untimely demise of the Chantells led Francis to leave the music business for a considerable time. 'Some company in England wanted them on a tour, singing some little clubs like Palm Beach. I don't know who give them some weed, but the three of them went to jail. After they got bail, they had to stay in the country until they went on trial. Two of them got away and one of them do time. Because they were my main group and I was feeling so goddam disgusted over what happened, my whole vibes cut: I decide I'm not going to mess around with this damn recording no more. I went to America, working for this firm that build aeroplane parts. I left my tapes in a friend's basement with my records, and the guy's basement flood out.'

Another entirely independent label issuing quality roots was JA Man, a West Kingston outfit formed by Dudley 'Manzie' Swaby and Leroy Hollett. 'The record thing started about '73-'74,' says the dreadlocked Manzie at Capo studio in Flatbush, 'but I really influenced from youth days. The school that we used to go to was right behind Studio One, so I used to go inna the studio before Mr Dodd come run we out. Don Drummond's mother used to live inna the same yard 'pon Love Lane with my grandmother, so more time him used to come round with him horn. '73, me live round a Craig Town, and Junior Byles used to live up the road, right near to gully bank, so more time we used to [go] down there and smoke herb, play a little guitar, until we just say, "Cho, let's go do two tune." Blacka Morwell a the first man that carry me down at Randy's , through him used to know the runnings. The first tune that me do was "See The Dread There" with Rupert Reid – he used to there inna the yard with me. The next tune was a version of "Drum Song" with Bobby Ellis. We do four tune, but me just give Morwell two: "Bit By Bit", and [I] don't remember the next one. Jah Whoosh used to deejay 'pon one sound over Jones Town, so Jah Whoosh did a tune named "Free Up Me Ganja" and me get a fight fi that tune because of the lyrics: "Babylon sell it, the lawyer love it, the doctor recommend it", all long before Peter Tosh do fi him tune ["Legalize It"]. "Take A Set", that was the biggest seller that come out of that session.'

In late 1975 the JA Man base of operations moved from Manzie's Love Lane home to 129 Orange Street, site of the House of Music record shop. By then, the outfit had already recorded 'Remember Me' with Junior Byles, the first of a handful of prime singles cut before the singer succumbed to mental illness. 'Then me no do no more tune a Randy's again,' Manzie recalls. 'Me gone a Channel One and do over "Movie Star" with Hortense Ellis as "Super Star", and do "Remember Me" and "Chant Down Babylon" with Junior Byles. When Junior start get kinky is really the tune named "Pitchy Patchy" – that's a little after him do "Fade Away" [an outstanding ode to Rastafari cut for Channel One].'

One of the label's finest singles is Dave Robinson's 'My Homeland', given an optimistic backing by the Revolutionaries. 'Dave Robinson and D. Brown used to live in a big yard together, so all the while me a say, "D. Brown, let me do two tune". It's really him who say, "Do two tune with Dave."'

After cutting further works with various singers and deejays, JA Man issued an untitled dub album in 1977. Dudley Swaby subsequently set up the Manzie label on Slipe Road in 1978, leaving the House of Music to became the Roots and Roots Shoe House, a new base of operations for Doctor Alimantado.

One of the most commercially successful local releases of 1977-8 was _Soul Reggae,_ the debut album by Beres Hammond, then lead singer with Zap Pow. _Soul Reggae_ was the first hit album to surface from Aquarius studio, but the disc was never issued overseas; ironically, it was the esoteric Zap Pow which had the benefit of a contract with Island. Hammond is at present one of the most in-demand of reggae singers, and his sensitive musical arrangements have also earned him widespread respect as a producer. In the isolated environs of Stony Hill, Beres leaves a session at his Sileckshan studio to make sure his guest is comfortable in the shade; after offering some freshly cut sugar cane, he relates the details of his long and varied career.

**Beres Hammond [David Katz]**

'I was born in a little community they call Annotto Bay. My father was a councillor for the area, a politician [for] the JLP. Those days politics was much better in Jamaica – they never used to have these guns. My mother, I never knew her as anything else but a person being around the house, taking care of all of us, because she had ten children. From [when] I was about nine, I used to come to Kingston on the weekends. I had sisters here, scattered all over, so I used to just go between all of them. Then I took the opportunity to go downtown and see the different record stores and studios. The voice that I really used to like was Alton Ellis and Ken Boothe – I thought they were exceptional. The Heptones, Melodians, I used to love their harmonies. On the radio stations here we were flooded with foreign music: the Beatles, Engelbert Humperdinck and Gilbert O'Sullivan, and I used to try to imitate them. Marvin Gaye, he stood out in my mind. Stevie Wonder, I thought he was brilliant. My American inspiration was shared too between Sam Cooke and Otis Redding. I used them to test out how well I could sing.'

Beres moved to Kingston full-time 'in my last year in primary school. I was doing the same juggling between the sisters, because then the dollars was very few.' His singing career began in the capital city: 'I did one recording when I was about eleven or twelve with Clancy Eccles ["Wanderer"], because Clancy was a friend of the family, but it was never released until a few years ago. One day when I was passing on Eastwood Park Road, an audition was taking place: Merritone, he was running a amateur talent series, and my brethren forced me to write my name down. I did four ballads and he asked me if I know any reggae, and at the time Delroy Wilson had "Rain From The Skies". I did that one and he said, "Yeah, everything all right." Wednesday night, the crowd was with me – this was VIP, a club in Half Way Tree. The response was overwhelming.'

About two years later, in 1975, Beres Hammond was drawn into Zap Pow. 'I was performing as a solo person, still doing amateur shows, and then there was Cynthia Schloss. We teamed up as a duet, went right up to Jamaica House quite often. I was also a member of a group called Tuesday's Children" One night, when the group got a gig in one of the hotels, Zap Pow was the backing band. After the show Dwight checked me and said, "Youth, you can sing. Come join we." '

Beres' rich timbre and expressive delivery brought Zap Pow a new scope, freeing its members to concentrate on their instruments and injecting an essence of soul into the group's intriguing combination of roots reggae and jazz. His talents helped the band score an album contract with Island in 1978, but he left the group the following year after experiencing greater success with his solo Aquarius disc. 'I wanted to get a feel of what outside of Jamaica was, so Zap Pow provided that. We went up to US and Canada, so I was with them from '75 until '79. Chris Blackwell signed us, but I thought if we got signed we'd be on billboards. I thought we would have been in Australia and New Zealand, Malaysia, but that didn't work out. So that's when I thought to myself that I should be doing something else, so I started working with [guitarist and arranger] Willie Lindo. Zap Pow was some political motivated kind of music – Zap Pow would just tell it like it is, different issues from my love thing. I had some songs that was burning up inside me, which was more a line between people – a family type of thing.

'When my album came out, it took less than a month for it to blow out all over the place. I released a single, "One Step Ahead" – it went like a bolt to number one, stayed there for three and a half months, stayed on the chart itself for maybe two years. We figured we would work with Aquarius because Herman Chin-Loy was always down our necks, plus he had a big studio there, not doing anything, because there was no hit songs coming from there – it was kind of a big graveyard, so working by Aquarius we would be undisturbed.'

'When I was in the studio, I didn't even want a person to come in and peek or walk through the door,' confirms Herman Chin-Loy. 'It breaks your concentration.' Herman also recalls that his studio was often used for radio advertising. 'A lot of commercials was done there by Boris Gardiner and Peter Ashbourne. Fabulous Five had a thing with my brother going on there.' He agrees that the studio lacked a roots sound, but says this was not his intention. 'I always wanted a roots sound – it's just that the studio was not capable. It had a better quality in terms of the top end, like for voicing, but for the bass and drum, which I really needed – because bass and drum is reggae to me – it didn't have it, no doubt about that. That's the reason I went down to Channel One and did some bass and drum, and came back to my studio and finished [it].'

Though Aquarius may not have achieved the ghetto bass-and-drum sound, as the only twenty-four-track studio on the island it was the perfect setting for the complex, big-band brass arrangements of Cedric Brooks' _United Africa._ Brooks' multi-layered score brought instrumental reggae to another level in 1978. Likewise, Rico's great _Man From Wareika_ album, recorded at Joe Gibbs', Randy's and Island's Hammersmith studio under Karl Pitterson's direction, was such a well-constructed set of reggae jazz that the esteemed Blue Note label licensed it from Island in 1977.

'I would say that was the first album I was able to perform to the best of my ability,' says Rico happily. '[Pitterson] got the best musicians to do this album. In Jamaica, we had Robbie and Sly and Ansel Collins. The first things that came to me were the things that I family used to play in Wareika Hills, with Count Ossie and Don Drummond. I'd say that was my first album with a lot of jazz in it, and creative elements. If you notice, I don't solo a lot – I put more melodies into the thing than solos.' One of the album's outstanding tracks was 'Africa', cut with session players in London. Its rhythm was also used by Ijahman Levi for a song on his acclaimed Island debut, _Haile I Hymn._

Blue Note was then a subsidiary of United Artists, who made further tenuous dips into the reggae market with material acquired from secondary sources. First, the company took over the catalogue of Tropical Sound Tracks after that label went bust. The result was the haphazard 'Anthology _Of_ Reggae' series, issued in 1978; these ranged from the concept albums co-produced by Clive Hunt and Geoffrey Chung to older Studio One compilations. The Magnum label, which issued reggae in the UK from 1977, was connected to Pye (though initially aligned with Philips); in 1978 it was relaunched as Ballistic, another subsidiary of United Artists. The material on Magnum and Ballistic was licensed by Mo Claridge, who had broken away from Creole records (established as a branch of EMI in 1971) to form the Mojo distribution company. To start with, Claridge issued singles by artists such as the Meditations on an independent label called Bam Bam; concurrent with Ballistic, he also issued roots material on Warrior.

**Roy Cousins in London [Dave Hendley]**

The first group behind which Claridge put significant promotion was the Royals, with a compilation album called _Pick Up The Pieces;_ according to Roy Cousins, Claridge acquired this material from Militant Barry, a deejay who worked with Keith Hudson and ran the Kiss record label in London, but Barry himself had received the material from a secondary source. As Cousins explains, 'When I was living at Grass Quit Glade [in Cockburn Pen], this guy David Mohammed's grandfather was the owner of the yard. He came up to England and become my link – I send the tape to him, and him took the tape to Militant Barry, and Barry take the album to Mojo.' After Cousins presented himself in England, Claridge cut out the middlemen and further material was licensed directly. 'When I come it was Pye who had _[Pick Up The Pieces];_ I went back to Jamaica and send up _Ten Years After._ I was closely associated with [South London sound system operator] Lloyd Coxsone, so Coxsone get a whole lot of dubs from _Ten Years After,_ play it all round London. John Telford was Mojo's publisher, so through Telford he set up a deal with United Artists. They send a first-class ticket and I bring up _Israel Be Wise._ United Artists take _Freedom Fighters Dub, Liberation Dub;_ them take over _Ten Years After and. Pick Up The Pieces'_

'Conscious Man', a radio-friendly song of romantic conduct recorded at Lee Perry's Black Ark by harmony trio the Jolly Brothers, first surfaced in 1977 on Seven Leaves, a label founded by Jamaican expatriate Tony Owens; Mo Claridge quickly licensed the song from Owens and issued it on Magnum. When re-released on Ballistic in 1978 it turned out to be the label's biggest success, skirting the lower regions of the UK pop charts and hitting further in Europe when issued on an extended 'disco mix' EP by United Artists the following year. Unfortunately, the anticipated Jolly Brothers album did not live up to expectations. After Roy Cousins introduced Claridge to the group, the uneven _Consciousness_ was recorded with Prince Jammy; further Black Ark material cut for Tony Owens, which retained a similar feel to the hit single, was kept unissued for many years following protracted disputes with Ballistic.

At the Black Ark, Lee Perry was inches away from toppling into an abyss: though the popularity of _Police And Thieves_ was matched by that of the psychedelic soul of George Faith's _To Be A Lover,_ by the end of 1978 Scratch and Island were in conflict over Blackwell's refusal to issue _Heart Of The Congos,_ the stunning debut album by spiritual harmony group the Congos. Island also failed to issue two of Perry's most adventurous self-produced albums, an experimental set by two Congolese singers and a pop-reggae album by Candy McKenzie, plus early work by deejay Mikey Dread and singer Earl Sixteen. After succumbing to various financial and personal pressures Perry decided to concentrate on an individual spiritual quest, and by early 1979 had shut the doors of his studio.

The Congos' lead vocalist, Cedric Myton, was also involved in Prince Lincoln Thompson's Royal Rasses, another group that scored a contract with Ballistic through connections with Roy Cousins. Thompson and Myton first sang together in the Tartans with Devon Russell and Lindburgh 'Preps' Lewis. The group scored big with the rock steady hit 'Dance All Night' for Federal, moved on to work for Caltone and Treasure Isle, and cut some self-produced numbers at Studio One before pursuing solo careers. By 1975 Thompson had formed the Royal Rasses, with harmony provided by Keith Peterkin and Clinton Hall, ghetto toughs known as 'Cap' and 'Johnny Kool'; Jennifer Lara also occasionally contributed. The group's piercing harmony, led by Thompson's anguished voice, caused interest in British reggae circles when singles such as the uplifting 'Love The Way It Should Be' and haunting 'Kingston 11' were issued in small quantity by London sound system operator Neville King.

**Prince Lincoln [Dave Hendley]**

As is alluded to on 'Kingston 11', which speaks of the harsh reality of the ghettos that make up West Kingston, Roy Cousins recalls that such sweet harmony was fostered in conditions of extreme hardship, where toughness becomes essential to survival. 'Prince Lincoln was a member of the Pigeon gang, like Devon Russell. Cedric was a saint out of the four of them. Prince pass his scholarship and went to Excelsior School, which is a top school in Jamaica, but because him always have him knife, he was expelled. Him mother kick him out of the house, so Prince Lincoln was living down Hunts Bay Road in a likkle board shack, planting greens for a living, never wear shirt nor shoes – when I take Mojo down there, him never have on a shirt. Prince did have the _Humanity_ album them time, produced by Errol Thompson, Marcia Griffiths' baby father, a deejay at JBC

Given major promotion, _Humanity_ was a sweeping success. As with the Royals' material, Brixton-based sound system Coxsone Outernational stoked up interest among the black population, whilst quality album packaging and a major 1979 European tour won progressive student fans. Unfortunately, subsequent albums aimed at the crossover market failed to reach either audience, signalling the demise of Ballistic in the early 1980s.

In an effort to match the super-stardom of Bob Marley, the major overseas promotion of singers and vocal groups such as Prince Lincoln, the Gladiators, the Mighty Diamonds, Culture and Dennis Brown brought the reggae message to increasingly wider audiences. Concurrently, though typically less polished, the hottest deejays of the period were also playing a crucial role in the international acceptance of Jamaican music.

**Watering the garden: Tappa Zukie [Dave Hendley]**
**CHAPTER TEN**

Oh Lord: Deejays and the International Pinnacle of Roots

_Trench Town, that's where Jah pick me and put me, so that's why when I sign up with Virgin I build a community centre and that Golden Age home, to give back to the people and show my appreciation to them... a lot of things could have happened to me, but God take care of my reward._

Tappa Zukie

_I become like a overnight hero... because reggae was not regarded as no substantial or enlightening art form._

Mikey Dread

IN THE LATE 1970s the deejay retained a crucial role in the international dissemination of reggae music. As the roots style continued to make an indelible impact overseas, deejays were accorded a particularly high status in Britain, where major labels helped the form to reach strong levels of popularity.

It was Virgin that ultimately had the greatest success with deejays, but by the time they tried to get their hands on Doctor Alimantado the toaster had largely reinvented himself as a singer. In late 1976 Alimantado gave a dynamic performance to a packed crowd at Kingston's Ward Theatre; he was set to repeat the event at the Carib on Boxing Day when disaster came in the form of a speeding bus, which nearly killed the toaster in a downtown Kingston street. Alimantado recalls the circumstances with a visible shudder: 'It was a very traumatic time in my life, when many people start to realize that what I was saying all these years is something that mean a lot to the world, so everybody seems to be zooming in on me, trying to take a bit. I was supposed to do a rehearsal for my first show at the Carib, and my friend invite me to the sea that morning and I went in the water and nearly drowned. I took in a lot of water - someone had to stand on my stomach and pump it out. I leave the sea, and I was going from here to the road there and the bus hit me down. I believe it wasn't an accident. Because my dread was flying at the time, I believe that's why the guy just swing around and hit me. The bus run me over and some people have to be shouting, go in front of the bus and stop the bus. In the hospital, a few things happen to me like food poisoning. My recuperation stage was very, very traumatic'

Fortunately, these traumas would bear wonderful fruit in the form of Tado's biggest hit, the life-affirming 'Born For A Purpose', which he sang, and its attendant toast, 'Life All Over'. 'When I came out of the hospital, I was lying on my bed at home and an idea came to me. I started to sing it and I needed to get a paper and pen to write it down, but I wasn't able to move and there was nobody else in the house. I pull myself off the bed, which was very painful, and get on my knees – you don't know the beauty of your foot until you'r e in that stage. It took me nearly half an hour to get a pen and paper, and I didn't knowT how to write music, but I tried to write it so that I know to how fall into a rhythm pattern so I could get the remembrance of it. When I was able to walk on crutches, I went to the studio and said to the musicians, "I'v e got this song to do and I haven't got any money." They say, "Cho, come man, no worry." Joseph Hoo-Kim said, "You working in the studio all the while. Just come." Really and truly, I didn't pay any money to get that song recorded – the musicians and the studio was free.

'When the song was recorded, Mighty Diamonds and everybody was going crazy over it. I went to Channel One and said, "I can't put it out now. Would you put it out for me?" I told them the song named "Reason For Living" but Joe Joe said, "No, this tune should name 'Born For A Purpose' ", so that's how it was originally on his label. Later on I put it on my label when I was able to move around.' The record's fame spread far and wide, finding a champion abroad in Johnny Rotten, who voiced approval of the song's defiant stance on London radio. Its success would result in Alimantado emigrating to Britain.

After he had recovered from his accident Doctor Alimantado gave his most notable performance to a capacity audience at the National Arena – a definite high point in his live career. 'Most people remember that perfonuance until this day,' he states proudly. He came to London in the latter half of 1977 with the intention of setting up a UK branch of his label; however, discussions with Island proved mutually unsatisfactory, and though Virgin licensed the sung twelve-inch 'Find The One' they were unable to establish a long-term agreement with Tado. 'When I came to England first, it was to set up a company, and [reggae retailers] Dub Vendor and Daddy Kool was supposed to be the managers, but in negotiations it break down. We had to go our separate ways, with me owing them some plane fare and a bit of money. At the time there were companies like Virgin willing to put half a million pounds on Doctor Alimantado, but Greensleeves offered to help me set up my own company, which was more important to me. Greensleeves' offer wasn't financially viable, but it offered me the best in a stable company.' Alimantado's _Best Dressed Chicken In Town_ became the company's inaugural album release in 1978.

After U Roy and I Roy, one of Virgin's most prominent deejays was Tappa Zukie, though his relationship with the company was turbulent and much of his best material was issued elsewhere. At Mixing Lab, Tappa is swaying on his feet, the sweat on his brow seemingly caused by rum rather than the afternoon heat. In West Kingston, where he was born David Sinclair in 1955, he endured a rough upbringing. 'I grew up in between Greenwich Town and Trench Town, the hardcore town. Most people know me as the "cork and tar": a cork and tar is a ball that you beat 'til it becomes likkle. It is so tough that when it hit you, it's like a bomb. The leather ball, if you'r e beating it, it bust wide open, but a cork and tar it never bust, and the smaller it get is the hotter it make. My parents, pressure reach them, and they didn't even have time to think about wre, so I have to go 'pon the road from [when] I was twelve. When I run away from home the people of Trench Town take to me, they say, "When your mother and your father forsake you, the lord God Jah will pick you up."'

It was in these youthful days that Tappa started deejaying on the I-Oses and Maccabees sets, prefiguring the later fame of youngsters like Little John and Beenie Man. 'I was the dancehall crave, the young thing that was happening in the dancehall. I was the first little-boy deejay, and I used to hear people saying I greater than U Roy, Big Youth and Dennis Alcapone.' Along with this early fame came wildness. 'I used to play sound and everybody rate me, and they say that I was a hyped youth. People say crazy things and I'l l do crazy things, so Bunny Lee, my brother and my mother decide to send me away to England to escape these crazy things I do. I was sixteen or seventeen years old.'

The move brought Tappa even greater exposure. 'I had never recorded, never appeared on a show before, and Bunny Lee introduced me on this show U Roy had at Woodlands Hall in Ladbroke Grove [London]. They played the rhythm for Slim Smith's "The Time Has Come" and I toast over it. At the time, everybody had me as a warrior. People was scared of me – for what, I don't know! I'm just a guy who stand up for my rights and don't let nobody push me around, so I figured the man is a warrior, so that's when I made that album _Man A Warrior.'_ The album, produced by Clement Bushay around 1973 and issued in 1975 by Klik records, caused quite a stir overseas. American new wave singer Patti Smith professed her admiration of it, and later brought the disc greater acclaim by facilitating a significant reissue.

Though Tappa's voice was still unbroken when the album was cut, he sounds surprisingly confident on most of it. On the title track, he translates the hardships of his street existence into a calmly charged defiance. Other tracks seem rather juvenile, particularly the comic-sounding 'Archie The Red Nosed Reindeer', a barb apparently aimed at one of the neighbourhood toughs. 'Archie was one of my friends,' Tappa explains. 'He used to live in White Street. Those guys was the big guys on the corner, but I was a shooter. I was a hit-man, so I was just troubling him, because Archie was a bad guy.'

When Tappa returned to Jamaica towards the end of 1974 he was chiefly employed as muscle. 'I was actually a bodyguard for Bunny Lee,' he confirms, 'and I enforce artists to work and record for him. Bunny Lee is like a father to me – me and my brother Blackbeard used to go to the studio with him.' Lee was at first reluctant to record the young man, so Tappa cut the strong 'Judge I Oh Lord' for his associate, Lloydie Slim. He then began voicing the odd tune for Lee, but during an altercation over the foreign issue of 'Natty Dread Girl Don't Cry' Tappa used threatening behaviour towards his boss; however, the pair quickly patched up their quarrel and Lee gave the toaster cuts of a number of his hit rhythms. These backing tracks and others obtained from Channel One gave Tappa the platfonii for further material, with driving rockers-style beats allowing for a more zestful and energetic performance.

Tappa then founded the Stars label, moving into the producer's chair with ease. In addition to producing his own vocal and dub material, he recorded exceptional work with Greenwich Farm vocalists such as Junior Ross and the Spears (notably 'Judgement Time') and Prince Allah ('Bosrah' and 'Heaven Is My Roof). In the summer of 1976 Zukie returned to the UK to license more material to Klik, who hit with the singles 'MPLA' and 'Pick Up The Rockers'. Tappa's dynamic stage shows, in which his vocal skills were matched by limber kung-fu moves, complemented the esteem that the _Man A Warrior_ album had already gained him; Klik quickly arranged to issue the popular _MPLA_ album. 'A lot of things could have happened to me, but God take care of my reward,' Tappa insists. 'I was rewarded when I came to England and the people nominate me to be a star, though I came to England to promote Junior Ross and Prince Allah.'

Virgin enjoyed great success with _MPLA after_ picking it up from Klik. As Tappa's star continued to rise, a Virgin contract for the _Peace In Tlie Ghetto_ album enabled him to offer tangible assistance to the underprivileged Trench Town community that had given him support when he most needed it. 'Trench Town, that's where Jah pick me and put me, so that's why when I sign up with Virgin I build a community centre and that Golden Age home, to give back to the people and show my appreciation to them.'

The subsequent _Tappa Roots_ album included Tappa's suggestive 'She Want A Phensic', one of the biggest reggae hits in England in 1978, but he then withdrew his allegiance from Virgin for political reasons. 'Virgin write me up as a Mafioso because I demands back my contract from them. No disrespect intended, but I heard at the time they was selling a lot of records in South Africa, but those monies wasn't coming back to England. Some people tell me they was investing in guns to use against my people in South Africa, so I confront Mr Branson, say, "I'm a black man and I don't support such things." He said, "Tappa Zukie, I'm surprised at you. What are you talking about?" I said, "That's the argument on the street, so I don't want to be a part of this", and that's how me and Virgin mash up. At the time U Roy was going for them in Africa, and Culture and I was going for Virgin in England. I had "Phensic", so I was happening for them. From there, no big company would deal with me, because I'm the cork and tar. That's how I decide to come back to Jamaica and establish my own thing.'

Released around the same time as 'Phensic' was the harder 'Living In The Ghetto', a first-hand account of life on the street that could only have come from someone who had spent the majority of their days in such surroundings. ' "Living In The Ghetto" is after me decide to make the community centre,' Tappa recalls, 'so it's like exposing the lifestyle. I was living deep in the ghetto then.'

**Voice of Thunder: Prince Fari [Beth Lesser]**

More hits followed, including the boastful 'Oh Lord', which swept the island more than any previous hit Zukie had done. His duet with Horace Andy on a recut of the Soul Syndicate's 'Natty Dread A Whe She Want' was initially destined for Virgin, but drew a strong response on both sides of the Atlantic when issued independently by Tappa; Jet Star's distribution of the extended twelve-inch was particularly successful in the UK. Like 'Living In The Ghetto', the half-sung 'Raggy Joey Boy' was another poignant description of ghetto life, but Tappa avoided the trend set by older deejays and never made the full shift from toaster to singer. 'That's when I get raggy there,' he says. 'That's when everybody say that Tappa Zukie was a terrorist, but I was just tired of seeing people taking advantage of poor people, so I have to stand up to defend them. You notice that _[Raggy Joey Boy]_ is the only album with a little singing on [it], because the way I take it with music is from one extreme to the other. I don't see myself just like a dancehall artist, I just see myself as a musician, so I'l l make jazz, funk, reggae, anything – the frame of mind and the vibes to really hit me at that moment.'

Of all the deejays signed to Virgin's Front Line, Prince Fari is perhaps the most legendary. 'What I really love about Prince Fari,' says Joseph Hill, 'is that he project the music as a source of spiritual strength. I love the way he handles the good lyrics that he got.' Fari was a gruff toaster who had a long prison record. The self-styled 'Voice of Thunder' hailed from Spanish Town and was originally known as King Cry Cry until producer Enos McLeod renamed him. His recording debut came at Studio One, where he had previously been employed as security; his smooth yet charged delivery on 'Natty Farmyard' gave a hint of things to come. Fari's first album, _Psalms For I,_ was a stunning religious concept disc cut for Lloydie Slim around 1975; the following year _Under Heavy Manners_ made good use of hit rhythms from Joe Gibbs, but it was the _Message From The King_ and _Long Life_ albums on Virgin's Front Line that brought him to a wider overseas audience in 1978.

U Brown also had two albums issued by Virgin Front Line, the excellent _Mr Brown Something_ and _Can't Keep A Good Man Down._ Hugh Brown became U Brown on the sound system circuit because he patterned his style after U Roy, whom he regularly heard when he was growing up. 'I usually live at 35 Bond Street and Duke Reid was next door,' the tall, bald toaster reveals, 'and in life, each one teach one.' Brown is waiting to take the mike at Leggo studio, about eight blocks from the spot where he first encountered U Roy; the cosy facility is one currently favoured by veterans and conscious Rasta youth.

'I started first with a sound named Silver Bullet from Tower Hill – Olympic Gardens,' he continues. 'I mess around with that sound for over a year, and then a friend was keeping a dance with another sound system named Sounds of Music, based in Waterhouse. I mess around with him about another fifteen months. I get my real big break when U Roy met with a motorbike accident, fracture his leg. I Roy wasn't around much to fill in, 'cause he was travelling very often then, so there was a dance keeping in Sunrise Crescent, and Dillinger was the one that they requested to deejay. A guy that work with Tubby's sound recommend me to Tubbys, and on the night Tubbys say he really want me round the sound. Each night I go, it's always more inspiration. Tubby's was the number one sound in the world that time, play everywhere in Jamaica – from Negril Point to Portland.'

U Brown's fame on King Tubby's sound led to his debut recording in 1975, produced by Joe Gibbs' cousin, then a law student in London. 'I first went into the studio with Winston Edwards and the first two songs were "Jamaican Tobacco" and "Wet Up Your Pants Foot" – 1 record those at Tubbys. My next was for Yabby You, "Wolf In Sheep Clothing" [aka "Dem A Wolf']. Yabby You sing a song on it named "Anti Christ". At that time, Tubby's was _the_ studio. Although it wasn't a studio where you lay rhythm tracks, most man always come at Tubby's to get a mix, because Tubby's have the spice. Yabby come there, Niney come there, Bunny Lee come like him a the resident man there.'

**U Brown at Leggo Studio [David Katz]**

After his time on Tubby's sound U Brown had a number _of_ other significant residencies, most notably on King Attorney, the sound system owned by PNP 'enforcer' Tony Welch and later known as Socialist Roots." When that sound encountered problems, U Browrn found himself on the mike at Jack Ruby's Hi Power in Ocho Rios. 'Jack Ruby take me from Kingston when politics was getting hot,' he says gravely, 'because one time Socialist Roots did have to stop play – too much pressure from the people and the police, and in those days police don't respect entertainer.' Ruby then had a supremely powerful sound system that had island-wide appeal.

It was through Striker Lee that U Brown started cutting self-produced material on the Hit Sound label around 1977. 'Bunny Lee is like another Coxsone,' he emphasizes. 'When you round Bunny Lee, you must learn something 'bout the business. Bunny Lee give me the first rhythm, a version of a Barry Brown song. That's where I started my first production, "Badness A Madness" – I gave it to Federal to distribute.' His biggest hit was 'Weather Balloon', a flight _of fancy_ narrated with much gusto at a session supervised by engineer Crucial Bunny at Channel One. 'I met a girl, June, and even now she's still my wife,' he quips happily, speaking of the song's obscure inspiration. 'Lyrically, things just build, and you go in the studio and try a thing.' Another high point for U Brown was his toast on the extended mix of Linval Thompson's 'Train To Zion', cut in 1977 for Socialist Roots.

At this point, Linval Thompson was reaching full stride as a vocalist and producer of his own material. Thompson says that, as a teenager in the early 1970s, he drew inspiration from the styles of Al Campbell, Dennis Brown and Johnny Clarke. 'I was born in Kingston in 1954, grew up in the Three Mile area,' he recalls. 'Mother and Father had their own business, like grocery stores. I was singing at school when Jacob Miller started recording for Coxsone, and I wasn't recording, but I was trying to, just like Johnny Clarke. We was living in the same area, Kingston 13.'

In the early seventies Thompson's parents emigrated to Brooklyn, where they took domestic work. Linval joined them and subsequently settled in Queens, where he remained for roughly two years; in order to further his singing career, he often mingled with expatriate musicians. 'Buccaneers was one of the first reggae band in New York,' he recalls. 'Bunny Ruggs was there, before he turned Third World, and Rick Frater. Me never perform with them, but I think I was trying to. Horace Andy used to come around that time also.'

Though progress was slow in terms of live performance, Thompson was able to make his debut recording within a year of arriving in America. 'I went to a studio in Brooklyn named Art Craft with Patrick Alley, a singer [that] sound like John Holt's style. I spend the money and do "There's No Other Woman In This World". I come back to Jamaica maybe '74, and the first thing I do is for "Stamma", K.Hobson – I know him from Orange Street area. He hear the first song that I recorded and he liked that sound, near like how Dennis Brown sound, so we do an original song named "Westbound Plane". I voice [it] on a rhythm track at Randy's studio. Then I do two songs for Phil Pratt ["Jah Jah Redder Than Red" and "Girl You'v e Got To Run"]. He hear the sound and everybody say that this new artist comes around, has that Dennis Brown sound. I do two for Scratch too, "Kung Fu Man," and the other one I can't remember.' Another number called 'Kung Fu', again celebrating martial arts, was cut the same year in Jamaica for Kass Harris, 'a guy that have a record shop'.

**Linval Thompson [David Katz]**

These early efforts formed stepping stones to success, which Linval achieved by 1975 when he teamed up with Bunny Lee. 'They create a little buzz,' he explains. 'That make Bunny Lee get interested in me and that's where the hit come – hit after hit.' His first recording for Striker was the highly popular 'Don't You Cut Off Your Dreadlocks', written despite the fact that Thompson sometimes trimmed and combed his hair. After the release of the _Don't Cut Off Your Dreadlocks_ album Thompson gradually took charge of his own creations, a process he describes as largely intuitive. 'I never plan it,' he modestly insists. 'It just happened. A guy will come and say he want me to do some songs. I say, "All right, I'l l sing some for you and I'l l sing some for myself, so I can get a cut of the rhythm tracks." That's the way I start, exchanging. The first song what I do name "I Love. Marijuana", I use Family Man on bass at Randy's . Socialist Roots hear the sound. I was the youngest artist in Jamaica on my own, so they ask if I could sing a song for them, play this instrumental track in the dance [a recut of the Studio One classic "Death In The Arena", aka "Whipping The Prince"] and I listen to it, never write no song, never have no idea, but I say, "Yes, I will voice it." Then we go to Channel One and the lyrics come to me just like that. "Train To Zion" was the biggest hit after "Don't Cut Off Your Dreadlocks" – mash up the whole of Jamaica.'

Not long afterwards, Thompson began licensing albums to a revitalized Trojan in the UK (now controlled by Marcel Rodd of Saga, specialists in low-budget classical LPs) and Clocktower records in New York. 'I do my first album, J _Love Marijuana,_ I do a dub album _[Negrea Love Dub],_ and I do a Big Joe deejay album name _African Princess._ I know him from Three Mile – he was on some likkle sound at that time.' In this period Thompson was also working with singers such as Cornell Campbell and Wayne Jarrett and deejays such as Trinity and Ranking Trevor, in addition to crafting further hits of his own such as 'Six Babylon', a tale of police brutality.

On the other side of Spanish Town Road, a few streets south of the Three Mile home ground of proto-dancehall stars Johnny Clarke, Linval Thompson and Al Campbell, heavier and more deeply religious roots music was being constructed by a core of committed artists in Greenwich Farm. Bertram Brown's Freedom Sounds was a focal point for local singers concerned with truth, rights and justice; the label was based at Brown's home at 14 East Avenue, next to the family's wholesale liquor store. 'I was born right here in 1950,' Brown says in the Freedom Sounds yard, 'right here so me navel string cut. Me father was a cooper, they make barrel to store rum in, and my mother was a housewife, because it's ten pickney and me a the last one. Me grow up round here through '51 storm, all them bad elections, the wars and peace, and me still here with the youth them.'

**Champion of the underdog:**

**Bertram Brown [Bernard Warin]**

Bertram is a short man with a ruddy complexion who seems to be always on the go. Freedom Sounds was reactivated some years ago as a base of operations for veteran Rasta singers based nearby, and Brown is constantly called to the telephone; he has a dour countenance and seldom smiles. His bustling headquarters is a dilapidated concrete shell with records stacked haphazardly against the crumbling walls; the place is in bad need of a lick of paint and some new furniture, yet it retains a homely character and obviously continues to operate on a grass-roots ghetto level.

The Freedom Sounds label was officially inaugurated in 1975, though Brown says he began producing at the start of the 1970s; he says he got involved because local talent creating message music was largely being ignored. 'Me get the inspiration from Prince Allah and Milton Henry,' Brown explains. 'When me come from school, me see them a play them box guitar, and Prince Allah always a chant biblical songs 'bout "Lot's Wife" and "Great Stone", and me no see nobody a record him. The first job me father get for me was in the same barrel place, and we save up our money and go by Randy's , there so me lay me first rhythms: "Thank You Me Lord" and "Judgement Time" wdth Prince Allah, and "Cornbread And Butter" with Milton Henry. Songs like those, me use Soul Syndicate _every_ time. Then we include Philip Fraser, Earl Zero, Rod Taylor, Frankie Jones, Sylvan White. All them likkle man come, we a cook and eat and live together, all of we live right round the place. Bunny Lee lives two doors from me, so me grow up see Slim Smith, Ken Boothe and Cornell Campbell a come through. I remember when Earl Zero sing "None Shall Escape The Judgement", him do it for Bunny Lee and Johnny Clarke sing it over.

'So when I see all them things I say the youth them have talent, and them have the message most of all to really show the people truth and rights, because where there's no truth there can't be a kingdom and a proper government, so it's that we a seek within the music to make the people understand, cause without them understanding we is lost. Freedom Sounds is here, from those days, to give the people good music which portrays love and harmony at all times and discipline within mankind as a whole, show how mankind fi live with one another. I deal with who the people reject, who the people nah listen to. I always have a notion that the stone that the builder refuse always come back to be the headstone.'

Working with Soul Syndicate and cutting dubs at King Tubby's studio, Bertram Brown oversaw some of the heaviest roots music of the period, his artists' indignant lyrics matched by fearsome rhythm tracks and dubs that emphasized the songs' foreboding and menacing qualities. Brown says the area's dominant dancehall had long been a great inspiration. 'Right beside my yard was the biggest dancehall inna Western Kingston, the New Style Lawn. Coxsone usually play there, Duke Reid, Sir George the Atomic. I grow up attending these dances, listening to all these good musics, getting the inspiration and vibes.'

The man called Prince Allah – born Keith Blake – is also known as the Gentle Giant, and the epithet seems entirely appropriate: his hefty frame is almost always crowned by a broad and radiant smile, he is courteous and helpful to everyone and seems governed by a peaceful religiosity. It was in church, in fact, that the quality of Blake's voice was first noted. 'My mother and father used to love go to church, so when I was about seven them carry me to Hope Chapel, Methodist, and I sang "Our Father", and everybody said, "Yes!" After I leave school, I had a friend by the name of Soft [Roy Palmer] and one by the name of Milton [Henry]. They love music and Milton could play the guitar, so we used to sing together. We met Joe Gibbs – in those times he just come from Guantanamo Bay. Him carry we to studio and we did two songs for him, "Hope Someday" and "Fay" as the Leaders.'

**The Freedom Sounds gate [David Katz]**

After recording a handful of other sides for Gibbs, at the start of the 1970s Blake renounced all things worldly to join the followers of Prince Emmanuel Edwards at the 'Bobo Dread' camp in Bull Bay, Saint Thomas. The Bobo branch of Rastafari has come to be associated with fundamentalism, particularly after a dramatic expansion in the 1990s; some of the faithful have suggested that Edwards himself was another manifestation of Selassie. Drumming and chanting have always been central to their worship. 'I went up to the Bobo Camp, was up there about six years just chanting,' Blake recalls. 'So when I leave, I start dealing with record. Up at Emmanuel we used to read about Melchizedek, this High Priest that have no mother or father, no beginning nor no days at the ending of time. So when I leave there and come home on Sea View Avenue, Tappa Zukie, as a likkle youth, he used to run likkle weed, but him with Bunny Lee now, and Bunny Lee decide fi help him out so that he could get a little studio time. We went to the Ark studio. Lee Scratch Perry, he really does the arrangements. The harmony, it's Soft and Tony Brevett of the Melodians.'

The result was the arresting 'Bosrah', a 1976 single credited to Ras Allah and the Spears. 'When I small, I used to love Mohammed Ali, the boxer,' explains Blake, 'so through me love Mohammed Ali so much, everybody just call me Allah. After Tappa Zukie went to England with that tune, we do a LP up at Channel One _[Heaven Is My Roof] with_ "Funeral", "Daniel In The Lion's Den".' 'Funeral', one of the album's outstanding tracks, was a symbolic rejection of society. 'When I was up at the Bobo, we used to read the Bible every day. Him say, "Let the dead bury them dead", so it's just that likkle vibes there. At that time, when I look inna myself, I say it's not just a _physical_ funeral, because even the system round you is like a funeral. So I say I nah gonna follow them system, that mean I'm not going go to them funeral. I wasn't really dealing with just a physical dead, like my father and mother, because if them dead, me a going go to them funeral. I looking at the system: it's like a funeral, just lead to destruction the Same way.'

**Prince Allah [Steve Mosco, courtesy of Jah Warrior records]**

Other strong material was cut for Freedom Sounds, such as 'Lot's Wife' and 'Bucket Bottom', which used a proverb to illustrate Allah's disappointments within the music industry. 'It was just a thing I was going through with some of the promoters,' he explains. 'I say, "Every day them bucket go a well, one day the bottom must drop out. You use me, one day it's going to stop."' Another outstanding track was the 1977 single 'Great Stone', again inspired by Bible readings at the Bobo camp. Bertram Brown says King Tubby greatly shaped this record by adding a custom sound effect to illustrate better Allah's message of a 'great great stone just a come to mash down Rome'.

'King Tubby mixed that, and I think that is one of the wickedest mix we have ever made with that rumbling,' Brown exclaims. 'You hear the stone coming in that song. We get a rumbling sound first from some thunder sound we get out of a movie, get it and stretch it, put it 'pon a two-track [tape]. We usually work with King Tubby because we love King Tubby, he inspire we a lot. The greater portion of work was done by King Tubbys. Scientist did things still, but him was just an assistant, because Jammys used to mix in between. Even before Jammy become a producer, we a work with them man there as the engineer. All of them man there is Tubby's understudy, so King Tubby really do a lot for music business.'

After his return to Jamaica and installation as Tubby's assistant engineer, Jammy rapidly established a reputation as a skilled mixer; he had already inched into the production sphere before moving back to Kingston. 'I produce one record I released in Canada,' he explains. 'That was Nana McLean, a do-over song by the name of "Single Girl". When I came back to Jamaica in 1976, I redid it in a better fashion with the same artist.' Jammy was subsequently instrumental in shaping what would be known as the Waterhouse style, working largely with neighbourhood singers who favoured a particular vocal slurring. 'All of those artists that I used to work with, they grew with us in the Waterhouse area: Black Uhuru, Locksley Castell, the Travellers. I was the young producer coming up in the area. Everybody think about supporting me, and I used to cater for them. We were just experimenting in those days, but some of those songs became legendary.'

The Waterhouse act to become most famous was Black Uhuru, a vocal trio with an ever-shifting line-up. The group was initially formed as Uhuru, the Swahili word for unity, with Garth Dennis as lead singer and Don McCarlos and Derrick 'Duckie' Simpson providing harmony.
