Eventually, when Reid is satisfied enough to take a short break, he speaks of early group work cut in 1982 for a London-based roots label. 'Robert and Michael Palmer, they was Negus Roots,' Reid explains. 'They used to live in Waterhouse, but they went to England and live, and [when] they come back down in Jamaica they want to work with me. They put out two singles, "If I" and "Sister Dawn". When they came back to Jamaica, they want to do a album. I was moving around with my friends them, they like singing, so we just say, "Why not name ourselves as a band, Voice of Progress? We did the album _Minibus Driver._ Then, when we were supposed to do the follow-up album, they was taking up a lot of studio time, screwing up things, so I just went back solo.'

**Refuting the Babylonian media:** **Junior Reid [David Katz]**

Jammy then cut dancehall hits with the singer, notably 'Boom Shack A Lack' and 'Higgler Move'; in Britain the strongest impact was made with the moving 'Jailhouse', released through North London sound system operator Ken "Fatman" Gordon on the KG Imperial label. This hit material, plus the album Reid cut for Jammy with the High Times Players, showed the singer establishing his own particular interpretation of the Waterhouse vocal style. Reid went on to record for Junjo, but it was after hooking up with Sugar Minott's Youth Promotion in 1984 that Reid cut his biggest hit: 'Original Foreign Mind', a perceptive take on the lure of immigration, rose high in the Jamaican charts and also made an impact on the growing American reggae scene. Reid was then drafted into Black Uhuru on Michael Rose's surprise departure, just after the group won a Grammy for _Anthem_ – the first such award for a reggae album. 'People start tell me you can fire or hire, so I just say forget this,' explains Michael Rose. 'From [when] it start reach them extremes there, I'm turned off.'

'I was asked to join Black Uhuru,' Junior Reid states with pride. 'It was a great time. I link up with Black Uhuru with a hit song, "Fit You Haffe Fit". 'Reid injected fresh energy into the group, particularly on live performances, but returned to his solo career in the late 1980s. He says he left the group 'due to music politics' more than anything else; his ominous description of events is a reminder that in their heyday Black Uhuru got greater international attention than any contemporary reggae act. 'After them kill Bob Marley, they never want nothing more so powerful,' Reid insists, 'and Black Uhuru was the next group to get big promotion behind, so they just try to mash up the whole reggae and keep it 'pon a level like how you see them have it now. So me just go forward solo.'

Some of the hit material that Half Pint and Junior Reid recorded for Jammy benefited from the hands of Scientist, who became so disgruntled with the state of affairs at Channel One that he left for the twenty-four-track Tuff Gong shortly afterwards, opting for the more peaceful environment of the complex at 56 Hope Road. 'Nobody really wanted to give me credit,' he grumbles, 'and I'm the only one down there with an electronic background, so if anything get damaged or not working properly I could get it to work some. Everybody getting jealous, because everybody older than me, but I am bringing stuff to the studio that they never did before. I went to Tuff Gong and the sound changed. Before, only uptown bands like Third World going to Tuff Gong, the hardcore dancehall thing was not being done at Tuff Gong. One of my strongest supporters there was Tristan Palmer, and then Early B. All of those dancehall people started going to Tuff Gong – Tuff Gong took away all the customers from Channel One. After that they closed down because they lost all their clientele.'

Joseph Hoo-Kim says Channel One's demise stemmed partly from his brother Ernest's departure from the music business. 'Ernest didn't like the confinement – he couldn't take being in the studio too much, so he began to operate gambling machines again. I bring Kenneth in the business, the same time with Frankie Paul, but it begin to get down when I and Kenneth didn't get along.'

Scientist says the atmosphere at Tuff Gong was initially more appealing than the one he had left at Channel One. 'Tuff Gong had Chow, a very good maintenance engineer – the studio was kept very good. Matter of fact, it was the most comfortable place I worked. Round at Tubbys and Channel One you have to deal with all the bad ghetto elements. When I went to Tuff Gong, I could finally breathe and just be an engineer.' Unfortunately, such tranquillity was not to last. 'I was at Tuff Gong about a year-and-a-half, didn't have to worry about too much of the gangster runnings, but some of the same elements started to come there because I carry that sound wherever I go... but I was fed up with the whole runnings, it was becoming too much of a rat race. I didn't feel the vibes, so I come to America in 1985. My sisters, my mother, everybody was living here.' Thus the last of the classical dub masters made his exit from Jamaica, and a notable decline in dub albums followed.

Meanwhile, other artists were taking reggae's liberating message even further afield. 'I'm the first artist to go to Japan, bringing sound system,' says Sugar Minott, ever in touch with the dancehall experience, 'and Sister Carol go over there and Frankie Paul. They didn't know that there was a thing called sound system – the first time we play, they put this little diagram in the magazine to show you how to hook it up. I have to be the selector at the same time, trying to teach some Japanese youth.' The impact of reggae on the Japanese consciousness was such that acts like the abstract instrumental group Mute Beat would eventually collaborate with the Jamaican artists who inspired them – a strong testimony to the transcendent qualities of a music that has crossed seemingly impenetrable social and linguistic barriers. 'Reggae music is the only music in the world that every nation relate to,' says Cocoa Tea. 'If them don't understand the lyrics, at least the rhythm gets to their mind, their heart – it penetrates.'

Ironically, it was a Japanese creation that brought indelible changes to Jamaican popular music at the start of 1985. The Casio company produced a number _of_ inexpensive keyboard instruments, including easily portable models with pre-set rhythms and built-in keyboard melodies. One such pre-set provided the blueprint for Wayne Smith's 'Under Mi Sleng Teng', a paean to the wonders of marijuana that would change the face of the Jamaican recording scene. Smith was another Waterhouse singer with a high tone; he started recording for Jammy in 1980 and was featured on mid-eighties' Channel One material. A friend, Noel Daley, had a Casio keyboard, and the pair discovered a pre-set that inspired Smith to write 'Sleng Teng', borrowing heavily from the phrasing and structure of Barrington Levy's 'Under Mi Sensi'. Smith took the concept to Jammy, who got session player Tony Asher to construct the rhythm with drum machines and synthesizers, using the pre-set keyboard melody for the bass line. When Jammy first aired the resultant song at a Kingston clash with Black Scorpio sound, pandemonium ensued; the sheer minimalism of the rhythm bore the portents of a new style, its syncopation particularly enthralling to the dancehall audience. On its release, 'Sleng Teng' caused shock waves all over Jamaica, and shortly afterwards, all over the world.

The repercussions of the spectacular success of 'Sleng Teng' were many and varied. The song immediately spawned countless recuts by raw hopefuls and seasoned talent alike; among the most noteworthy are 'Pumpkin Belly' by rising dancehall singer Tenor Saw and Culture's delirious 'Catch A Rasta'. The success of 'Sleng Teng' made Jammy's tiny studio a completely self-sufficient reality; with keyboards, sequencer and drum machine he had enough to construct hit rhythms, voicing and overdubbing as necessary. He no longer had to rely on the more established, larger and better-equipped facilities like Harry J and Channel One; everything could be done right at home, in his converted bedroom. In time this approach would revolutionize the way recordings were made in Jamaica, as others began to convert similar spaces; the concept of the bedroom studio was also taken up in places like Chicago and London, where aspiring producers and club deejays used similar set-ups to turn tape loops and sampled sequences into dance music.

'The times change,' says Jammy of the shift from live to digital recordings. 'You have to go with the time if you want to eat food. If you want to earn a living from the music, you have to do whatever is going on today. In the early seventies, when I used to record at Channel One or Harry J, we used to do live music. Then we just want to experiment, so we did something with the "Sleng Teng" and it changed the whole scene, and that was great for me – even today, everybody is using electronics, computerized drums, drum machine. I really revolutionized that part of the music with "Sleng Teng". It was [being done] a little before "Sleng Teng", but "Sleng Teng" was the big hit that made everybody switch over.'

Though most producers adapted to the digital form without fuss, certain key figures were less keen. 'What really bother me is I didn't like computer,' says Joseph Hoo-Kim, noting that 'Sleng Teng' signalled the end of Channel One. 'When the computer sound come in, I run one session with Sugar Minott and one with Diamonds, and after that I say, "No." I didn't like the sound and I didn't like one man playing a rhythm, so I didn't run no more session after that.'

The incredible popularity of 'Sleng Teng' prompted further changes and innovations, but this time the shift was more than stylistic: the very way music was created in Jamaica became different, requiring less finance and demanding technical rather than musical ability. Although the change in style seems drastic when looked at in retrospect and from the outside, to the Jamaican dancing public of the time it made perfect sense. The rugged rhythms of computer drums reflected the harsh reality of modern urban life, providing exciting fuel for the dancehall audience, and in any case the predominant sound had been heading in this direction for a long time through increased use of synthesizers and electronic drumbeats.

As noted throughout this book, Jamaica's dancehall culture has always reflected the desires and tastes of the island's public; I have also tried to show that reggae music has undergone endless cycles of renewal as its creators experiment with new techniques in their quest for fresh sounds and styles that can be created easily in difficult conditions. In this sense the digital explosion can be viewed as part of the overall continuity, albeit one that often perplexes roots enthusiasts. 'Sleng Teng' may have loosened the floodgates and brought Jamaican music headlong into the digital age, but the new music was ultimately a continuation of what preceded it, and a vast number of further shifts in style, form, and content have succeeded it right up to the present day. Though sexually suggestive 'slackness' and violent 'gun talk' came to dominate for a time – typically propagated by deejays over the barest of computer rhythms – reggae music would come full circle with the new roots movement that came to the fore from 1993 and saw more singers and live instruments returning to the mix as well as a revival of Rastafari elements. These days, Jamaican popular music can take any number of bewildering forms: hardcore dancehall deejays like Bounty Killer and Elephant Man perform the Kingston equivalent of American 'gangsta rap' over an electronic version of the ancient Pukumina beat, whilst artists like Luciano, Everton Blender and Bushman present an updated classical roots, appealing to an international audience through a spiritual message set to a complex backing of live and electronic instruments. Somewhere in between are the fiery Bobo Dread ranters like Capleton and Sizzla, who preach fundamentalist values over rhythms often more akin to heavy metal or hardcore punk than anything that was once known as reggae; veterans such as Dwight Pinkney craft easy-listening instrumental recuts of classic hits, and performers like Lieutenant Stitchie and Carlene Davis blend reggae and country elements with gospel music. In fact, the changes in Jamaican music since 'Sleng Teng' are so numerous, brought by a preponderance of new artists and others in the music industry, that another book is needed to place these figures in their proper context.

**Unleashing the computer: Prince Jammy [Beth Lesser]**

Changing times naturally bring forth different sounds due to a variety of factors. During the height of the digital era, Rico Rodriguez suggested a political motivation behind the advent of the deejay. 'Most of the music in Jamaica is business music,' he stated with disdain, 'or one particular party may have deejays who they support. This particular party is in power, so the show that they put on, it's pure deejays on it. They put the deejay first, because the deejay can utter phrases to suit a political party that an instrumentalist cannot perform, so the deejay thing has become a political element of the scene in Jamaica and musicians are treated like shoe-shine boys. I am a Rastaman who give out against society utterly. We don't defend PNP or JLP or whatever P – we defend people. No one owns music. People with money will speculate about music, but the musicians have the final argument.'

'Since them kill Bob Marley, them try to kill reggae from there,' says Junior Reid, alluding to a broader conspiracy. 'They consider Bob Marley the king of reggae, so they would really have to switch the business and make it be more computerized. Certain companies start say that they'r e not looking for one individual, they'r e looking for ten man on one rhythm, so you go to England and you don't make no money. That become the trend, and then when that producer come back to Jamaica, him tell the guys, "This is _how_ the reggae going now. You have to have ten man on the same rhythm or you'r e not going to eat any food", so every man just gone a [certain] way, and when the people in America and England hear reggae coming out of Jamaica now, them say we are not creative any more since Bob Marley and those guys not around – we are not creative any more, because ten of us is on the same beat. The people say reggae dead. Bob Marley was a soldier just like we, but at the same time I don't want to be a next Marley, because what them do to Bob Marley, I don't want them do that to me. We don't come as no lamb to the slaughter in this judgement, we come as the Conquering Lion of the tribe ofjudah, elect from Jah, ever-living God, earth's rightful ruler. So right now them have to hand over everything.'

Sugar Minott adds some perceptive thoughts on why reggae's militant message waned during the digital onslaught of the late 1980s: 'People just want to hear songs that make them feel good in the long run,' he reasons, 'make them feel happy. It's not every time you want to tell somebody, "They'r e bombing up Belfast" – that's not music, that's politics.

'We use the music to send a message what we feel and the struggle that we see. When me likkle, me used to sing "I want to go to Africa", because we used to believe in it, but now you want to go to Africa, you take a plane, simple as that! [Repatriation] did not happen the way we wanted it to happen. We have to face the reality, 'cause it don't make sense singing music that don't mean nothing. Right now, I don't like no songs so much about "Babylon" – it don't make no sense. People come say, "This is a federation of Rastafari", and I was a Twelve Tribes, sing for free, "Power to the people" and all that shit. But in the long run, everybody's just the same. If you deal with music, [and] used to believe in [something], but if you get to know that it's not so, you can't be feeding people [something] false, so my music is getting even more real – it's getting happy again. When I used to sing "Man Hungry" it was real, I wasn't making it up, but [if] people's going to sing "We want repatriation", you can go to Africa if you want easy now. I understand there's people go to Gambia to do shows, but we never know that. We was like, "Where is Africa? Somewhere on the map. We want to reach it, that's where our people [are] from, that's our ancestor." I used to say, "Africa is the black man's home" for my only kid, so now you know that you can go there I ain't going to sing that again.

'Like they say the Cold War done, some different things, so you can't go on that same way... how the music come now in Jamaica now, they have F-150s, they got Benz, how they going to sing about "Man Hungry"? They want "Grooving in my jeep", but in our days we don't roll with no jeep, man suffer, so man say, "Babylon a put on the pressure", 'cause that was the only way you could sing out the frustration. The music get so different now, I have to adjust and appreciate all those things I used to take for granted.'

Though the liberating message that drew many to reggae may have gone underground in its birthplace, by the time Jamaican music went digital reggae's solid foundation of inspired creativity and devoted religious faith had built a truly global house with many mansions, inspiring a great audience well beyond its land of origin. I leave the closing words to Cocoa Tea, one of the artists to have consistently sung conscious material, to emphasize reggae's universality: 'Reggae music is the heartbeat of the people. Reggae music highlight the struggles of the people, no matter what race of life people come from, 'cause reggae music come fi make the people them get help. It there to uplift the mind and refresh the soul and to teach us how to live in love and harmony. Reggae music preach equal rights and justice. We have to unite black and white: you have your struggles you a go through, me have mine, it's only that I have the medium in which to express your struggles and highlight mine too [at] the same time. One song say "Not because you live on the hill and I live down there on the flats, everything that you do indirectly affects me whether you know it or not", so it no matter where you want [to] live or who you want [to] be: the war that a gwan down a Israel, the shooting and killing that go on a Jamaica, election business whe a gwan in America, the mad cow disease whe a gwan in a Europe, the AIDS that kill all the people in Africa, we'r e going to highlight all of those struggles, because what? It's all of us problem together, me brethren, so we all must come together and try and tackle it, one by one.'

'Abrahams used to sell Calypso to Mr Shalit through Stanley Motta, and the man take my record and give Shalit, says is fi him own. If you look 'pon the original [Bluebeat] record, you will see the writer [is credited as] Abrahams. I went to Eddie Seaga and him send me to his attorney, who write them, threatening to sue. Sonny Bradshaw and them advise, say it was good for me; after a while me say me will go with him, and the man became one of me greatest teachers, me best friend. Mr Shalit is a man who know some ten-plus languages; he is a man who was an intelligence [officer] in the United States Army, jump out of airplanes in Germany 'cause him knew the dialect, so he is a very, very wise man. What I had at the time was a sort of village concept of things, and Mr Shalit internationalized my mind.'

The Niyabinghi order is an orthodox branch of Rastafari that takes its name from an anti-colonial religious movement active in East and Central Africa in the early 1900s; to 'chant Niyabinghi' is to sing praises to Jah and voice opposition to Babylon, with the backing of 'Niyabinghi' drumming, itself based on burru drumming techniques.

In 1970 Daley constructed a studio of his own at 43 Waltham Park Road, but was unable to equip it properly; 'Yin Mas Gan' is thus a tantalizing example of the kinds of sounds he might otherwise have created. 'It was designed by some Germans,' Daley notes. 'Graeme Goodall know them. I wasn't going for no mediocre equipment – I wanted a Neve console, twenty-four-track. I spent three months at Neve in Neasden [London] as an engineer, because I was gonna bring down a partially assembled console, but because of the non-payment of royalties [by British record companies] from my investment of my recordings I had to withhold furnishing my studio.'

CHAPTER NINE

. Clarke says the Buccaneers were 'a Jamaican group that played dance music and Top 40 for weddings'. The Bluegrass Experience featured drummer Sparrow Martin, guitarist Eric Frater and keyboardist Glen Adams.

. As Big Youth explains, 'When they put him in prison and he was coming out, him say, "After I enter through this door, no one else shall enter", and no one has entered back through that door. He said that place is going to be a car park... and it have to be, because they were the ones that form the constitution of that place, but through him was the darker spade, them push him out.'

. Dennis Brown and Cynthia Richards were also with the group at that time.

. Norman worked with Llans Thelwell and the Celestials; Ralston played in a steel band. 'I did two LP with Llans Thelwell in the sixties,' Norman reveals. 'We were working at the Colony Hotel in Montego Bay, before it turned into the Half Moon. I worked there for six or seven years. Llans Thelwell was an engineer at the airport – he was the bass player. We were singing songs like "Up, Up, And Away", and Qim Reeves'] "Green Grass Of Home". We record those songs at Dynamic, and that's where I learned a bit of drumming, one night when the drummer didn't turn up.' Ralston adds, I was playing in Irving Ellis and the Northcoasters steel band, that was about '68–' 70. Irving is the younger brother of Alton Ellis. He came to Falmouth to coach this youth club and give me leadership of the band. I was playing the bass drum first, five big drums, then I play the one they call the guitar pan – it's close to the bass.'

. Norman also recalls cutting unreleased material with Lee Perry in this period: 'Bunny Lee is a producer [who] would get other people involved: when we go to Kingston, if Bunny Lee is not doing a session and another producer is recording, he would take the producer and say, "Record this guy." We did some songs for Lee Perry that way, four or five tracks. That was '70–' 71, but I never hear them [again]. I remember one of them was "Reggae For Days": "Why won't you come and do the reggae with me, reggae reggae reggae for days." '

. Norman says other group members featured on the album include 'Paul Hurlock, he was in the Cardigans and Llans Thelwell and the Celestials with me, and he have his own one-man band at the time. Glen Stair, he's still in Jamaica, one of the cocktail pianists playing in the hotels. Bongo Asher start play percussion with us, he wrote "Give Rasta Praise" and "African Liberation".'

. Singles recorded include 'Gone Me Gone' by Linford Gilzing, a singer 'from the hills, about ten miles from Falmouth'; 'God Bless The Youth' by The Mystics, 'Eric Bernard and two of his brethren'; 'Babylon A Fight Rastaman' by 'Allah', aka Austin Williams, 'he used to play a bit of percussion while we were rehearsing'; and 'Whip Them No Skip Them' by toaster Sir Lee of the Screaming Target sound system 'from Deeside, about twelve miles from Falmouth', which was particularly popular when issued in Britain.

. 'Glen Marcinick, the guy that owned Tops, he was involved in the [original] "It's Not Who You Know",' Norman explains. 'He came to Jamaica to cut dubs at the time for [Brixton sound system owner Lloyd] Coxsone, and he went in the studio and make some music – we thought it was Bunny Lee's session. When I came to England, I get to realize he was the one who did it, and I gave him a couple of tracks to release.'

. The material includes 'I'm Tipping' and 'Sweet Sixteen', with Third World as session musicians.

. 'The drummer was Benbow', Hines recalls, 'the bass player was Clayton Downie, and Clayton went back and played rhythm guitar. Then Pablo put some haunting overdubs on it.'

. As Hines notes, 'The rhythm was laid at Joe Gibbs or Harry J. Jacob Miller was playing the drum originally on that, and it was overdubbed by Horsemouth, and Robbie Shakespeare play the bass.'

. Other members include Caiman Scott, the brother of Scotty, and Ferris Walters.

CHAPTER TEN

. Tado says backing vocals were provided by Bim Sherman, Mike Brooks and Pauline Lindsey; the musicians include bassist Errol 'Flabba' Holt, drummer Sly Dunbar, guitarist Bingy Bunny, trumpeter Bobby Ellis, trombonist Vin Gordon, plus Bobby Kalphat on piano and Dennis Ferron on organ.

. As Brown notes, 'The original name for the sound was Soul Attorney and the original owner was Rupert Brown. Because of political things, the sound have to pack up. Tony Welch bought the sound and it plays for a couple of years as King Attorney, and then they change it to Socialist Roots.'

. As Lloyd Daley notes, Jack Ruby's was the largest tube amplifier that was ever built in Jamaica – it was forty KT88s, built by Lloyd Daley, the Matador.'

. The song was issued by Coxsone in 1964. Dennis says Andy was 'a brethren we meet in Trench Town, his name is Reuben Anderson'.

. Musicians on 'Folk Song' include drummer Cornel Marshall, bassist Lloyd Brevett, plus Gladdy Anderson on piano and Winston Wright on organ.

. Dennis says Clive became aware of the group through Waterhouse resident Denver Williams, a relative of his stepmother, Patricia Chin. Session players on the disc include drummer Carlton Barrett, bassist Lloyd Parks and guitarists Chinna Smith and Alva Lewis.

. As Rose recalls, 'Me get involved in the whole tourist thing with a band named Happiness Unlimited. Beechwood Avenue, at Hummingbird Restaurant, that's where we used to rehearse. You have to sing every type of music: the BB King thing, R&B, reggae, calypso, dinner music'

. Rose says the song was backed by a tailor named Winston Samuels (aka 'Thread') and Danny White, 'a guitarist whe come from the roots neighbourhood, but them man go a high school and university'.

. Dennis recorded an earlier version in 1972 with Niney at Joe Gibbs', which was also popular in Jamaica.

CHAPTER ELEVEN

. The album was first issued in Jamaica on Taxi as _Stalk Of Sensimenia._

. As Williams recalls, 'With the Now Generation I produced Delroy Wilson with "I Wish It Was Me", a Tyrone Taylor original. Along with that was the Versatiles' "Stronger Strong" and "Give Them Bread". It was [Dudley] Earl, Spanner and Robert, three brethren from Rollington Town – they were brethren with Junior Byles and the rest of the people them. We went into the studio with the Wailers' band – at that time it was called Rhythm Force – just after the period of the Hippy Boys. It was Family Man, Carly, Glen Adams, Reggie – the usual crew.'

. The earliest-known cut of this rhythm in Jamaica is Dodd's 'College Rock', a melodica instrumental credited to Big Willie; the melodic structure stems from Eddie Floyd's soul hit 'I'v e Never Found A Girl'.

. Other notable members include former In Crowd guitarist Michael Murray and bassist 'Professor' Larry Silvera.

. Ital is a strictly naturalistic way of life propagated by some followers of Rastafari.

. The frequently versioned rhythm was based on a popular Cuban hit.

CHAPTER TWELVE

. Interview with David Rodigan, Capital Radio, London, 1981.

. Cultural Roots was originally composed of Hubert Brooks, Norman Gallimore and Wade Dice; a later incarnation featured Devon Russell.

. 'Prince' Huntley Dakin, who sang back-up with Burrell on the tune, is the operator of the Modern Printey on Maxfield Avenue and founder of the Greedy Puppy label.

. The song was originally created by the Corporation.

. The song's greatest overseas success came in 1989 when it became a hit in Washington DC because of a dance that the public created to go with it; a remix by Miami Sound Machine made sure the song remained in the charts.

. The song was a sizeable Rasta hit in 1974, produced by Lloyd F. Campbell with Niney's assistance.

. Cocoa tea is a sweet breakfast drink, whilst cerassee tea is a medicinal beverage made from an extremely bitter vine.

. Tea notes that this first session almost didn't happen, as 'the first day I was going to the studio I was held with a spliff – first time ever I get hold with a spliff in a me life. Police stop the car, and me have a draw of weed in me pocket and me get it off, but Lee Van Cliff tell the police is mine – I just gave him a spliff, he said he nah go a jail for no man's weed. The policeman lock me up and carry me to Half Way Tree, and I was lucky that time because I got bail the same day.'

. Paul notes that the song was first cut several years earlier for Monica's records of Toronto.

**David Katz with Prince Buster [David Katz]**

David Katz is author of one previous book, _People Funny Boy: The Genius Of Lee Scratch Perry._ He also contributed to the books _The Rough Guide to Reggae_ and _A Tapestry of Jamaica._ His writing and photographs have appeared in many different international publications, including _The Guardian, Mojo, Rhythm_ and the _Beat._ He has coordinated and annotated several retrospective collections of Jamaican music, released an original record in France, and has co-hosted reggae radio programmes on three continents. Originally from San Francisco, he currently lives in London, where he sometimes deejays at select night spots.
**Acknowledgements**

I would like to thank wholeheartedly all those who consented to be interviewed; their honest recollections and poetic utterances relate the story of reggae's solid foundation. I would also like to thank all the fellow journalists, photographers, friends, family members and other colleagues who assisted with this book; your contributions are greatly appreciated. A select few deserve special mention for helping me track down key individuals: in Jamaica, Max Romeo, Winston Jarrett, Gilmour Grant of the Silvertones, plus Bertram Brown, Donovan Joseph and Delroy 'Issachar' Bailey of Freedom Sounds; in New York, Clive Chin of Impact/ Above Rock and Ras Kush of the Black Redemption sound system. Lee 'Scratch' Perry also receives my unwavering gratitude for initiating the quest for knowledge by insisting I be his ghost writer in 1987. For providing me with relevant material and contacts I would like to thank record industry personnel including: Chris Wilson and Joshua Blood at Heartbeat, Steve Barrow, Bob Harding and Dominic at Blood And Fire, Ira Heaps at Jammyland, Dana Smart at Hip-O, James Dutton and Mike Coe at Motion, Trevor Wyatt formerly at Island, Gerry Lyeseight at Palm, Doctor Alimantado at Keyman, Roy Cousins at Tamoki Wambesi, Alvin Ranglin at GG, Linval Thompson at Thompson Sounds, Mikey Dread of Dread At The Controls (for more information visit www.mikeydread.com), Neil Fraser and Holly at Ariwa, Geachey at Rockers International, Linton Kwesi Johnson and Sharmilla Beezmohun at LKJ, Sharon Scott, Peady and all at Greensleeves, Joy Ellington at VP London, Sarah Watson at Virgin, Hugh Francis at Jet Star, Aaron Yarborough at Roots Foundation, Michael Goldwasser and Eric Smith at Easy Star, Ray Hurford at Muzik Tree and Ossi Sillman, Seija and the late Tero Kaski at Black Star, Colin Moore and Junior Delgado at Incredible Music, John Mason and Kenn Jones at Reggae Retro/Trybute, Manwell Tabone at Malta's Reggae Club, Ethan Reid and Angela Scott at Soul Jazz, Henk Targowski at Anachron, Guillaume Bougard at Tabou 1, Jeremy Collingwood and Bruno Blum at JAD, Tony Rounce at Westside, Laurence Cane-Honeysett and John Reed at Trojan/Sanctuary, Bibi Seaton at Sprint, Mitch Grayson at Graylands, Andre Jackson at Koptic Voice, Eric Jones at Ejahness Reggae Comprehensive, Gaylene Martin at the Coalition, Steve Barker at Big Cigar, Mark Gorney at Worldisc, Jaime Nack at One Drop Productions, Roger Steffens, CC Smith, Chuck Foster, Michael Turner and all at the _Beat,_ Thibault Ehrengardt at _Natty Dread,_ James Dillon at Amoeba, Andrew Rush at Recycled, Dave Rosencrans at the Experience Music Project, discographer Richard Noblett and all at Interstate Music, Debbie Ballard at Absolute PR and Dorothy Howe. Tenzing Sonam and Ritu Sarin assisted with Indian surnames, Dave Hallworth helped make an important editorial connection, and Chris Menist commented on an early draft of Chapter One. 'Natty Seven' of Sunshine Cars in Kingston also deserves a huge big-up for being the most patient and reliable taxi driver I have ever encountered. Finally, I thank my partner, Claudia Bernard, for her love and continual support.
